Adaptive Dialogue Policies for Museum
Conversational Agents:
A Comparative Study of Hierarchical and
Flat Reinforcement Learning with
Gaze-Based Engagement
Daniel Bourdon
A thesis submitted in partial fulfillment of the requirements for the degree
of
Master of Interaction Technology
University of Twente
Enschede, The Netherlands
December 2025
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
Abstract
Museum conversational agents must balance educational content delivery with
visitor engagement adaptation. This thesis investigates whether hierarchical rein-
forcement learning (Option-Critic) provides benefits over flat Actor-Critic policies
for museum dialogue agents that integrate gaze-derived engagement signals and
adapt to dynamic action availability. Through five hypotheses tested across 53
experimental conditions, we systematically compare architectures, reward designs,
simulator types, state representations, and option structures in a Dutch Golden Age
portrait museum domain.
Our simulator-based results suggest that flat Actor-Critic policies substantially
outperform hierarchical alternatives in this setting: flat policies achieve 98.7%
exhibit coverage while the best-optimized hierarchical configuration reaches 55.5%,
leaving a 43.2 percentage point gap. This gap persists across interventions including
deliberation cost tuning (H1), augmented reward shaping (H2), improved simulator
signal clarity (H3), compact state representations (H4), and reward-aligned option
design (H5). Reward-aligned design provides the largest hierarchical improvement,
reducing option collapse by 72.6 points, yet cannot close the performance gap.
Compact dialogue-act representations (23-d) maintain performance while achieving
84% dimensionality reduction compared to dense embeddings (143-d).
These findings suggest that when dynamic action masking already provides
temporal structure, hierarchical learning may introduce termination and credit
assignment challenges without corresponding benefits. For museum dialogue systems
with exhaustible content and structured prompts, flat reinforcement learning may
provide an effective foundation, though real-world validation with museum visitors
remains necessary.

Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
1 Introduction
Museums are still working to rebuild steady, frequent visitation after the pandemic, and
the fight for attention—on-site and online—has only grown. Recent surveys show the share
of U.S. adults who visited a museum in the past year is back to roughly pre-pandemic
levels (about 28%), yet overall recovery remains uneven, largely because repeat visits
have dipped rather than interest itself [American Alliance of Museums, 2023b,a]. In the
UK, national figures likewise report a sharp rise in both physical and digital engagement
through 2023/24, pointing to strong demand for richer, more tailored experiences [Museums
Association, 2024]. The practical challenge follows: deliver interactions that flex to a
visitor’s goals, time, and prior knowledge—without scaling staff one-to-one.
Conversational agents (CAs) become effective in museum settings once they move
beyond scripted menus and sustain multi-turn dialogue. Early deployments—such as the
embodied guide Max [Kopp et al., 2005] and the twin agents Ada and Grace [Swartout
et al., 2012]—showed how naturalistic guidance can enrich gallery experiences. However,
many first-generation systems leaned on brittle rule flows: they were expensive to update
and struggled when visitors deviated from expected paths or asked for deeper context.
Cross-domain reviews echo these limits for rule-based and narrowly scoped chatbots:
sensitivity to out-of-distribution queries, escalation under ambiguity, and compounding
maintenance costs as decision trees expand [Laymouna et al., 2024, Gruenhagen et al.,
2024]. Consequently, museums require agents capable of reasoning over long, multi-turn
exchanges while remaining grounded in curatorial knowledge and audience needs.
Reinforcement learning (RL) is a natural fit for sequential decision-making of this
kind. An RL agent learns through interaction to maximize cumulative reward [Sutton and
Barto, 2018], which maps well onto selecting dialogue policies under uncertainty. Still, a
single flat policy can falter in open-ended conversation: it must juggle near-term coherence
with longer-horizon goals like learning outcomes and sustained engagement. Hierarchical
reinforcement learning (HRL) addresses this by breaking behavior into temporally extended
sub-policies (options), supporting decisions at multiple levels of abstraction and improving
credit assignment across long spans [Sutton et al., 1999, Dietterich, 2000]. In dialogue terms,
that means high-level choices (e.g.,explain,ask,transition) with low-level realizations
that adapt in the moment—an approach backed by recent work on hierarchical policies in
both task-oriented and open-domain settings [Saleh et al., 2020, Wang et al., 2021].
In museums, agents must do two things at once: deliver accurate, situationally
relevant content and keep people engaged. Measuring engagement in a robust, ethical
way on the floor is non-trivial. Multimodal work shows that simple, observable gaze
signals—such as dwell time on an object of interest—track attention and cognitive effort
in interactive learning contexts, including VR and situated experiences [Adhanom et al.,
2023, Moreno-Arjonilla et al., 2024]. These measures are appealing in practice: they are
captured passively (e.g., via VR/AR or instrumented exhibits) and distilled into compact
features that correlate with on-task behavior.
This thesis examines reinforcement learning approaches for museum dialogue through
a comparative evaluation of hierarchical (SMDP) and flat (MDP) policy architectures.
Engagement and learning support are treated as co-equal objectives, with gaze-derived
attention features serving as a principled, low-intrusion proxy for engagement. We outline
the research direction as: (i) implement both hierarchical option-based and flat action-
selection policies with gaze-informed rewards; (ii) ground actions in institution-specific
knowledge through structured prompts; and (iii) systematically compare architectures to
9
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
identify when and why hierarchical structure helps or hinders museum dialogue, testing
across different simulators, reward functions, and state representations. Earlier embodied
and virtual guides [Kopp et al., 2005, Swartout et al., 2012] motivate the setting; recent
progress on adaptive agents supports real-time responsiveness to user state [Woo et al.,
2024].
This thesis investigates whether hierarchical RL with learned termination, gaze-based
engagement signals, and structured prompt engineering provides measurable improvements
in museum dialogue management. The comparative evaluation examines when hierarchical
structure helps or hinders performance, testing whether signal quality—rather than
architectural complexity—determines learning capacity.
1.1 Contributions
This thesis contributes five methodological and empirical advances:
• Compares hierarchical and flat policy architectures: Systematically evaluates SMDP
(hierarchical) and MDP (flat) reinforcement learning for museum dialogue across
multiple experimental conditions, identifying when hierarchical structure helps or
hinders performance and revealing the critical role of simulator signal quality in
policy learning.
• Integrates gaze-based engagement signals for real-time control: Embeds gaze-derived
dwell time as a continuous, objective engagement signal that directly informs policy
decisions, enabling adaptive pacing based on visitor attention without interrupting
dialogue flow.
• Demonstrates signal-dependent learning capacity: Shows that flat policies learn
diverse, adaptive strategies when provided clear reward signals, establishing that
observed option collapse in hierarchical policies reflects training signal limitations
rather than fundamental algorithmic failure.
• Develops structured prompt engineering for knowledge-base grounding: Creates slot-
filled prompt headers that ensure knowledge-base grounding and on-plan compliance,
reducing hallucination while maintaining natural language fluency through LLM-
based realization.
• Analyzes state representation efficiency: Demonstrates that compact dialogue-act
representations achieve near-full performance of dense embeddings with substantial
dimensionality reduction, suggesting deployment benefits for real-time museum
interactions.
1.2 Thesis Structure
Following background and related work (2), we formalize the problem as a hierarchical
semi-Markov decision process (4). Section 5 describes the system architecture, Section 6
details the training methodology, and Section 7 formulates testable hypotheses. The
experimental evaluation addresses each hypothesis through systematic comparisons,
followed by discussion (Section 9) and conclusion (Section 11).

2 Background and Related Works
The preceding challenges—personalization across visitor profiles, balancing education
with engagement, and responding to real-time context—require dialogue systems that go
beyond scripted flows.
Diverse Visitor Profiles, Engagement Balance, and Context Awareness
Museum visitors vary widely in background, prior knowledge, motivations, and interests.
Conversational agents must therefore personalize both content and pacing to sustain
relevance and learning. Decades of museum-learning research highlight that visitors’
experiences are shaped by personal, sociocultural, and physical contexts, reinforcing the
need for adaptive dialogue rather than one-size-fits-all scripts [Falk and Dierking, 2016].
Recent sector-wide surveys similarly show heterogeneous expectations around ”community
connection” and interaction styles across audience segments, underlining the value of
personalization in digital mediation [American Alliance of Museums, 2024a,b]. In practice,
museum chatbots too often under-measure visitor experience—revealing a gap between
deployment and rigorous evaluation of how different audiences engage—so systems should
incorporate continuous UX assessment and user modeling [ ˇStekerov´ a, 2022].
Balancing educational depth with enjoyable, conversational flow remains a core
challenge. Empirical deployments in museums and cultural spaces show that thoughtfully
designed conversational/interactive systems lift engagement and satisfaction when they
adapt the level of detail and the interaction style to the visitor (e.g., task-oriented
vs. exploratory) [Robinson et al., 2008, Chai-Arayalert and Chai-Arayalert, 2024, Chin
et al., 2024]. Emerging work on human-centric AI in museums echoes this, advocating
visitor-centered adaptivity and participatory design to avoid over-standardized narratives
and to better serve both casual and expert visitors [Derda and colleagues, 2025].
Strong context awareness is required—tracking where the visitor is, what they have
already seen/asked, and immediate behavioral cues (e.g., dwell, movement), then using that
state to time interventions and choose the right granularity. The museum-tech literature
(from mobile guides to AR) demonstrates that location and behavior signals enable timely,
relevant guidance, while contemporary HCI surveys argue for richer, multimodal context
models that combine spatial, historical, and interaction data for adaptive interfaces [Raptis
et al., 2005, Wang et al., 2022, Tang et al., 2025]. These findings establish three intertwined
design problems for museum conversational agents: (1) personalization for diverse visitor
profiles, (2) a principled balance between education and enjoyment, and (3) robust context
awareness to deliver the right content at the right moment.
2.1 Limitations of Traditional Dialogue Systems
Early systems demonstrated naturalistic guidance but relied on fixed rules that de-
grade under visitor variability. The embodied guideMaxand virtual agentsAdaand
Graceshowed that social dialogue enriches gallery experiences, yet these systems were
predominantly rule-based and brittle under the variability required for personalization,
engagement balance, and context awareness. Systems like the embodied guideMax[Kopp
et al., 2005] and the virtual twinsAdaandGrace[Swartout et al., 2012] showed that
naturalistic language and social behaviors enrich the gallery experience. Cross-domain
reviews echo the limitations of such scripted or menu-driven systems: sensitivity to
out-of-distribution queries, escalating fallback behavior under ambiguity, compounding
11
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
maintenance as decision trees expand, and difficulty incorporating continuous evaluation
of visitor experience [Laymouna et al., 2024, Reimann et al., 2024]. In dialogue-system
research more broadly, policy control based on fixed rules is known to degrade with noise
and ambiguity, motivating probabilistic decision-making and learned policies [Williams
and Young, 2007, Young et al., 2013]. These observations motivate moving beyond static
flows to policies that adapt over multi-turn interactions, remain grounded in curatorial
content, and can trade off engagement with coverage of educational goals.
Rule-based dialogue systems struggle with:
• Scalability: Difficulty in scaling to the large combinatorial space of visitor intents,
exhibit contexts, and follow-up questions; maintaining trees/menu flows becomes
costly and brittle [Reimann et al., 2024].
• Personalization: Limited capacity to adjust content depth and style to individuals or
segments, compared with learned policies that condition actions on user state/history
[Kwan et al., 2023].
• Contextual Integration: Challenges in fusing real-time signals (location, dwell/engagement,
prior turns) into action selection; robust context use typically requires probabilistic
state tracking and policy optimization [Williams and Young, 2007, Reimann et al.,
2024].
These limitations highlight the need for adaptive dialogue management. In practice,
social CA architectures grow into multi-component pipelines (speech/vision, domain
models, affect, timing) where layered components coordinate action selection [op den
Akker and Bruijnes, 2012]. Hand-authoring rules at each layer does not scale to museum
floor variability, motivating learned policy approaches where RL discovers coordination
strategies from data.
These limitations motivate exploring reinforcement learning approaches that adapt
policies through interaction rather than pre-specifying all rules. The following subsections
review three technical foundations: hierarchical RL for temporal abstraction, museum-
specific conversational agents, and gaze-based engagement sensing.
2.2 Related Work: Hierarchical RL, Museum CAs, and Gaze-
Based Engagement
2.2.1 Reinforcement Learning for Dialogue Systems
Dialogue systems benefit from RL’s ability to optimize for competing objectives—task
success versus engagement, brevity versus thoroughness [Williams and Young, 2007, Kwan
et al., 2023]. The central design question is whether to use flat or hierarchical policy
structure.
Flat RL approaches. Standard MDP-based dialogue policies treat each turn as an
independent decision, selecting actions to maximize cumulative reward without explicit
temporal abstraction. This approach has proven effective in task-oriented settings: flat
policies trained with REINFORCE or actor-critic methods achieve strong performance on
slot-filling and information retrieval benchmarks [Williams and Young, 2007, Su et al.,
2017]. The appeal is simplicity—flat policies avoid the overhead of option selection and
termination learning, and their action-value estimates update directly from immediate
rewards. When action spaces are moderate and reward signals are dense, flat policies can
12
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
outperform hierarchical alternatives that introduce unnecessary abstraction [Nachum et al.,
2018]. However, flat policies face credit assignment challenges as episode length grows:
with 30–50 turn dialogues, determining which early actions contributed to final outcomes
becomes difficult without structural inductive bias. However, flat policies face credit
assignment challenges as episode length grows: with 30–50 turn dialogues, determining
which early actions contributed to final outcomes becomes difficult without structural
inductive bias.
Hierarchical RL approaches. HRL addresses this by decomposing policy decisions across
multiple abstraction levels [Sutton et al., 1999, Dietterich, 2000]. Feudal architectures split
manager and worker roles [Casanueva et al., 2018]; option-based approaches let strategies
span multiple turns [Weisz et al., 2018, Su et al., 2017]; and recent work learns when to
terminate options rather than fixing durations [Chen et al., 2023b]. All three improve
long-horizon credit assignment, but each makes different trade-offs. Feudal RL requires
careful domain decomposition; option-based frameworks need thoughtful initialization;
MAXQ provides theoretical guarantees but suits discrete rather than adaptive termination.
The options framework aligns well with dialogue where strategies should persist during
high engagement and terminate when attention drops.
The open question. Whether hierarchical structure actually helps depends on the
domain. In task-oriented dialogue with clear subgoals (e.g., booking flights: gather
destination, then dates, then preferences), hierarchy provides natural decomposition.
In open-ended educational dialogue, the benefit is less clear—visitors may not follow
predictable paths, and the overhead of option persistence may outweigh gains from
temporal abstraction. Prior work does not resolve this for museum settings, where
engagement and coverage must be balanced dynamically. We therefore compare both
architectures empirically rather than assuming hierarchy wins.
This empirical uncertainty motivates the experimental design pursued in this thesis:
we implement both hierarchical (SMDP) and flat (MDP) architectures under identical
conditions—same reward functions, state features, prompt engineering, and knowledge
base—and test across multiple simulator designs, reward configurations, and state
representations. Rather than assuming hierarchical structure provides benefit based
on literature from task-oriented domains, we treat it as a hypothesis to be validated in
the specific context of open-ended museum dialogue. The following sections establish
the technical foundation for this comparative evaluation: formal problem definition (§4),
system architecture (§5), training methodology (§6), and hypothesis formulation (§7).
2.2.2 Museum Conversational Agents
Early museum conversational agents established the value of naturalistic, socially-aware
dialogue. The embodied guideMaxfocused on social presence—embodiment and small
talk enriched gallery experiences but content selection remained scripted [Kopp et al.,
2005]. Virtual agentsAdaandGraceadded context-awareness, tracking visitor history
and adapting responses, but still relied on hand-authored flows [Swartout et al., 2012].
Neither learned dialogue policy from interaction data, and neither adapted pacing to
real-time engagement signals. More broadly, these systems share three limitations: (i)
hand-authored rules do not scale to diverse visitor needs and require extensive maintenance
as content evolves, (ii) fixed interaction patterns cannot respond to engagement drops or
intent shifts mid-conversation, and (iii) evaluation rarely goes beyond deployment reports
to rigorous assessment of learning outcomes.
13
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
Rule-based systems offer interpretability and predictable behavior but require domain
expertise for authoring and maintenance; learned policies can adapt to diverse patterns
but may lack interpretability and require careful reward design. The gap between early
rule-based systems and modern learned policies remains largely unexplored in museum
settings, particularly for balancing educational objectives with visitor engagement.
2.2.3 Gaze-Based Engagement in Interactive Systems
Gaze reliably tracks attention across settings: dwell time correlates with cognitive engage-
ment in VR/AR learning [Bozkir et al., 2021, Dubovi, 2022], aligns with content focus
in mobile museum contexts [Mokatren et al., 2016], and signals interest in human-robot
interaction [Admoni and Scassellati, 2017]. The common finding is that simple gaze
metrics—especially dwell—generalize well across populations and tasks.
The critical question is not whether gaze works, but how it integrates into policy
learning. Most systems treat gaze as an evaluation metric: researchers analyze where
visitors looked after the fact to assess engagement. Few close the loop—feeding gaze into
real-time control so the agent responds to attention drops as they happen. This matters
because engagement is volatile: a visitor losing interest at turn 15 needs intervention
at turn 16, not a retrospective insight in the post-session analysis. Methodological
considerations (metric choice, calibration, noise handling) are well-understood; the missing
piece is integration with policy learning.
2.2.4 Synthesis: The Gap
Prior work demonstrates each component in isolation: RL improves dialogue policy
learning, museum CAs show the value of personalized guidance, and gaze features provide
reliable engagement signals. The research gap is integrating these components while
choosing the appropriate architecture.
Two design paths are plausible.Flat policies(MDP) offer simplicity: direct action
selection without option overhead, easier debugging, and potentially faster learning when
reward signals are clear.Hierarchical policies(SMDP with options) offer temporal
abstraction: strategies that persist across turns may improve credit assignment and
behavioral coherence, but add complexity through option selection and termination
learning.
Prior work does not resolve which architecture suits museum dialogue. Task-oriented
benchmarks favor hierarchy when subgoals are clear; open-ended settings show mixed
results [Nachum et al., 2018]. Museum dialogue falls in between: there are natural strategic
phases (explain, question, transition) but visitors do not follow predictable scripts. We
therefore test both architectures under identical conditions, expecting hierarchy to help
but not assuming it.
The specific contributions are: (a) learned option termination conditioned on engage-
ment signals rather than fixed thresholds, (b) gaze features integrated directly into policy
learning rather than post-hoc analysis, and (c) structured prompt engineering for KB
grounding. We implement these for both flat and hierarchical architectures to isolate the
effect of temporal abstraction.
Scope and Limitations. While this thesis addresses the integration gap, several aspects
remain outside its scope. First, individual differences in visitor engagement patterns (e.g.,
cultural background, prior expertise, physical fatigue) are not modeled—the state machine
simulator uses aggregate behavioral patterns rather than personalized visitor profiles.
14
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
Second, the gaze-to-engagement mapping assumes dwell time is a reliable proxy across
populations, though calibration requirements and individual gaze patterns may introduce
noise in real deployment. Third, the knowledge graph structure is fixed; dynamic content
updates or visitor-contributed knowledge are not addressed. These limitations inform the
scope of claims and suggest directions for future work.
2.3 Markov Decision Processes
Note: Readers familiar with reinforcement learning fundamentals may skip to Section 6
for the Option-Critic framework used in this thesis.
Reinforcement Learning (RL) provides a framework for agents to learn optimal
behaviors through interactions with an environment. This interaction is commonly
modeled as a Markov Decision Process (MDP), defined by the tuple ( S,A, P, R, γ ), where:
•S: a finite set of states,
•A: a finite set of actions,
•P (s′|s, a): the probability of transitioning to state s′ from state s after taking action
a,
•R(s, a): the immediate reward received after taking actionain states,
•γ∈ [0, 1]: the discount factor that prioritizes immediate rewards over distant future
rewards.
To better illustrate the agent-environment interaction underlying Markov Decision
Processes, Figure 1 provides a high-level schematic adapted from Sutton and Barto [2018].
It highlights how the agent observes the current state st, selects an action at, and receives
both a new states t+1 and a scalar rewardR(s t, at) from the environment in response.

from Sutton and Barto [2018].

2.4 Value-Based and Policy-Based Methods
RL algorithms are broadly categorized into:
• Value-Based Methods: These focus on estimating the value function V (s) or the
action-value function Q(s, a), which represent the expected return from a state or
state-action pair, respectively. An example is Q-learning, where the update rule is:
Q(s, a)←Q(s, a) +α
h
r+γmax
a′
Q(s′, a′)−Q(s, a)
i
• Policy-Based Methods: These directly optimize the policy π(a|s) without relying on
value functions. Techniques like policy gradients adjust the policy parameters in
the direction that increases expected rewards.
RL for dialogue faces three fundamental challenges. Credit assignment is murky—which
turn actually helped?—especially when rewards arrive only at episode end [Peng et al.,
2017]. Data efficiency matters because collecting real conversations is expensive. And
action spaces explode as dialogue complexity grows, making flat policies struggle without
structural inductive bias.
Museum dialogue amplifies these issues: episodes run 30–50 turns, reward signals
(engagement, coverage) are noisy and delayed, and visitors follow unpredictable paths.
Whether hierarchy helps address these challenges—or introduces unnecessary overhead—is
an empirical question we test directly.
2.5 Hierarchical Reinforcement Learning
The scalability issues outlined above arise because a flat agent must decide at every
time step whichprimitiveaction to take. As tasks become longer and rewards sparser,
learning such fine-grained control quickly becomes sample-inefficient and prone to myopic
exploration.Hierarchical Reinforcement Learning(HRL) tackles this problem by endowing
the agent withtemporal abstraction: beside elementary actions it may selectmacro–actions
(oroptions)—closed-loop policies that unfold over multiple steps [Dayan and Hinton,
1993, Dietterich, 2000, Sutton et al., 1999]. An HRL agent therefore learns a policy π
that operates on several time-scales, delegating short-horizon control to lower layers while
higher layers concentrate on long-term strategy.
When actions can last more than one step we model the environment as asemi-Markov
decision process(SMDP). The one-step transition kernel P (s′ |s, a ) is replaced by a joint
distribution over next state and duration,
P(s ′, τ|s, a), τ∈N +,
so that planning and learning algorithms generalise naturally while accounting for the
elapsed timeτ.

Figure 2: Hierarchical learning dynamics. A high-level policy π selects a macro-action σ
(black arrow) spanning several primitive actionsa; the low-level policyπ σ controls those
primitives. Value estimates at both levels ( Vπ in green and Vσ in grey) propagate credit
over different temporal horizons. Adapted from Ribas-Fernandes et al. [2011].
Figure 2 illustrates how hierarchical control factors credit assignment: the green returns
Vπ bridge long gaps between decisive moments, while grey returns Vσ guide the execution
of the macro-action itself.
Figure 3: Conceptual illustration of Hierarchical Reinforcement Learning. The diagram
showcases the interaction between high-level policies (options) and low-level actions,
emphasizing temporal abstraction and modular decision-making. Adapted from Bougie
and Ichise [2022].
Since the 1990s a series of foundational algorithms—Feudal RL [Dayan and Hinton,
1993], MAXQ decomposition [Dietterich, 2000], and the Options framework [Sutton et al.,
1999]—have shown that temporal abstraction affords three principal advantages:
• Long-term credit assignment: extended actions collapse delayed rewards onto higher-
level decisions, accelerating learning.
• Structured exploration: sampling entire sub-policies, rather than isolated primitives,
enables coherent exploration over sparse reward landscapes.
• Knowledge transfer: reusable options capture task sub-structure and transfer across
domains or fine-tune with little additional data.
These properties make HRL a natural candidate for complex, open-ended dia-
logue—where an agent must juggle immediate conversational moves with overarching
curatorial or pedagogical goals. Before surveying contemporary systems, this section
17
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
briefly recaps theOptionsframework that operationalizes temporal abstraction in the
proposed setting and fixes notation for the remainder.
2.5.1 Options Framework
The most well known HRL method is known as the options framework by Sutton et al.
[1999]. In the HRL options framework, an agent selects high-level actions (options), each
of which encapsulates a policy πω, an initiation set Iω, and a termination condition βω.
Anoptionωis defined as a tuple:
ω= (I ω, πω, βω)
- Iω ⊆ S : the set of states where the option can be initiated, - πU|ω (a|s, ω): the intra-option
policy, -β ω(s)∈[0,1]: the probability the option terminates in states.

Adapted from Sutton et al. [1999].
Figure 4 illustrates the conceptual advantage of HRL, and specifically the options
framework. HRL with options enables structured transitions between these levels: the
agent selects an option, which then executes a sequence of low-level actions internally
until a termination condition is met.
The options framework provides modularity: structuring decisions into options and
actions enables exploration over longer horizons and potential transfer across tasks.
In dialogue, these advantages materialize as high-level strategies (introduce, question,
transition) realized by lower-level utterances. However, whether these theoretical benefits
transfer to open-ended museum dialogue remains an empirical question tested in this
thesis.

2.5.2 Hierarchical control and learning for long, coherent conversations
Hierarchy in dialogue means the agent separateswhento change strategy fromhow
to realize utterances while a strategy is active. Concretely, the proposed framework
models four strategic options (Explain, AskQuestion, OfferTransition, Conclude) and lets
a high-level policy over options decide which one to start in statest, while a low-level policy
executes utterances until the option stops. This is precisely the setting of options over
SMDPs [Sutton et al., 1999]: a manager samples ωt ∼π Ω(ω|s t), the active option follows
an intra-option policy at+k ∼π U|ω t(a|s t+k, ωt) for k = 0, . . . , τ− 1, and a termination
function βωt(st+k) ∈ [0, 1] decides—at each intermediate state—whether control returns
to the manager. Ifτis the (stochastic) duration until termination, the option-value is:

Two coupled learning problems follow directly from this structure.Between-option
learningfits the manager πΩ(ω|s ) that selects the next strategic move; in the case
of Conversational Agent in a museum, s can include user intent cues and user interest
metrics, so the manager can prefer options that relate to user focus and what they
specifically say allowing the agent to pivot to more appropriate options when metrics
like attention or intent shift.Within-option learningfits each option’s intra-option policy
πU|ω (a|s, ω )—how to realize turns while ω is active—and its termination βω(s)—when
to stop and hand control back. Termination governs pacing. A good option encompassing
a conversational strategy like explaining should continue across multiple turns while
dwell remains high and end quickly when dwell drops or the visitor asks a question.
Option–Critic [Bacon et al., 2017] is designed for this: it provides SMDP-consistent
gradients to learn both πU|ω and βω from interaction, rather than fixing option length by
hand.

When AO > 0 (staying in the option beats handing control back), this pushes βω(s) down

This formal split mirrors the practical needs of a museum guide. The manager chooses
a strategy at a cadence that matches the visitor’s focus and goals; the option then handles
utterance-level realization and decides when that strategy has run its course. In symbols,
the continuation probability inside an option is 1 −β ω(st+k), so the termination time τ
has a state-dependent hazard; longerτunder sustained attention increases P
k<τ γkrt+k,
while early termination shortens exposition and frees the manager to switch strategy.
Because γτ scales the bootstrap to V (st+τ), choosingappropriate τ is an integral part of
credit control rather than an afterthought.
Empirically, hierarchical control has delivered consistent gains in dialogue. Feudal
decompositions route decisions through a manager–worker split, reducing the branching
factor per decision and scaling multi-domain managers [Dayan and Hinton, 1993, Casanueva
et al., 2018]. MAXQ shows that decomposing tasks into sub-tasks yields reusable structure
and better exploration [Dietterich, 2000]. For end-to-end task systems, HDNO couples
a high-level controller over latent dialogue acts with a low-level surface realizer under
the options view and improves task success and human ratings on MultiWOZ compared
to flat baselines [Wang et al., 2021]. In composite task completion, learning around
sub-goals simplifies exploration and improves sample efficiency when rewards are sparse
or delayed [Peng et al., 2017], and subgoal discovery can further automate the hierarchy
for large action spaces [Tang et al., 2018]. Open-domain settings also benefit: hierarchical
policies improve coherence and goal completion by letting a high-level controller steer
local generation [Saleh et al., 2020]. More recently, guidance signals and offline structure
have been used to stabilize or pre-shape hierarchical policies at scale—human-in-the-loop
pruning and evaluation to regularize option choices across domains [Rohmatillah and
Chien, 2023], and simulation-free latent policy planning that discovers fine-grained skills
from logs before online finetuning [He et al., 2025].
Taken together, these results motivate a pattern to adopt: an interpretable option set
whose manager πΩ is trained to read engagement and intent, with each option learning both
when to engage a conversational strategy ( πU|ω ) and when to stop ( βω). In the proposed
setting, this means a conversational strategy like explaining persists while engagement
metrics are strong; termination increases when attention plateaus or the visitor’s intent
shifts; and a strategy associated with boosting engagement like asking a question can
then take over. This is the mechanism by which hierarchy delivers long-horizon coherence
without sacrificing local responsiveness.
Option design principles: conversational function vs. reward alignment.
20
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
A central question in hierarchical dialogue ishow to partition the action space into
options. Two principled approaches emerge from the literature, motivating this thesis to
explore both designs systematically.
Function-based designgroups actions by communicative function, drawing on dialogue
act taxonomies from computational linguistics. ISO 24617-2 defines communicative
functions (Inform, Question, Directive, Commissive) validated by empirical dialogue
act research [Bunt et al., 2011, Stolcke et al., 2000]. In educational settings, the ICAP
framework and tutoring research identify explain, scaffold, and question as core pedagogical
moves [Chi and Wylie, 2014, VanLehn, 2011]. This motivates a 4-option design (Explain,
AskQuestion, OfferTransition, Conclude) where each option corresponds to a distinct
communicative function.
Reward-aligned designgroups actions by reward component alignment, drawing on
intrinsic motivation and option discovery literature in HRL [Kulkarni et al., 2016,
Machado et al., 2017, Pathak et al., 2017]. This creates one-to-one reward-option
correspondence: Explain (novelty), Engage (engagement), Transition (coverage), and
Conclude (termination). The Explain option is masked at the option level when exhibit
facts are exhausted, following action masking principles [Huang et al., 2020] to ensure
options are only available when their associated rewards are achievable.
These two approaches represent complementary trade-offs: function-based options offer
behavioral flexibility (multiple subactions per option) while reward-aligned options offer
learning efficiency (clearer credit assignment). Both designs use four options, enabling
controlled comparison of grouping philosophy. Figure 5 illustrates both structures.

providing behavioral flexibility with multiple subactions per option. The reward-aligned

when exhibit facts are exhausted (shown in red), forcing termination/option switch
structurally rather than relying on learned termination behavior.
2.5.3 Engagement sensing with gaze: signals, robustness, and use
In-gallery engagement must be inferred as the interaction unfolds and without intrusive
probes. Eye tracking is well suited to this requirement because it delivers a continuous,
unobtrusive record of visual attention that has been repeatedly associated with on-task
focus and cognitive–affective involvement in immersive learning settings [Bozkir et al.,
2021, Dubovi, 2022, Mikhailenko et al., 2022]. Among available gaze measures, dwell
22
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
time on the currently relevant Area of Interest (AOI) stands out as a dependable
approximation to engagement for object-centered experiences. Conceptually, dwell
aggregates micro-movements (fixations and interleaved small saccades) into a single
proportion of time that the gaze remains within the AOI for a short analysis window
aligned to conversational turns. Formally, with Wt a rolling window and TAOI the set of
timestamps fixated within the exhibit AOI, the online dwell ratio is
dwellt = |TAOI ∩W t|
|Wt| ∈[0,1],
optionally smoothed across adjacent windows to dampen transient saccades.
Methodological syntheses emphasize that AOI-based dwell is comparatively robust
to small calibration drift and sampling noise, provided AOIs are defined consistently
and windowing is reported [Holmqvist et al., 2011, Orquin and Holmqvist, 2018]. In
classroom-scale VR, dwell tied to object-of-interest has shown face validity and convergent
validity as a marker of on-task attention, tracking when learners remain focused on the
pedagogically relevant target [Bozkir et al., 2021]; multimodal studies of VR learning
likewise report that sustained looking relates to cognitive and emotional engagement
[Dubovi, 2022, Mikhailenko et al., 2022]. Together, these findings support dwell as a
stable, turn-grained indicator of whether the current exhibit retains attention.
Fixation-based summaries offer a complementary but more method-sensitive view of
engagement. Metrics such as fixation rate and mean fixation duration have been linked
to levels of cognitive processing and load in immersive tasks [Kim et al., 2023, Wang
et al., 2023], yet their reliability depends on algorithmic choices in fixation detection
(velocity/dispersion thresholds) and on eye tracker characteristics, which can introduce
between-study variability [Holmqvist et al., 2011]. As a result, fixation dynamics are
informative for characterizing thequalityof looking—sustained scrutiny vs. fleeting
glances—but warrant careful reporting of detection parameters and sampling rates to
ensure interpretability across deployments.
A further dimension is the spatial distribution of gaze, often summarized by entropy
or dispersion indices. Gaze entropy quantifies how concentrated or scattered visual
attention is across competing AOIs; higher entropy typically reflects scanning, divided
attention, or exploratory search, whereas lower entropy accompanies focused scrutiny
[Gugerty et al., 2017]. Recent interactive- and VR-based studies extend this interpretation,
showing that entropy tracks attentional shifts and elements of cognitive load, albeit with
sensitivity to window size and AOI granularity [Nguyen et al., 2024, Lee et al., 2024].
This sensitivity highlights a measurement caution: dispersion measures are powerful for
describing attentional organization and longer-run trends, but they are noisier at very
short horizons and must be interpreted in light of the underlying segmentation.
Finally, there is support for the validity of these measures in museum-adjacent contexts.
Mobile eye-tracking studies that pair guidance with on-the-move viewing demonstrate that
gaze can be captured and aligned with content outside of laboratory conditions [Mokatren
et al., 2016], and broader HRI reviews highlight social gaze as a reliable channel through
which attention and interest are manifested in interactions with artificial agents [Admoni
and Scassellati, 2017]. Taken together, the literature positions AOI dwell as a primary,
robust indicator of moment-to-moment engagement in object-centered experiences, with
23
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
fixation dynamics and dispersion as complementary descriptors that enrich—but do not
supplant—the core attentional signal provided by dwell.
2.5.4 Intent features as compact, turn-aligned signals
Effective turn-by-turn decision making requires distilling raw utterances into a small
set of features that say what the visitor is doingnowand how certain that reading
is. Classic pipelines used dialogue acts or domain-specific intent labels to turn open
text into policy-ready observations, improving downstream control in noisy, real-time
settings [Williams and Young, 2007, Young et al., 2013]. Two shifts make these signals
far more reliable in multi-turn museum conversations: (i) contextual encoders that
interpret an utterance in light of speaker role and local history, and (ii) pretrained
language models with light task heads that learn robust intent cues with modest
supervision. DialogueBERT exemplifies the first trend, encoding turn order and roles to
lift multi-turn intent recognition over non-contextual baselines, while few-shot variants
such as IntentBERT show strong transfer across intent sets with limited labeled data
[Zhang et al., 2021b,a]. Figure 6 sketches this idea: DialogueBERT encodes each utterance
with token, position, speaker-role, and turn-order signals, then uses lightweight task heads
to output compact intent features with calibrated confidence. This makes the ‘now’ state
explicit and policy-ready without relying on long, brittle text histories.
Figure 6: Turn- and role-aware intent encoding. DialogueBERT augments a pretrained
encoder with token, position,role, andturnembeddings and lightweight task heads,
producing compact, calibrated features suitable for turn-by-turn control [Zhang et al.,
2021b].
DialogueBERT produces a few compact outputs: a coarse act/intentwith calibrated
confidence; a cue to question type (e.g.,whyvs.what) that predicts explanatory shape;
a short-history embedding that tracks unresolved topics; and light entity grounding so
references align to the exhibit knowledge base rather than surface strings. These features
24
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
map directly to policy choices and conversational strategies such asexplain,probe,clarify,
ortransition. Evidence from task-oriented dialogue confirms that small, well-chosen state
summaries improve response selection, error recovery, and turn-taking compared with
text-only policies and rule trees [Kwan et al., 2023, Casanueva et al., 2018, Wang et al.,
2021].
Empirical results support both effectiveness and efficiency. On fine-grained single-
domain data (e.g., BANKING77), intent models built on pretrained encoders achieve
strong accuracy with compact representations [Casanueva et al., 2020]. In multi-turn
settings, explicitly encoding role and turn structure yields consistent gains over single-turn
baselines [Zhang et al., 2021b]. Industry-facing studies report further improvements
when lightweight retrieval or context fusion is added on top of a compact state (average
multi-point lifts without heavy supervision), reinforcing the value of concise, context-aware
features over raw text alone [Liu et al., 2024].
Given this, Intent classifiers degrade under domain shift and when users produce
unseen or overlapping intents; long-tailed labels and multi-intent utterances amplify this
[Larson et al., 2019, Casanueva et al., 2020]. Multi-turn OOD detectors that leverage
short dialogue history outperform single-turn detectors, suggesting that even a thin slice
of context helps separate unfamiliar intents from in-scope variants [Lang et al., 2024].
The practical takeaway is to treat intent features asdescriptive inputs with uncertainty,
not rigid gates: pair compact, turn-aligned signals with confidence or entropy and let the
policy decide when to trust them or defer to other cues [Zhang et al., 2021a].
2.5.5 Synthesis and open gap
The preceding components integrate coherently. Hierarchical control supplies the
long-range backbone for conversation: a manager selects a strategy (an option) that
can persist across several turns, while an intra-option policy realizes the utterances
until a learned termination returns control [Sutton et al., 1999, Bacon et al., 2017].
Prompt-engineered headers then sit at the realization boundary: each option (Explain,
AskQuestion,OfferTransition,Conclude) owns a short, human-readable template
with slots, paired with a compact context vector, so the language model can speak
naturally but stay aligned with the active strategy [Robino, 2025, Wang et al., 2024]. The
manager’s state is small and actionable: turn-aligned intent cues (role- and history-aware
encoders) indicate what the visitor is doing now and with what confidence, and gaze dwell
over the relevant object acts as a robust online proxy for attention in object-centered
settings [Zhang et al., 2021b,a, Holmqvist et al., 2011, Orquin and Holmqvist, 2018,
Bozkir et al., 2021, Dubovi, 2022]. Together, these signals decide when to persist with
a strategy and when to switch, while the header keeps surface language fluent and on-style.
This pattern is consistent with results in dialogue and planning: decompositions
reduce branching and improve multi-domain control; coupling a high-level controller
over latent acts with a low-level realizer improves task success and human ratings on
MultiWOZ; open-domain variants report better global coherence when a controller steers
local generation [Dietterich, 2000, Casanueva et al., 2018, Wang et al., 2021, Saleh et al.,
2020]. It also mirrors grounded hybrids where an external controller keeps a fluent
generator on task without strangling expressiveness [Ahn et al., 2022]. Training methods
map directly: actor–critic with semi-MDP returns focuses credit on option choice and
termination (Option–Critic), while SAC, or conservative Q-learning cover on-policy,
25
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
off-policy regimes; even modest RL on the decision layer improves multi-turn robustness
[Bacon et al., 2017, Schulman et al., 2017, Haarnoja et al., 2018, Kumar et al., 2020, Su
et al., 2017, Weisz et al., 2018].
Prior museum agents established the value of guidance but relied heavily on
hand-authored rules and fixed pacing [Kopp et al., 2005, Swartout et al., 2012].
Contemporary work demonstrates each ingredient in isolation—hierarchy, intent features,
prompt schemas, or gaze-driven engagement—but there is little evidence for aunified,
option-based controller that (i) learns when to switch strategies from online engagement
(dwell) and intent cues, and (ii) realizes each strategy with a slot-filled prompt header so
surface language remains flexible and natural. Thus,learned timing, compact state, and
prompt-level realization have not been evaluated together in situated, educational dialogue.
This thesis implements and evaluates that integration. A manager policy reads a
compact state (engagement features derived from dwell, a short dialogue history summary,
and turn-aligned intent features) and selects among a small, interpretable option set.
Each option is realized through a structured prompt header with explicit constraints and
knowledge-base guardrails so that LLM generation remains grounded. Termination is
learned (Option–Critic) rather than fixed, enabling strategy persistence when engagement
remains high and switching when it drops or intent shifts. Evaluation compares hierarchical
(SMDP) and flat (MDP) policies under matched rewards, prompts, and simulator settings
to isolate the effect of temporal abstraction.
3 Research Questions
This thesis explores whether hierarchical reinforcement learning with structured prompt
engineering provides a justified design choice for museum conversational agents. We
investigate five key architectural and algorithmic decisions: hierarchical policy structure,
reward shaping, simulator signal quality, state representation efficiency, and prompt
grounding mechanisms.
Main research question
To what extent is a hierarchical reinforcement learning approach a justified design choice for
museum conversational agents? Specifically, can a proof-of-concept system using options
with learned termination, structured prompt headers, efficient state representations, and
explicit reward shaping demonstrate measurable control of long-horizon engagement,
content coverage, and adaptive pacing while remaining practical to operate and maintain?
To answer this main question, we pose five supporting questions that test the core
components of the approach. These questions form a logical progression: RQ1 tests
the core architectural choice (hierarchy vs. flat), RQ2-3 examine training signal quality
(reward design and simulator fidelity), and RQ4-5 optimize implementation efficiency
(state representation and option design). Success on RQ1 is necessary but not sufficient;
the framework succeeds only if all five yield positive results.
26
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
RQ1: Does hierarchical policy structure (option-based manager with learned
terminations) improve long-horizon coherence, learning efficiency, and training
stability compared to a flat policy under the same rewards and prompts?
This question tests whether temporal abstraction via options addresses the scalability lim-
itations of flat policies (large action spaces, poor credit assignment). It examines whether
hierarchical structure enables sustained strategic commitment, adaptive termination based
on engagement signals, and more stable training dynamics.
RQ2: Is explicit reward shaping (responsiveness, transition penalties, conclude
bonus, question-asking incentive) necessary for learning diverse dialogue
strategies, or can the agent learn effective behavior through engagement
and novelty signals alone?
This question tests whether explicit reward components are necessary for learning balanced
dialogue strategies beyond fact explanation, addressing the challenge of sparse feedback
in long-horizon dialogue. It examines whether augmented rewards improve question-
answering behavior, transition timing, and overall policy diversity for both hierarchical
and flat architectures.
RQ3: Does simulator signal clarity affect learned policy quality and strategy
diversity?
This question tests whether the fidelity of the training signal matters for policy learning. It
examines whether non-overlapping dwell ranges and explicit visitor state modeling (State
Machine simulator) produce qualitatively different policies compared to probabilistic,
overlapping signals (Sim8), and whether clearer signals accelerate learning or improve
strategy diversity.
RQ4: Can state representation be made more efficient (compact dialogue-act
classification) without sacrificing performance?
This question tests whether computational efficiency can be improved through state space
optimization. It examines whether reduced-dimensionality representations (dialogue-act
probabilities) maintain performance while reducing inference cost and training sample
complexity.
RQ5: Does option design philosophy affect learning efficiency and policy
quality—function-based options grounded in dialogue-act theory versus reward-
aligned options that mirror the reward structure (novelty, engagement, cover-
age, and termination)?
This question tests a fundamental HRL design choice: how should the option space be
structured? We compare two 4-option designs: (i) a function-based set aligned with
communicative functions (ISO 24617-2; SWBD-DAMSL) [Bunt et al., 2011, Stolcke et al.,
2000] against (ii) a reward-aligned set where each option maps to a single reward component
(Explain→novelty, Engage→engagement, Transition→coverage, Conclude→termination
bonus), motivated by intrinsic-motivation and option-discovery work [Kulkarni et al., 2016,
Pathak et al., 2017, Singh et al., 2005, Machado et al., 2017]. By holding option count
constant, this comparison isolates the grouping philosophy.
27
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
Validating all five questions would establish that hierarchical RL with learned termina-
tion, gaze-based engagement, and structured prompting provides a principled, deployable
approach to adaptive museum dialogue. Each research question maps directly to a
corresponding hypothesis (H1–H5) in Section 7 that specifies testable predictions and
evaluation metrics.
4 Problem Formulation
To answer the research questions posed above, the conversational museum agent is
formalized as a hierarchical semi-Markov decision process (SMDP). Throughout this
formalization, we follow standard notation: calligraphic uppercase for sets ( S, A, O),
bold lowercase for vectors (f t,h t), and italic lowercase for scalars and indices ( st, at, γ).
This mathematical framework enables modeling multi-level decision-making: high-level
strategic choices (options) and low-level utterance realization, with learned termination
conditions that adapt to engagement and intent signals. The formalization specifies the
state space (gaze-based engagement features, dialogue history, intent/context embeddings),
action space (hierarchical options and subactions), reward function (baseline: engagement
and novelty; with optional auxiliary components), and learning objective (maximizing
expected cumulative reward via actor-critic with SMDP returns). This framework provides
the theoretical foundation for the system architecture and training methodology described
in subsequent sections.
4.1 Hierarchical Framing
The agent is modeled as a two-level hierarchical semi-Markov decision process (SMDP),
where the high-level policy selects communicative strategies (options) such asExplainor
AskQuestion, while the low-level policy selects utterances within these strategies, using
structured templates to maintain clarity and safety during generation. This structure
directly addresses the challenges of long-term credit assignment and exploration in sparse-
reward, multi-turn settings noted in HRL literature [Bacon et al., 2017, Peng et al.,
2017].
4.2 State Representation
The agent’s state space is designed as a structured vector that captures both what the
visitor is observing and how they are communicating. At each dialogue turn t, the state
is defined as:
st = [ft,h t,i t,c t]
Where each component captures a different aspect of the interaction:
• Focus Vectorf t: Indicates which exhibit the visitor is currently looking at. For 5
exhibits, the vector is defined as:
ft = [ft,1, ft,2, . . . , ft,5, ft,∅]
where exactly one ft,i = 1 (the exhibit currently in focus) and all others = 0. The
dimension is 6.
28
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
•Dialogue History Vectorh t: Tracks what has been discussed:
ht = [hexh

t ]
whereh exh

is 5 + 4 = 9. (For option-ablation variants, this term scales with the number of
options.)
• Intent Embeddingi t: Captures what the visitor is trying to communicate using
DialogueBERT [Zhang et al., 2021b] specifically in the last turn. For each visitor
utterance ut, the 768-d pooled representation is computed and kept compact by
projecting it with a fixed random projection matrixP ∈R 64×768 (held constant
across training runs for reproducibility):
et = DialogueBERT(ut,role = ”user”)∈R 768,i t =Pe t ∈R 64.
DialogueBERT encodes turn and speaker-role context and reports strong performance
on multi-turn understanding, aligning with this use case [Zhang et al., 2021b].

Total state dimension (5 exhibits, 4-option baseline): 6 (focus) + 9 (history) + 64 (last-turn

State dimension scaling: For n exhibits and |O| high-level options, the total dimension is
(n + 1) + (n + |O|) + 64 + 64 = 2n + |O| + 129. This design scales linearly with the number
of exhibits and with the size of the option set, while keeping the semantic embeddings
fixed at 128 dimensions total.
The last-turn vectori t is a compact ”semantic coordinate” for ut (64 numbers that
preserve the main meaning cues from the original 768-d space), whilec t carries a small,
separate summary of very recent dialogue (another 64 numbers). Keeping these as
two small pieces lets the policy read both ”what’s happening now” and ”what just
happened” without handling the full high-dimensional encoder output. Reducing 768 →64
preserves semantic distances: random projections maintain geometric structure with high
probability (Johnson-Lindenstrauss lemma), and empirical work shows that compact
sentence embeddings (e.g., SBERT/MiniLM at 384-d) preserve semantic structure to a
useful degree [Reimers and Gurevych, 2019, Wang et al., 2020].
This state design provides turn-aligned intent and short-context summaries while
keeping the observation vector compact enough for stable actor–critic training. Realization
details (dialogue action tokens, prompt headers, and KB grounding) are specified in
Section 6.6.
4.3 Action Space
Actions are modeled as options over subactions. The high-level policy chooses one of four
options; within an option, the intra-option policy selects a subaction. Each subaction

has a clear behavioral contract and typical effects on the state (e.g., knowledge coverage,
engagement). This thesis explores two principled approaches to grouping subactions into
options:function-based design(grouping by communicative function) andreward-aligned
design(grouping by reward component). Figure 5 illustrates both structures. Table 1
summarizes the subactions implemented in the system, shown here organized under the
function-based design.

Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
The option-subaction hierarchy mirrors contemporary dialogue-policy practice, mod-
eling strategic intents and surface realizations at complementary abstraction levels
[Rohmatillah and Chien, 2023, He et al., 2025]. Subactions provide tactical variants tailored
to moment-to-moment visitor signals, while options encode communication strategies for
museum interpretation: storytelling, inquiry, navigation, and closure [Chai-Arayalert and
Chai-Arayalert, 2024, Derda and colleagues, 2025, Woo et al., 2024]. This hierarchical
structure lets the option manager reason about long-horizon pacing while intra-option
policies focus on grounded realization [Reimann et al., 2024].
The planner takes the chosen option and subaction from the manager, builds shared
context (focus exhibit, LAST UTT, brief history, facts used/available), and hands off to the
matching subaction builder. Each builder has a simple contract:ExplainNewFactpicks a
new fact ID;RepeatFactandClarifyFactoperate only over facts already used; question
subactions respond to LAST UTT;SuggestMovecomputes coverage and selects the exhibit
with the most remaining unexplained content; andWrapUpcloses the session. The HRL
layer decides when to keep or switch options through learned termination.
Option design approaches. This work explores two principled approaches to grouping
subactions into options, each with distinct theoretical grounding and practical trade-offs.
Function-based designgroups subactions by communicative function, following estab-
lished dialogue act taxonomies from computational linguistics. Table 2 maps each option to
its corresponding communicative function category, grounded in ISO 24617-2 [Bunt et al.,
2011] and validated by empirical dialogue act research [Stolcke et al., 2000, Traum and
Hinkelman, 1992]. This design ensures the agent’s strategic repertoire covers core dialogue
acts: delivering information (Explain), engaging through questions (AskQuestion),
guiding navigation (OfferTransition), and social closure (Conclude), supported by
the ICAP framework’s identification of Interactive and Constructive engagement modes
[Chi and Wylie, 2014].

4.4 Reward Function Design
The reward function is designed to align the agent’s behavior with the goals of maintaining
visitor engagement while efficiently delivering diverse, relevant information about the
exhibits.

engagement signal. Other gaze features (fixation duration, saccade patterns, gaze entropy)
may provide additional engagement signals not captured by dwell alone. Future work
should explore multi-feature gaze representations that combine multiple eye-tracking
metrics for richer engagement estimation.

4.6 Summary
The formalization above connects the agent’s architecture to the research questions:
HRL manages dialogue strategy, gaze-based dwell tracks engagement in real time, and
structured prompts keep LLM outputs fluent yet on-plan. The design is modular—state,
rewards, options, and prompts can each be swapped or extended without rewriting the
rest. Section 5 describes the concrete system components (environment, simulator, agent,
action execution), and Section 6 details the training algorithm and reward extensions.
5 System Architecture
While the formal problem definition establishes the theoretical foundation for hierarchical
control in museum dialogue, deploying a working conversational agent requires concrete
implementation of the environment, simulator, agent architecture, and action execution
mechanisms. This section documents how the abstract formalization is realized in practice,
covering the system components that enable training and evaluation. The architecture
separates concerns: the environment computes state and rewards, the simulator provides
contingent visitor responses, the agent implements hierarchical decision-making, and the
dialogue planner translates strategic choices into natural utterances.
5.1 Environment Implementation
The environment implements the SMDP formulation from Section 4, providing state
computation, reward calculation, and action masking.
5.1.1 Knowledge Base
The knowledge base contains information about five Dutch Golden Age portrait exhibits,
curated based on a previous VR experiment conducted by a prior student. Table 5
summarizes the knowledge base structure.
36
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
Table 5: Knowledge Base Statistics
Exhibit Facts (AOIs)
The African King Caspar 7
Head of a Boy in a Turban 4
Portrait of Dom Miguel de Castro 4
Portrait of Pedro Sunda 3
Portrait of Diego Bemba 3
Total 21
Each fact corresponds to an Area of Interest (AOI) within an exhibit—a visually
distinct element (e.g., clothing, accessories, symbolic objects) with associated historical
and artistic information. Facts consist of 1–2 sentence descriptions averaging approximately
15 words each.
5.1.2 State Space Computation
The state vector st = [f t, ht, it, ct] is computed as follows. The focus vectorf t is a
one-hot encoding of the current exhibit (or no-focus state). The dialogue history vectorh t
concatenates exhibit completion ratios (facts mentioned per exhibit divided by total facts)
and normalized option usage counts. The intent embeddingi t is computed by projecting
the DialogueBERT encoding of the last user utterance through a fixed linear projection
matrixP ∈R 64×768. The dialogue contextc t applies the same projection to the average of
DialogueBERT encodings from the last three exchanges. This design scales linearly with
the number of exhibits: fornexhibits, the total dimension is 2n+ 5 + 128 = 2n+ 133.
5.1.3 Action Space and Masking
The hierarchical action space consists of four options, each with subactions. Action
masking enforces dialogue coherence:Concludeis masked until at least 3 facts and 2
exhibits have been covered;ExplainNewF actis masked when all facts for an exhibit
are exhausted;RepeatF actis masked when no facts have been mentioned yet. These
masks prevent structurally invalid actions and guide the policy toward coherent strategies.
5.1.4 Reward Calculation
Rewards are computed per-turn according to the reward function specification (Sec-
tion 4.4.1). The baseline reward function uses two components: engagement reward
reng
t = dwellt (weighted by we = 1.0) and novelty reward rnov
t = |Fused(t)| − |Fused(t− 1)|
(scaled by αnov = 1.0 for baseline experiments). Additional components (responsiveness,
transition insufficiency, conclude bonus, question-asking incentive) may be enabled for
augmented reward configurations. See Table 44 for parameter values across all experiments.
5.2 Simulator Design: Contingent Visitor Responses
For training, we use lightweight simulators instead of live visitors to provide immediate,
reproducible feedback on the agent’s dialogue strategies. The simulator’s primary output—
dwell time ratio—serves as the engagement signal in the reward function, making the
37
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
simulator design critical to what the agent learns. A well-designed simulator must balance
two competing demands: (i) providelearnable signalswith sufficient dynamic range that
the agent can discover which actions improve engagement, and (ii) avoid beingtrivially
solvedby simple heuristics that do not generalize to real visitors.
We implement and evaluate two simulator architectures that represent different points
on this trade-off. TheProbabilistic Response Simulator(Sim8) emphasizes stochasticity
and persona diversity, producing varied feedback that prevents overfitting but may intro-
duce ambiguous signals. TheVisitor State Machine Simulatoremphasizes deterministic
state transitions grounded in museum learning literature, producing clearer signals that
may accelerate learning but risk oversimplification. Testing both simulators allows us to
assess how simulator design affects learned policy quality and strategy diversity.
5.2.1 Probabilistic Response Simulator (Sim8)
Sim8 serves as our primary simulator for baseline comparisons. It is based on a visitor
simulator developed in prior work, trained on VR-based museum visitor interactions from
the same exhibition setting (Dutch Golden Age portrait collection). The original simulator
employed a dialogue generation model (T5) and a gaze synthesis model (Conditional
VAE), both conditioned on visitor persona (Agreeable, Conscientious, Neurotic based on
Big Five traits) and exhibit focus (Area of Interest).
To enable efficient RL training without requiring Colab infrastructure or large neural
model dependencies, we adapted the original Sim8 into a production friendly implemen-
tation that preserves the key behavioral patterns while improving reproducibility and
computational efficiency. The adapted simulator:
• Preserves response type logic: Determines visitor response types (question, statement,
acknowledgment, confusion, follow-up) based on agent behavior and conversation
context, with persona-specific tendencies (e.g., Neurotic visitors exhibit higher
baseline confusion rates).
• Preserves dwell range patterns: Uses the same overlapping dwell ranges by response
type observed in the original training data (Table 6), ensuring the engagement
ambiguity that makes credit assignment challenging is maintained.
• Maintains six gaze features: Synthesizes the same six eye-tracking features—scanpath
length, saccade span, gaze entropy, fixation change rate, dominant object ratio,
and entry latency—with persona-specific statistical distributions derived from the
original VR training data.
• Adds engagement shaping: Incorporates additional engagement dynamics (question
spacing, transition success probability, explain spam penalties) to provide clearer
learning signals during RL training while preserving the stochastic variability of the
original simulator.
The simulator determines response type at each turn based on agent behavior and
conversation context. The six gaze features synthesized per turn are: scanpath length (total
gaze path), saccade span (fixation jump distance), gaze entropy (attention distribution),
fixation change rate (gaze shift frequency), dominant object ratio (focus concentration),
and entry latency (time to first fixation). Dwell time—the primary engagement signal for
38
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
rewards—is derived from these features and response type, with base ranges reflecting
patterns observed in the training data.
Table 6 shows the base dwell ranges by response type, reflecting patterns learned from
the training data:
Table 6: Sim8 dwell ranges by response type (overlapping ranges in bold)
Response Type Dwell Range Engagement
Acknowledgment / Follow-up [0.75,0.95] High
Question [0.40,0.70] Moderate-high
Statement [0.30,0.60] Moderate
Confusion [0.25,0.50] Low
The overlapping ranges reflect ambiguity patterns in the training data: confusion
(0.25–0.50) overlaps with question (0.40–0.70), capturing the natural variability in how vis-
itors signal engagement states. This empirically-observed overlap makes credit assignment
challenging for policy learning—a visitor with 0 .45 dwell could be confused or moderately
curious. Reducing this ambiguity through theoretical signal design (State Machine) may
produce more diverse learned policies.
The adapted Sim8’s behavioral patterns are derived from prior VR visitor data but
have been simplified for RL training efficiency. While the core dwell ranges and response
type distributions match the original training data, some neural model stochasticity has
been replaced with statistical sampling. Results may not generalize to real museum
deployment where visitor behavior exhibits greater variability.
5.2.2 Visitor State Machine Simulator
The State Machine simulator was developed specifically to address a limitation observed
in Sim8: the empirically-grounded engagement signals from Sim8 were effectively non-
responsive to agent actions due to overlapping dwell ranges that obscure the relationship
between dialogue strategies and visitor engagement. This ambiguity makes it difficult to
assess whether the learning algorithms (MDP and SMDP) can fundamentally adapt their
behavior in response to engagement feedback, or whether observed policy differences stem
from the signal quality itself.
The State Machine simulator was designed to test a specific question:Can MDP and
SMDP architectures learn to use engagement-based actions (such as asking questions)
when hard-coded increases and decreases in engagement are implemented?By providing
deterministic, non-overlapping state boundaries, the State Machine isolates the effect of
signal quality on policy learning. If agents learn diverse, engagement-responsive strategies
under clear signals but not under ambiguous signals, this suggests the learning algorithms
are capable but limited by training environment fidelity.
The State Machine simulator serves as a pedagogical alternative, designed to provide
clearer learning signals than Sim8. Its design philosophy prioritizes signal clarity by
exaggerating the consequences of agent actions, we create an environment where credit
assignment is unambiguous. The tradeoff is reduced realism—real visitors do not provide
such clear-cut feedback. Tests involving the State Machine will test whether this
pedagogical approach produces faster learning and more diverse policies, despite being
less representative of deployment conditions.
39
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
The Visitor State Machine models visitor engagement as an explicit finite-state machine
with nine states (ranging fromHighlyEngagedtoDisengaged), deterministic triggers,
and non-overlapping dwell ranges. Compared to Sim8, it uses stricter thresholds (3+
instead of 4+ forOverloaded/F atigued) and implements anti-spam mechanisms that
make exploitative strategies unprofitable. The state machine design draws on established
findings:
The state machine design draws on established findings:
• OVERLOADED: Working memory limits of approximately 4 items [Cowan, 2001]
suggest visitors become overwhelmed after consecutive fact explanations without
interaction [Bitgood, 2013]. Recent research shows that information overload from
dense text and monotonous layouts causes cognitive overload [Zhang et al., 2024],
with digital tools increasing cognitive load without improving learning outcomes
[Chen et al., 2023a].
• FATIGUED: Adaptive museum agent research shows that spaced questioning every
3–5 turns maintains engagement; pure lecturing causes fatigue [Woo et al., 2024].
Recent work demonstrates that language arousal matched to topic improves visitor
satisfaction [Li et al., 2025], while physiological monitoring shows cognitive workload
increases with emotionally intense exhibits [Smith et al., 2025].
• CURIOUS: Gricean conversational maxims [Grice, 1975] predict that visitors expect
their questions to be answered; deflection violates the relevance maxim. Recent
research shows thatrelevanceviolations are the most frequent and most frustrating
in conversational systems [Panfili et al., 2021]. Multi-perspective conversational
agents enhance engagement by presenting diverse viewpoints [Wang et al., 2025].
• CONFUSED: Conversational repair sequences [Schegloff et al., 1977] show that
confusion requires explicit clarification to restore mutual understanding. Recent work
demonstrates that detecting ambiguous questions and asking clarifying questions
improves accuracy [Li et al., 2022], with multi-modal clarification systems showing
substantial gains [Shi et al., 2025]. Museum chatbots track unanswered questions to
enable targeted clarification [Consortium, 2023].
• READY TO MOVE: Variety-seeking behavior after exhibit satiation is well-
documented in museum studies [Bitgood, 2013]. Recent research shows that visitors
adapt their pace when satiated [Krukar and Dalton, 2020], with layout and guide
interface design significantly affecting transitions [Johnson et al., 2025]. Free-flow,
unstructured paths delay satiation and sustain satisfaction [Falk and Dierking,
2018].
The simulator maintains a visitor state s from a set of seven core states (plus two
escalation states:HighlyEngagedandBoredOfTopic) ranging from highly positive
to completely disengaged. State transitions are triggered by deterministic thresholds or
random events. Table 7 lists all states; Figure 7 visualizes the core 7-state transition graph.

The simulator also models exceptional engagement: when the agent maintains varied
engagement (using 2+ different action types over 3+ turns while the visitor remains
Engaged), the visitor escalates toHighlyEngagedwith the highest dwell range
([0.90,1.00]). This state decays back toEngagedafter 2 turns without varied stimulus,
teaching the agent that sustained diverse engagement yields bonus rewards.
Wrong actions have consequences: usingAskQuestionwhen the visitor isCurious
(deflection) triggers a transition toConfused; continuing to explain whenReadyTo-
MovetriggersF atigued.
To prevent the agent from indefinitely avoiding transitions at exhausted exhibits, the
simulator implements escalating penalties. If the visitor remains inReadyToMovefor
3+ turns without the agent offering a transition, the state escalates toDisengaged—
representing a visitor who has mentally checked out. Dwell time inReadyToMove
also decays at −0.10 per turn, creating progressively stronger negative signals. Recovery
fromDisengagedrequiresOfferTransitionwith only 50% success rate, teaching
the agent that prevention (timely transitions) is far more effective than recovery. This
escalation mechanism addresses the failure mode observed in early training where agents
would spamRepeatF actorClarifyF actat exhausted exhibits to avoid the novelty
penalty for repeating facts.
The State Machine implements three additional mechanisms to prevent exploitativeEx-
plainNewFactspam cycles (where an agent collects novelty rewards, triggersOverloaded,
recovers, and repeats):
1. Stricter thresholds:Overloadedtriggers after 3 consecutive explains (vs. Sim8’s
implicit 4+), andF atiguedtriggers after 3 turns without questions.
2. Recovery fatigue: Each successful recovery reduces future recovery success rates by
15%, capped at 45% reduction after 3 recoveries. This makes the spam-recover-repeat
cycle progressively less viable.
3. Cumulative overload penalty: EachOverloadedepisode in a session lowers the
dwell floor by 0.05, creating escalating costs for repeated information dumps.
These mechanisms ensure that exploitative strategies become unprofitable after 2–3 cycles,
encouraging the agent to learn genuinely diverse, visitor-responsive behaviors. Sim8 does
not implement these mechanisms, testing whether policies can learn appropriate behavior
from ambiguous signals alone.
Beyond tracking engagement numerically, the State Machine simulator generates
naturalistic visitor dialogue that reflects the current emotional state. Each state has
an associated LLM prompt template that instructs the language model to produce
utterances consistent with that state’s psychological profile. For instance, when the
visitor isOverloaded, the prompt instructs generation of responses expressing cognitive
fatigue (e.g., “That’s a lot to take in...” or “Wait, I’m getting confused with all these
details”). WhenCurious, the prompt generates inquisitive responses with follow-up
questions (e.g., “What year was this created?” or “How does that relate to the artist’s
earlier work?”). This prompt-conditioned generation ensures that the textual dialogue
presented to the agent aligns with the underlying engagement state, providing coherent
multimodal training signals where both the numerical dwell time and the linguistic
42
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
content reflect the visitor’s simulated emotional state.
