The State Machine simulator provides a 0.20 dwell gap between positive states
(minimum 0.75) and negative states (maximum 0.55), compared to Sim8’s overlapping
ranges. Empirical testing shows that an agent using optimal recovery actions achieves 71%
higher cumulative dwell than an agent ignoring state signals, compared to approximately
12% improvement under Sim8. With theDisengagedescalation mechanics, the gap
widens further: agents that fail to transition at exhausted exhibits see dwell drop to
[0.05, 0.15], creating an unmistakable penalty. This difference in signal strength motivates
testing whether clearer simulator signals produce qualitatively different learned policies.
The State Machine’s explicit state labels, anti-spam mechanisms, and non-overlapping
dwell ranges may appear overly engineered compared to real human behavior. However,
these design choices model what happens when visitors provide clear feedback—which real
visitors do, though through different modalities. A visitor who becomes overloaded does not
maintain neutral 0.40–0.70 dwell; they look away (dwell < 0.30), verbally express confusion
(”wait, that’s too much”), or physically disengage. A curious visitor actively focuses on
details (dwell > 0.70) and asks follow-up questions. The State Machine exaggerates the
*clarity* of these signals for pedagogical purposes, but the underlying structure—discrete
behavioral states with distinct engagement levels—reflects how humans communicate
attention in museum settings. Sim8’s overlapping ranges (confusion 0.25–0.50 vs question
0.40–0.70) represent measurement noise and individual variability, but they obscure
the central tendency that State Machine captures: engaged visitors provide distinctly
different signals than disengaged ones. Both simulators serve valid purposes: Sim8 tests
policy robustness under realistic ambiguity; State Machine tests learning capacity under
ideal signal conditions. Real deployment likely falls between these extremes, and H3
establishes that the algorithm’s performance scales with signal quality—a finding with
43
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
direct implications for human-in-the-loop training where multimodal visitor feedback
(gaze + verbal + physical proximity) may approach State Machine clarity rather than
Sim8 ambiguity.
5.2.4 Transition Logic: Probabilistic Acceptance
Both simulators model transitions as probabilistic events conditioned on exhibit completion.
When the agent selectsOfferTransition, the simulator:
1. Computes current exhibit completion:c=|F current|/|Ftotal|.
2. Mapscto acceptance probabilityp accept (staged: 0.20 / 0.50 / 0.80 / 0.95).
3. Draws a Bernoulli random variable; on success, updates current exhibit to
target exhibit; on failure, remains at current exhibit with a confused response.
This mechanismincentivizesdepth before breadth by making transition success more
likely after sufficient content delivery, counteracting the tendency toward superficial
breadth-seeking behavior. Research on museum learning emphasizes that visitors need
time for meaning-making before moving on [Falk and Dierking, 2016], and abrupt exhibit
switches disrupt narrative coherence [Chai-Arayalert and Chai-Arayalert, 2024], providing
theoretical justification for this staged probability design.
5.3 Agent Architecture
The agent implements hierarchical decision-making through an Actor-Critic network
with option-level and subaction-level policies. The network outputs option probabilities,
intra-option subaction probabilities, termination probabilities, and value estimates. Option
selection follows the Option-Critic architecture [Bacon et al., 2017]: the agent selects an
option, executes it for multiple turns (with learned termination), then selects a new option
based on the updated state. Action masking constrains available options and subactions
based on dialogue state.
5.4 Action Masking and Strategic Constraints
To prevent structurally invalid actions and guide the policy toward coherent strategies, we
implement action masking at both the option and subaction levels. Masking dynamically
adjusts the available action set based on the current dialogue state, effectively encoding
domain constraints as hard constraints on the policy [Huang et al., 2020, Tang et al., 2021].
5.4.1 Option-Level Masking
The primary option-level constraint is onConclude: the agent may not end the session
prematurely. We define two thresholds:
•min facts before conclude = 3 : At least 3 facts must have been mentioned across
all exhibits.
44
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
•min exhibits before conclude = 2 : At least 2 distinct exhibits must have been
discussed (i.e., have|F e|>0).
If either condition is unmet,Concludeis masked (unavailable). This prevents the
pathological policy of concluding immediately for safety, which occasionally emerged in
early unconstrained training (yielding zero novelty and conclude bonus, but avoiding any
risk of low dwell from bad explanations—a local minimum).
The thresholds are set based on minimal tour expectations: a “guided tour” that
covers only one exhibit with 1–2 facts is not a tour at all. The values (3 facts, 2
exhibits) represent the bare minimum for a coherent session. Empirically, trained policies
typically exceed these thresholds significantly (avg. 8–12 facts, 5–7 exhibits), so the
masking acts as a safety guardrail rather than a binding constraint during normal operation.
5.4.2 Subaction-Level Masking
Within theExplainoption, we mask subactions based on knowledge availability:
• ExplainNewFact is masked if all facts for the current exhibit have already been
mentioned (|F new
current| = 0). This prevents the agent from attempting to introduce
“new” facts when none remain, which would force the LLM to either hallucinate or
produce a generic non-explanation.
• RepeatFact is masked if no facts have been mentioned yet for the current exhibit
(|F used
current| = 0). This prevents the agent from trying to “repeat” something that was
never said.
• ClarifyFact is always available (no masking), as clarification can be provided even
without explicit fact repetition (e.g., defining terms, providing analogies based on
prior context).
Action masking is implemented by setting invalid action logits to −∞ (or −1010 in
practice) before applying softmax, ensuring invalid actions have zero probability and are
never sampled during policy selection [Huang et al., 2020]. This follows the standard
invalid action masking approach for policy gradient algorithms, which preserves valid
gradient updates while preventing structurally invalid actions [Huang et al., 2020]. The
masks are computed via get available subactions(option) and applied during both
option and subaction selection in the actor network. As a safety fallback, if an invalid
action index is somehow selected (e.g., due to index misalignment), the environment’s
apply action masks() method clamps it to the first available valid action, ensuring
dialogue coherence.
Masking serves two purposes. First, it providessafety: the agent cannot produce
structurally nonsensical moves (e.g., repeating a fact that was never mentioned). Second, it
providespedagogical guidance: by disablingExplainNewFactwhen the exhibit is exhausted,
we force the agent to either ask questions, transition, or conclude—preventing it from
“spinning” on the same exhibit indefinitely. Studies of hierarchical RL show that task-
structure-aware masking improves both sample efficiency and final performance by pruning
the action space to semantically valid choices [Huang et al., 2020].
45
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
5.5 Summary: System Architecture
The architecture documented in this section operationalizes the formal hierarchical
framework into a practical, trainable dialogue agent. The environment computes state
and rewards, the simulator provides contingent feedback that attempts to reflect realistic
visitor behavior and reward good dialogue moves with high engagement and positive
responses, penalizing poor moves with confusion and disengagement. The agent imple-
ments hierarchical decision-making through Option-Critic architecture, and grounding
mechanisms (fact verification, prompt contracts, action masking) ensure the agent stays
faithful to the knowledge base and produces structurally coherent dialogue strategies.
Together, it is hypothesized that these components enable the hierarchical policy to
learn long-horizon conversational strategies: the manager learns which options to deploy
and when to switch based on engagement and intent signals, while intra-option policies
learn how to realize those strategies through structured prompting of the LLM. The result
is a system that balances the flexibility and fluency of large language models with the
coherence and goal-directedness of learned hierarchical control.
6 Training Methodology
The formal problem definition (Section 4) establishes the theoretical foundation, and the
system architecture (Section 5) describes the implementation. This section documents
the learning algorithm: how the agent updates its option policy, intra-option policies, and
termination functions from experience. We detail the actor-critic update rules, advantage
computation, termination learning, and training stability mechanisms.
6.1 Actor-Critic for Hierarchical Options
We adopt an actor-critic scheme tailored to the options framework [Sutton et al., 1999,
Bacon et al., 2017]. The agent maintains three learnable components: (i) the option policy
πΩ(ω|s) selecting which strategy to pursue, (ii) intra-option policies πU|ω (a|s, ω) selecting
subactions within each option, and (iii) termination functions βω(s) determining when to
end each option. The critic learns three value functions to score these decisions.
We implement the full Option-Critic algorithm [Bacon et al., 2017], which uses
termination-mixed bootstrapping targets U(s′, ω) for option and intra-option value updates.
The termination mixing function U(s′, ω) = (1 −β ω(s′))QΩ(s′, ω) + βω(s′)V (s′) accounts
for whether the option continues or terminates at the next state, enabling proper credit
assignment in hierarchical policies. The termination learning mechanism (Section 6.2)
uses the option-level advantage AO(s, ω) = QΩ(s, ω) −V (s) to drive termination decisions,
following the standard Option-Critic formulation.
6.1.1 Value Functions
The critic maintains three value functions, each serving a distinct role in the hierarchical
decision process:
•V (s): State value—expected return from state s under the current hierarchical
policy. For brevity we writeV(s) for the state-value induced by the current policy
over options.
46
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
•Q Ω(s, ω): Option value—expected return from executing optionωin states.
•Q U(s, ω, a): Intra-option value—expected return from taking subaction a within
optionωin states.
These functions are related hierarchically: V (s) =P
ω πΩ(ω|s)QΩ(s, ω) and QΩ(s, ω) =P
a πU|ω (a|s, ω)QU(s, ω, a).
6.1.2 Value Function Updates
Value functions are updated using temporal difference learning with TD(0) targets
computed at each step, following the Option-Critic formulation [Bacon et al., 2017]. The
key innovation of Option-Critic is the termination-mixed bootstrapping target U(s′, ω),
which accounts for whether the option continues or terminates:
U(s ′, ω) = (1−β ω(s′))QΩ(s′, ω) +β ω(s′)V(s ′)
where βω(s′) is the termination probability of option ω at state s′. When βω(s′) ≈ 0,
the option continues and we bootstrap from QΩ(s′, ω); when βω(s′) ≈ 1, the option
terminates and we bootstrap fromV(s ′). The TD targets are:
yV
t =r t +γV(s t+1), y QΩ
t =r t +γU(s t+1, ωt), y QU
t =r t +γU(s t+1, ωt)
The corresponding TD errors are:
δV
t =y V
t −V(s t), δ QΩ
t =y QΩ
t −Q Ω(st, ω), δ QU
t =y QU
t −Q U(st, ω, a)
Value functions are updated using these TD errors: V (st) ←V (st)+ αδV
t , QΩ(st, ω) ←
QΩ(st, ω) + αδQΩ
t , and QU(st, ω, a) ←Q U(st, ω, a) + αδQU
t , where α is the learning rate
and γ is the discount factor. The termination-mixed target U(st+1, ωt) ensures that both
option and intra-option value functions properly account for option continuation versus
termination, enabling accurate credit assignment in hierarchical policies.
6.1.3 Advantage Computation
Following Option-Critic [Bacon et al., 2017], we compute two advantage functions that
drive policy updates at different levels of the hierarchy:
The option-level advantage measures whether continuing the current option outper-
forms switching:
AO(s, ω) =Q Ω(s, ω)−V(s)
Positive AO indicates the option is better than average; negative AO suggests switching
would improve performance.
Meanwhile, the intra-option advantage measures whether a specific subaction
outperforms the option’s average:
AU(s, ω, a) =Q U(s, ω, a)−Q Ω(s, ω)
This guides subaction selection within the active option. Both advantages are
normalized per batch (zero mean, unit variance) to reduce gradient variance [Henderson
et al., 2018].
47
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
6.1.4 Policy Gradient Updates
The option policy updates using option-level advantages:
∇θΩJ=E[∇ θΩ logπ Ω(ω|s)·A O(s, ω)]
The intra-option policies update using intra-option advantages:
∇θU J=E

∇θU logπ U|ω (a|s, ω)·A U(s, ω, a)

These gradients are computed per-step using TD errors computed at each timestep, but
gradient updates are accumulated during the episode and applied in a single backward pass
after each episode completes. This approach combines online per-step value estimation
with episode-level policy gradient updates, following standard actor-critic practice [Bacon
et al., 2017, Henderson et al., 2018].
6.2 Termination Learning
A fundamental challenge in hierarchical reinforcement learning is determining when to
terminate an option and return control to the high-level policy. The termination function
βω(s) ∈ [0, 1] represents the probability of ending option ω at state s. Learning effective
termination is critical: premature termination fragments coherent multi-turn strategies,
while delayed termination traps the agent in suboptimal options that no longer serve
the current dialogue context. Option-Critic addresses this through a learned termination
gradient that uses the option-level advantage signal [Bacon et al., 2017]:
∇βω J≈ −E[∇ βω βω(s)·A O(s, ω)]
where AO(s, ω) = QΩ(s, ω) −V (s) measures whether continuing the current option
outperforms switching to another. When AO > 0, the gradient drives βω(s) → 0,
encouraging continuation; when AO < 0, it drives βω(s) → 1, encouraging termination.
This mechanism enables the agent to learn adaptive pacing, persisting with valuable
strategies while exiting when context changes or switching would improve performance.
However, the standard Option-Critic termination gradient can suffer from option
persistence—a known failure mode where agents remain in options even when switching
would be beneficial, particularly when option-level advantages remain marginally positive
due to accumulated past rewards [Harb et al., 2018]. To address this, a deliberation cost
mechanism can be incorporated that penalizes option persistence per step: rt ←r t −c delib
where cdelib > 0 is a tunable parameter. This creates consistent negative pressure for
option continuation, encouraging termination when AO(s, ω) ≈ 0 and making persistent
option execution marginally costly. The deliberation cost is subtracted from all rewards
during training before computing TD targets, providing complementary counter-pressure
to the learned termination function. This approach follows established practices in
hierarchical RL where small per-step costs help prevent degenerate policies that exploit
option persistence [Harb et al., 2018]. In the baseline configuration, deliberation cost is
set to zero ( cdelib = 0.0) to evaluate the standard Option-Critic formulation; values in the
range 0.001–0.015 have been shown to improve option switching in hierarchical dialogue
settings.
48
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
6.3 Training Stability
Actor-critic methods for hierarchical RL face several sources of instability: high variance
in policy gradients due to hierarchical credit assignment, gradient scale mismatches
between option-level and intra-option advantages, and value function divergence from
learning multiple Q-functions simultaneously. We employ four standard stabilization
mechanisms from the reinforcement learning literature to ensure stable training across all
three hierarchical components.
Advantage normalization standardizes advantages per batch to zero mean and unit
variance, reducing variance in policy gradients without biasing the expected gradient
direction [Henderson et al., 2018]. This is particularly important when option-level
advantages operate at different scales than intra-option advantages, as hierarchical value
functions can have different magnitudes. Entropy regularization adds −λHH(π) to the
policy loss, penalizing deterministic policies and maintaining exploration throughout
training. We use λH = 0.01 for both option and intra-option policies, following standard
practice in actor-critic algorithms [Bacon et al., 2017]. Gradient clipping limits gradient
norms to 0.5, preventing exploding gradients that can destabilize training when value
functions learn at different rates or have different scales. Finally, value clipping constrains
value predictions and targets to [ −10, 10] in the baseline configuration, preventing value
function divergence that could cause unbounded policy gradients. These mechanisms are
standard in deep RL implementations and are particularly critical for hierarchical methods
where multiple value functions and policies must learn simultaneously [Henderson et al.,
2018, Bacon et al., 2017].
6.4 Hyperparameters
Table 9 summarizes the baseline training hyperparameters used for initial architecture
comparisons. Hyperparameter values are selected to follow established Option-Critic
practice where applicable (termination learning, advantage computation), and common
actor-critic conventions otherwise, with specific values chosen to balance stability and
learning efficiency in dialogue settings.
Core learning parameters follow the Option-Critic architecture [Bacon et al., 2017]:
learning rate (1 × 10−4) and discount factor. We use γ = 0.99 in the primary baseline
experiments; we additionally evaluate alternative discount factors in Section 8.14 to assess
sensitivity. The entropy coefficient ( λH = 0.01) matches the Option-Critic paper and
maintains exploration without excessive randomness, consistent with actor-critic literature
[Bacon et al., 2017, Henderson et al., 2018]. Termination regularization (0.01) scales
the termination loss in the total objective, matching the Option-Critic regularization
parameter that prevents premature termination [Bacon et al., 2017].
Loss weighting follows common actor-critic practice: the value loss coefficient (0.5)
balances policy and value learning in the actor-critic objective, following established
practices in deep RL implementations [Henderson et al., 2018]. Stabilization hyperparam-
eters use conservative bounds selected for training stability: gradient clipping (norm 0.5)
limits gradient magnitudes following established practices in actor-critic algorithms [Mnih
et al., 2016, Henderson et al., 2018], and value clipping constrains value predictions and
targets to [ −10, 10] to prevent value function divergence, using bounds selected to allow
learning while preventing unbounded growth [Henderson et al., 2018]. Deliberation cost
(cdelib = 0.0) is set to zero in the baseline configuration; this parameter can be tuned (set
to values>0) to address option persistence following Harb et al. [Harb et al., 2018].

Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue

Parameter Description Value

Discount factor (γ) Future reward weighting 0.99
Entropy coefficient (λH) Exploration bonus weight 0.01

6.6 Action Execution and Grounding
The hierarchical policy selects high-level options and low-level subactions, but these must
be realized as concrete utterances through the LLM-based dialogue planner. This section
details how subactions are executed, how knowledge-graph grounding is enforced, and
how hallucination is detected.
6.6.1 Dialogue Action Tokens
The option manager commits to a conversational strategy and decides when to switch;
realization translates that strategy into natural utterances. Each option has a compact
prompt header—a short template with slots for context—that frames the next turn. The
LLM realizes the turn freely under that header.
This is a variant of plan-aware prompting that combines LLM fluency with hierarchical
control. Conversation Routines demonstrate that reusable prompt schemas with context
slots keep generation on-plan while preserving natural language [Robino, 2025]. Planning
tokens show that prefixed step cues improve structure and controllability [Wang et al.,
2024]. Together, these results motivate using lightweight headers to shape turns while
keeping text natural.
The limitation is temporal: prompt headers decide what to say now, not when to
change tactics. That decision sits with the strategic layer. The termination function—not
an arbitrary token budget—governs when the header should change.
6.6.2 Prompt Construction
Each subaction has a behavioral contract: what the agent must do, which facts it may
reference, and which it must avoid. Table 1 in Section 4 summarizes all subactions
implemented in the system and Appendix 15 shows the abbreviated prompts for each
subaction. The dialogue planner implements these contracts through subaction-specific
prompt builders. Here we detail prompt construction for two representative examples that
illustrate the design principles:ExplainNewFact, which demonstrates knowledge-graph
50
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
grounding and fact selection, andOfferTransition, which demonstrates coverage-aware
state computation.
ExplainNewFact introduces a fact from the knowledge graph that has not been
mentioned for the current exhibit. The prompt builder retrieves all facts for the current
exhibit, filters out those already mentioned, and constructs a prompt with header OPTION:
Explain | SUBACTION: ExplainNewFact | FOCUS: [exhibit] . The context block lists
available new facts with IDs and instructs: “Choose ONE new fact and explain it in 2–3
sentences. Include the fact ID in brackets.” Here is an example prompt:
OPTION: Explain
SUBACTION: ExplainNewFact
FOCUS: The Annunciation Triptych
LAST UTT: ‘‘What’s the symbolism in this painting?’’
Available new facts:
- The dove represents the Holy Spirit [AN 002]
- Gold leaf was used for divine figures [AN 003]
Your response (include fact ID):
The LLM generates: “The dove you see at the top represents the Holy Spirit [AN 002],
a common symbol in Annunciation scenes.”
OfferTransition assesses coverage and proposes moving to the exhibit with the
most unexplained facts. The prompt builder computes coverage for all exhibits,
selects e∗ = arg maxe(|F total
e | − |F used
e |), and constructs a prompt with header OPTION:
OfferTransition | TARGET: [e*] . This ensures transitions are coverage-aware rather
than random.
6.6.3 Fact Verification
A critical failure mode for LLM-based agents is hallucination. We enforce grounding
through post-generation verification:
1. Extract fact IDs from the agent’s utterance via regex:[A-Z]{2} \d{3}.
2. Validate against knowledge graph: check if each ID exists in the current exhibit’s
facts.
3. Classify as: (i) new and valid, (ii) repeat (already mentioned), or (iii) hallucinated
(not in KB).
Hallucinated facts do not contribute to the novelty reward. Early experiments
showed that explicit hallucination penalties caused the agent to avoid facts entirely—an
overcorrection. Instead, the absence of positive reward and correlation with low-dwell
responses naturally discourage hallucination. Empirically, hallucination rates dropped
from 8% early in training to under 2% at convergence. The implementation maintains
per-exhibit fact sets:
self.facts_mentioned_per_exhibit: Dict[str, Set[str]] = {
ex: set() for ex in self.exhibit_keys
}
51
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
After each turn, the environment parses fact IDs, validates them, and updates the set.
Only validated facts from the current exhibit’s knowledge subgraph count toward rewards,
preventing cross-contamination.
7 Hypotheses
Having established the system architecture and training methodology, we now formulate
testable hypotheses that address the research questions posed in Section 3. Each hypothesis
corresponds directly to a research question and specifies testable predictions with clear
success criteria. Table 10 summarizes the mapping between research questions and
hypotheses.
Table 10: Research Question to Hypothesis Mapping
Research Question Hypothesis
RQ1: Hierarchical structure H1: SMDP vs MDP under baseline rewards
RQ2: Reward shaping H2: Augmented reward function effectiveness
RQ3: Simulator signals H3: Visitor simulator signal quality + recovery
behavior
RQ4: State efficiency H4: State representation efficiency
RQ5: Option design philosophy H5: Function-based (4 options) vs reward-aligned (4
options)
7.1 H1: Hierarchical Policy Structure
Hypothesis: An SMDP (hierarchical) policy will outperform an MDP (flat) policy on
long-horizon dialogue objectives when both are trained under identical baseline reward
conditions (engagement + novelty only). Systematic hyperparameter optimization will
improve SMDP performance beyond baseline configurations.
Rationale: Hierarchical structure via options provides temporal abstraction that
enables sustained strategic commitment over long dialogue horizons. Flat policies must
make primitive action decisions at each turn, making it difficult to maintain coherent
multi-turn strategies and attribute credit across extended sequences. Options allow the
policy to commit to high-level strategies (e.g., explaining an exhibit) over multiple turns,
improving credit assignment for long-horizon objectives. The baseline reward function
provides a clean foundation for testing architectural differences without confounding effects
from auxiliary reward shaping.
H1 includes systematic hyperparameter optimization to test whether SMDP perfor-
mance can be improved through tuning. This follows a funnel-style experimental design:
(1) baseline comparison (SMDP vs MDP), (2) sensitivity analyses (gamma, learning rate,
entropy coefficient, max option duration), (3) interaction studies (gamma×learning
rate, gamma×entropy), (4) Option-Critic ablations (deliberation cost, termination
regularization), (5) advanced architectural modifications, and (6) termination mechanism
alternatives. This systematic exploration tests whether hyperparameter optimization can
address known Option-Critic challenges [Bacon et al., 2017, Harb et al., 2018] and close
any performance gap between hierarchical and flat architectures.
Predictions:
52
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
1. SMDP will achieve higher episodic return compared to MDP under baseline condi-
tions
2. SMDP will achieve higher exhibit coverage compared to MDP, indicating more
effective exploration of available content
3. SMDP will maintain more balanced option distribution compared to MDP’s action-
level patterns, indicating effective utilization of hierarchical structure
4. Systematic hyperparameter optimization will improve SMDP performance metrics
beyond baseline configurations
7.2 H2: Augmented Reward Function Effectiveness
Hypothesis: Adding auxiliary reward components (responsiveness, transition penalties,
conclude bonus, question-asking incentive) improves policy learning beyond the baseline
reward function for both SMDP and MDP architectures.
Rationale: Based on Sim8’s reward structure, engagement optimization is expected
to be more difficult to learn, whileExplainNewFactdominance is a direct consequence
of the novelty reward being exclusively associated with this action. This mechanistic
relationship between reward structure and action selection creates an inherent bias
toward continuous explanation. Auxiliary reward components, grounded in museum
conversational agent literature, provide explicit incentives for behaviors the simulator
undervalues: responsiveness [Woo et al., 2024, Chai-Arayalert and Chai-Arayalert, 2024],
transition timing [Falk and Dierking, 2016, Reimann et al., 2024], coverage breadth [Derda
and colleagues, 2025, Bitgood, 2013], and proactive questioning [Woo et al., 2024]. Both
architectures use optimized hyperparameters from H1 to isolate reward engineering effects
from hyperparameter tuning.
Predictions:
1. Both SMDP and MDP will achieve higher episodic return and exhibit coverage
under augmented rewards compared to baseline rewards
2. Both architectures will show more balanced action distribution under augmented
rewards, with increased usage of behaviors explicitly incentivized by auxiliary
components
7.3 H3: Visitor Simulator Signal Quality
Hypothesis: A theory-driven simulator with clearer reward signals (State Machine) will
produce policies with greater strategy diversity and more effective use of recovery actions
compared to an empirically-informed simulator with overlapping signals (Sim8).
Rationale: This hypothesis tests a fundamental tradeoff in simulator design: empirical
grounding versus signal clarity. Sim8’s data-driven approach produces overlapping
dwell ranges (Table 6) that reflect the ambiguity observed in real visitor gaze patterns.
The State Machine prioritizes pedagogical clarity: its non-overlapping ranges (Table 7)
provide clear separation between positive and negative states, making credit assignment
unambiguous. The State Machine also implements stricter thresholds and anti-spam
mechanisms that make exploitative strategies unprofitable. When engagement signals are
53
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
clear and informative, both SMDP and MDP architectures can learn effective engagement
strategies that respond appropriately to visitor state.
Recovery behavior refers to the agent’s ability to recognize when the visitor is in a
negative state (confused, overloaded, disengaged) and take appropriate actions to restore
engagement. For example, when a visitor is confused, the agent should useClarifyFact
orAskQuestionactions rather than continuing to explain new facts. When a visitor
is overloaded, the agent should pause explanations and use recovery actions. In Sim8,
recovery actions lack explicit reward signals, making it impossible to determine whether
RL can learn to respond appropriately to engagement states. The State Machine simulator
addresses this limitation by providing clear, state-dependent reward signals that enable
learning of context-appropriate recovery behaviors, justifying its development as a testbed
for engagement-responsive dialogue policies.
Predictions:
1. State Machine-trained policies will achieve greater strategy diversity compared to
Sim8-trained policies for both SMDP and MDP architectures
2. State Machine-trained policies will demonstrate more effective recovery behavior
compared to Sim8-trained policies
7.4 H4: State Representation Efficiency
Hypothesis: State representation can be made more efficient without sacrificing perfor-
mance through compact dialogue-act classification.
Rationale: Real-world deployment requires computational efficiency. Dialogue-act
classification is a compact representation that maps utterances to probability distributions
over communicative functions (e.g., Inform, Question, Directive, Commissive) following
established dialogue act taxonomies [Bunt et al., 2011, Stolcke et al., 2000]. Rather than
using dense text embeddings that capture semantic content, dialogue-act classification
provides a lightweight summary of what communicative function the visitor’s utterance
serves. Reduced-dimensionality representations (23-d dialogue-act probability vectors
rather than 143-d DialogueBERT embeddings) may maintain policy quality while reducing
inference cost and improving data efficiency. This tests whether reduced dimensionality
maintains learning effectiveness while reducing computational requirements.
Predictions:
1. Compact dialogue-act classification will achieve comparable episodic return and
exhibit coverage compared to dense DialogueBERT representation
2. Compact representation will achieve faster training convergence compared to dense
representation
7.5 H5: Option Design Philosophy
Hypothesis: Option design philosophy affects how SMDP learns credit assignment,
termination behavior, and strategy diversity. A reward-aligned design (grouping actions by
reward component) will produce more balanced option usage and faster learning compared
to a function-based design (grouping actions by communicative function).
Rationale: Option design is a fundamental choice in hierarchical RL. Two principled
approaches emerge from the literature:
54
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
Function-based design. Groups actions by communicative function, following dialogue
act taxonomies [Bunt et al., 2011, Stolcke et al., 2000, Chi and Wylie, 2014]:
•Explain→Inform:ExplainNewFact,RepeatFact,ClarifyFact
•AskQuestion→Question:AskOpinion,AskMemory,AskClarification
•OfferTransition→Directive:SuggestMove
•Conclude→Closing:WrapUp
Advantage: Multiple subactions per option allow flexible within-option strategies. Risk:
Diffuse credit assignment—the same option can produce different reward types. When
ExplainNewFactis masked, the agent can still select Explain using fallback subactions.
HRL literature suggests termination functions may struggle when options mix reward
types [Bacon et al., 2017].
Reward-aligned design. Groups actions by reward component alignment [Kulkarni
et al., 2016, Machado et al., 2017], with option-level masking as a structural constraint
[Huang et al., 2020]:
•Explain(novelty):ExplainNewFactonly; masked when exhibit exhausted
•Engage(engagement):RepeatFact,ClarifyFact, allAskQuestionvariants
•Transition(coverage):SuggestMoveonly
•Conclude(termination):WrapUponly
Advantage: One-to-one reward-option correspondence enables clean credit assignment.
Option masking ensures options are only available when their associated rewards are
achievable. Risk: Reduced within-option flexibility.
Both designs use four options, isolating the grouping philosophy. Figure 5 illustrates
both option structures.
Predictions:
1. Reward-aligned design will produce more balanced option usage compared to
function-based design
2. Reward-aligned design will learn faster compared to function-based design due to
cleaner reward-option alignment
3. Both designs will achieve comparable final performance given identical option count
and action coverage
8 Evaluation
To test the hypotheses formulated in Section 7, we use a controlled simulator-based
evaluation. This section describes how we run experiments and what we measure.
55
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
8.1 Experimental Setup and Metrics
All experiments follow the same basic protocol. We fix the exhibit itinerary, visitor persona
seed, and knowledge base across runs, changing only the model variant. This isolates
architectural and algorithmic effects from environmental variability. Each condition trains
for 1000 episodes with n≥ 3 random seeds. We report mean ± standard deviation and
compute behavioral metrics from the final 100 episodes after training converges.
We evaluate all models on five core metrics that enable direct comparison across
hypotheses, plus additional metrics specific to each hypothesis’s research question.
8.2 Core Metrics
The five core metrics used across all experiments are:
• Episodic Return: Cumulative reward per episode, measuring overall policy perfor-
mance
• Exhibit Coverage: Percentage of facts mentioned from the knowledge base (0–100%),
measuring content delivery breadth
• Engagement (Mean Dwell): Average normalized gaze attention across turns (0.0–1.0),
measuring visitor attention
• Learning Efficiency: Episodes to reach 80% of final mean return, measuring sample
complexity
• Policy Stability: Standard deviation of returns in final 100 episodes, measuring
convergence reliability
8.3 Results Roadmap
This thesis tests five hypotheses comparing hierarchical (SMDP/Option-Critic) and flat
(MDP/Actor-Critic) reinforcement learning for museum dialogue agents:
•H1: Can systematic optimization close the SMDP-MDP performance gap?
•H2: Do augmented reward components improve SMDP learning?
•H3: Does simulator signal clarity affect learning quality?
•H4: Can compact state representations maintain performance?
•H5: Does reward-aligned option design reduce option collapse?
Primary metric: Exhibit coverage (% of 21 facts mentioned). Secondary metrics:
Return, Option Collapse Index (OCI), policy entropy.
Statistical reporting: All results use n≥ 3 seeds unless marked “exploratory (n=1)”.
We report mean ± SD computed from the final 100 episodes of each seed. Per-seed results
are shown for key comparisons to demonstrate consistency.
Verdict format: Each hypothesis concludes with a verdict:Supported,Partially
supported, orNot supported.
56
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue

8.6 Evaluation Plans by Hypothesis
8.7 H1: Hierarchical Policy Structure
H1 tests whether systematic hyperparameter optimization can improve SMDP performance.
All conditions use baseline rewards (engagement + novelty only) to isolate architectural
effects from reward shaping.
8.7.1 Design
H1 follows a funnel-style experimental design progressing from broad exploration to
targeted interventions:
1. Baseline Comparison: SMDP vs MDP (flat) under identical baseline conditions
2. Sensitivity Analyses: Single-parameter sweeps for gamma (discount factor), learning
rate, entropy coefficient, and max option duration
3. Interaction Studies: Factorial designs testing gamma×learning rate and gamma×
entropy interactions
4. Option-Critic Ablations: Deliberation cost and termination regularization mecha-
nisms from foundational OC literature
5. Advanced Architectural Modifications: Separate learning rates, diversity rewards,
value clipping adjustments, and other architectural interventions
6. Termination Mechanism Alternatives: Beta supervision and hard duration budgets
as alternatives to learned termination
8.7.2 Metrics
H1 uses the 5 core metrics plus option distribution (usage frequency across options),
Option Collapse Index (OCI), and policy entropy to diagnose option collapse and compare
SMDP vs MDP performance.
8.8 H2: Augmented Reward Function Effectiveness
H2 tests whether adding auxiliary reward components improves learning for both SMDP
and MDP.
59
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
8.8.1 Design
H2 involves two comparisons: (1)Within-architecture: SMDP-Augmented vs SMDP-
Baseline (from H1) and MDP-Augmented vs MDP-Baseline (from H1), testing whether
augmented rewards improve performance for each architecture; (2)Cross-architecture:
comparing improvement magnitude between SMDP and MDP to test whether reward
shaping benefits differ by architecture.
The augmented reward function adds four components to the baseline (see Section 4.4.1
for complete specification): (1)Responsiveness component( wresp = 0.25): rewards
timely answers to visitor questions; (2)Transition insufficiency penalties( −0.20 for <
2 facts): penalizes premature transitions; (3)Conclude bonus( wconclude = 0.2): rewards
broader museum coverage at wrap-up; (4)Question-asking incentive( wask = 0.15):
rewards proactive question-asking.
Both architectures use optimized hyperparameters from H1 to isolate reward engineer-
ing effects from hyperparameter tuning.
8.8.2 Metrics
H2 uses the 5 core metrics plus action distribution patterns and improvement deltas. We
compute ∆SMDP = Augmented−Baseline and ∆MDP = Augmented−Baseline to compare
reward shaping benefits across architectures.
8.9 H3: Visitor Simulator Signal Quality
H3 tests whether the State Machine simulator (clearer signals) produces better policies
than Sim8 (overlapping signals).
8.9.1 Design
H3 is the only hypothesis that uses the State Machine simulator; all other hypotheses use
Sim8 as the training environment. Both simulators produce the same interface (dwell ratio
as engagement signal), but differ in design philosophy: Sim8 prioritizes ecological validity
(overlapping dwell ranges, forgiving recovery), while the State Machine prioritizes signal
clarity (non-overlapping ranges, stricter thresholds, anti-spam mechanisms). Architecture,
state, policy, prompts, and rewards are identical to baseline. The only difference is the
simulator providing training feedback.
8.9.2 Metrics
H3 uses the 5 core metrics plus recovery behavior metrics. We measure recovery action
rate (proportion of recovery actions in negative states), recovery success rate (fraction
that return visitor to engaged state), option entropy, and transition entropy to assess
strategy diversity and recovery effectiveness.
8.10 H4: State Representation Efficiency
H4 tests whether compact dialogue-act classification (23-d) maintains performance com-
pared to dense DialogueBERT embeddings (143-d).
60
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
8.10.1 Design
H4 compares compact dialogue-act representation (23-d) against dense DialogueBERT
baseline (143-d). The variant maintains the same hierarchical policy, prompts, and rewards
as baseline. Both representations are evaluated under identical training conditions to
isolate state representation effects. We evaluate on both Sim8 and State Machine to assess
generalization across simulator types.
8.10.2 Metrics
H4 uses the 5 core metrics plus episodes to 80% convergence and state dimensionality
(23-d vs 143-d) to compare learning efficiency and performance trade-offs.
8.11 H5: Option Design Philosophy
H5 tests whether reward-aligned option design produces better learning than function-based
design.
8.11.1 Design
H5 compares two 4-option designs: the function-based baseline (grouping by communica-
tive function) against the reward-aligned alternative (grouping by reward component).
Both designs have four options, isolating the grouping philosophy while controlling for
option count. The reward-aligned design uses option-level masking, which structurally
prevents option persistence at exhausted exhibits; function-based design relies on learned
termination.
8.11.2 Metrics
H5 uses the 5 core metrics plus option collapse rate, option entropy, episodes to convergence,
and persistence episodes (count of episodes where agent remains in Explain option after
exhibit exhaustion) to compare design philosophies.
8.12 H1: Hierarchical Policy Structure
8.12.1 Overview
Preliminary experiments revealed a 49.6 percentage point performance gap between SMDP
(Option-Critic) and MDP architectures: SMDP achieves 49.1% ± 1.3% exhibit coverage
compared to MDP’s 98.7% ± 1.1%, despite identical hyperparameters, reward functions,
and training protocols. The SMDP exhibits severe option collapse (OCI: 88.3) with 88%
of episodes dominated by a single option, effectively reducing the hierarchical policy to a
single-option policy.
We address this gap through systematic optimization across six study types, following
a funnel-style experimental design: (1) baseline comparison quantifying the gap, (2)
sensitivity analysis identifying critical hyperparameters (learning rate, gamma, entropy,
duration), (3) interaction studies testing hyperparameter combinations (gamma×LR,
gamma×entropy), (4) Option-Critic mechanism ablations (deliberation cost, termination
regularization, combined), (5) advanced architectural modifications (separate learning
rates, value clipping, etc.), and (6) alternative termination mechanisms (beta supervision,
61
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
hard budgets). Results are reported for conditions with n ≥3 seeds, following RL evaluation
best practices [Henderson et al., 2018]. Coverage is measured as the percentage of facts
mentioned from the total 21 facts across all exhibits.
Table 13 summarizes the experimental studies conducted.
Table 13: Experimental Study Organization
Study Description
Baseline MDP vs SMDP comparison under identical
conditions
Sensitivity One-at-a-time hyperparameter analysis (LR,
γ, entropy)
Interactions Factorial designs testing hyperparameter com-
binations
OC Ablations Option-Critic mechanisms (deliberation cost,
termination regularization)
Advanced Architectural modifications (separate LRs,
value clipping, etc.)
Termination Alternative termination mechanisms (beta
supervision, hard budgets)
Note: All results use n≥3 seeds for statistical validity. Studies progress from baseline
comparison through systematic optimization.
8.13 Baseline Comparison: MDP vs SMDP
We establish the baseline performance gap by comparing MDP and SMDP architectures
under identical conditions: learning rate 1 × 10−4, discount factor γ=0.99, baseline
rewards (engagement + novelty only), 1000 training episodes, and 3 random seeds each.
Both architectures use DialogueBERT state representation (143-d) and Sim8 engagement
simulator.
Figure 8 shows the learning curves for both architectures. MDP achieves higher returns
throughout training, with a final average return of 52.2 over the last 100 episodes compared
to SMDP’s 18.3. The learning curves demonstrate that MDP learns more effectively,
achieving both higher coverage and higher returns.

Figure 9: Action and option distribution over training episodes comparing MDP and
SMDP baseline architectures. Top panel: MDP action distribution showing all individual
actions with distinct colors, demonstrating diverse action usage throughout training.
Bottom panel: SMDP option distribution showing severe option collapse. The gray shaded
region highlights final episodes (800–1000) to emphasize end-of-training behavior.Option
Collapse Index (OCI)measures the percentage of final 100 episodes where a single option
accounts for > 80% of all option selections. For fair comparison, MDP actions are grouped
into the same 4-option structure (Explain, AskQuestion, OfferTransition, Conclude) before
calculating OCI. The red dashed line at 80% marks the collapse threshold. Higher OCI
indicates more severe collapse: SMDP achieves OCI = 100.0%, meaning all final episodes
exhibit single-option dominance, while MDP maintains diverse option distribution (OCI
= 0.3%). This visualization directly demonstrates how SMDP’s hierarchical structure
collapses to a single-option policy, effectively reducing the hierarchical policy to a flat
single-action policy.
Figure 10 provides a detailed view of the learning dynamics, showing how the
performance gap emerges and stabilizes over the full 1000-episode training period. The
learning curves demonstrate that MDP achieves both faster convergence and higher final
performance compared to SMDP.

8.14 Sensitivity Analysis
Following baseline comparison, we conduct one-at-a-time sensitivity analyses to identify
which hyperparameters critically affect SMDP performance. We test four hyperparameters:
learning rate (controlling gradient step size), discount factor γ (planning horizon),
entropy coefficient (exploration-exploitation trade-off), and option duration limit τ (forced
termination threshold).
Table 16: Learning Rate Sensitivity Analysis (n≥3)
LR Return Coverage (%) OCI n
4e-5 22.7±0.8 45.3±2.1 37.8±5.2 3
5e-5 19.8±2.3 50.6±2.0 68.4±2.6 3
6e-5 24.6±10.9 39.0±21.7 78.4±2.6 3
7e-5 18.7±1.9 52.0±0.4 80.6±3.5 3
Note: All conditions have n=3. Results ordered by learning rate value. LR=6e-5 shows high
variance (±21.7% coverage), indicating unstable learning at this rate.
The learning rate sweep reveals coverage ranging from 39.0% to 52.0% across tested
rates, with overlapping confidence intervals preventing clear differentiation. LR=7e-5
achieves the highest mean coverage (52.0% ± 0.4%) with low variance, while LR=6e-5
shows exceptionally high variance (39.0% ± 21.7%), indicating unstable learning at this
rate. Lower learning rates (LR=4e-5) show reduced OCI (37.8) but also reduced coverage
(45.3%), suggesting a tradeoff between option diversity and exploration efficiency.
8.14.1 Gamma (Discount Factor) Sensitivity
Discount factor γ controls the agent’s planning horizon and value propagation. We test
four levels: γ∈ { 0.80, 0.85, 0.90, 0.95}, with additional replications at γ=0.80 due to
promising initial results.
Table 17: Gamma Sensitivity Analysis (n≥3)
γReturn Coverage (%) OCI n
0.80 19.7±4.0 50.6±4.5 68.5±11.4 3
0.85 21.2±1.8 48.5±4.9 67.9±5.5 3
Note: All conditions have n=3. Gamma 0.90 and 0.95 tested but insufficient seeds (n=1)
excluded from analysis.
Learning rate: Coverage ranges 39.0–52.0%, with LR=7e-5 achieving highest (52.0%
± 0.4%) and LR=6e-5 showing high variance (39.0% ± 21.7%). Gamma: Lower values
(0.80) achieve 50.6% ± 4.5% coverage, higher than 0.85 (48.5% ± 4.9%). Entropy: Higher
entropy (0.020) achieves best coverage (51.4%±1.3%), with range 47.6–51.4%.

8.18 Alternative Termination Mechanisms
Given that standard optimization achieves maximum 53.7% coverage (DC015), we test
alternative approaches that bypass learned termination or provide direct supervision: beta
supervision (heuristic termination targets based on exhibit completion) and hard duration
budgets (forced termination after fixed turn counts).
Table 25: Termination Mechanism Design
Approach Description
Beta Supervision Heuristic termination targets:
βtarget(s) = 1.0 if exhibit exhausted, 0 .2
otherwise. Tested with weights 0.3, 0.5,
0.8.
Hard Budget Force termination after fixed turn count
(τ∈ {3,5,8}).
Baseline Standard learned termination (no mod-
ifications).

Top configurations: High deliberation cost (53.7% ± 1.6%), γ=0.80 with LR=5e-5
(52.7% ± 3.8%), Medium deliberation cost + medium termination regularization (52.1%
±3.6%). SMDP coverage ranges 27–54% across all conditions (n≥3), with 45–49pp gap
to MDP (98.7%). Overlapping confidence intervals prevent clear statistical differentiation
between SMDP configurations, but the consistent large gap to MDP suggests architectural
rather than parametric limitations.
Verdict H1 is NOT supported: Systematic SMDP optimization does not close the
MDP gap. Best SMDP configuration (deliberation cost 0.015) achieves 53.7% coverage,
still 45.0pp below MDP baseline (98.7%). Hyperparameter tuning operates within a
constrained envelope; the gap is architectural, not parametric.

8.20 H2: Augmented Reward Function Effectiveness
H2 tests whether adding auxiliary reward components improves policy learning beyond
baseline rewards. We evaluate structural reward modifications designed to address the
option collapse problem identified in H1. All experiments use the baseline reward function
(Section 4.4.1) with auxiliary components enabled for augmented configurations. See
Table 44 for parameter values.
8.20.1 Experimental Setup
All experiments use SMDP (Option-Critic) architecture with H1 optimal hyperparameters
(γ = 0.80, LR=5 × 10−5) and train for 1000 episodes with n=3 seeds. All metrics reported
use the last 100 episodes for evaluation.
Table 28 describes the reward modifications tested.
Table 28: H2 Reward Modification Conditions
Condition Description
Baseline Control No modifications (H1 optimal hyperparame-
ters)
AggressiveExhaustion− 2.0 penalty for Explain at exhausted ex-
hibits
ZeroEngExhausted No engagement reward at exhausted exhibits
Augmented Baseline Full augmented rewards (H1 baseline hyper-
parameters)
Tuned Augmented Full augmented rewards (H1 optimal hyper-
parameters)
8.20.2 Results
Table 29 presents performance metrics for all conditions.
Table 29: H2 Performance Comparison (n=3)
Condition Return Coverage (%) OCI n
AggressiveExhaustion−4.2±12.6 51.7±3.6 39.7±21.3 3
ZeroEngExhausted 4.8±0.7 50.2±1.3 66.6±2.6 3
Baseline Control 20.3±0.5 49.1±0.7 63.3±6.2 3
Tuned Augmented 20.0±0.6 48.6±0.6 67.0±4.7 3
Augmented Baseline 18.2±0.6 49.2±1.4 88.7±1.0 3
H1 Baselines (for reference)
H1 SMDP Baseline 18.3±0.9 49.1±1.3 88.3±1.5 3
H1 MDP Baseline 52.2±2.7 98.7±1.1 0.3±0.6 3
OCI = Option Collapse Index (% episodes with>80% single-option dominance). Lower is
better.
AggressiveExhaustion achieves the best coverage (51.7% ± 3.6%), representing +2.6
pp improvement over Baseline Control (49.1%). It also achieves the lowest OCI (39.7 ±
74
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
21.3), indicating substantially reduced option collapse. However, AggressiveExhaustion
shows negative returns (−4.2±12.6) with high variance, suggesting instability.
ZeroEngExhausted shows modest coverage improvement (50.2% ± 1.3%, +1.1 pp)
with stable returns (4.8±0.7) but minimal OCI improvement (66.6 vs 63.3).
Augmented rewards (Augmented Baseline, Tuned Augmented) do not improve coverage
or OCI compared to Baseline Control. Augmented Baseline shows severe option collapse
(OCI: 88.7), matching H1 SMDP baseline.
Figure 18 compares coverage and OCI across all conditions.
Figure 18: H2 performance comparison: Coverage (left) and Option Collapse Index (right)
across all conditions. AggressiveExhaustion achieves highest coverage (51.7%) and lowest
OCI (39.7). Dashed lines show H1 baselines for reference.
Figure 19 shows option distribution for three key conditions.
Figure 19: Option distribution comparison: Baseline Control (left), AggressiveExhaustion
(center), Tuned Augmented (right). AggressiveExhaustion achieves more balanced
distribution with higher OfferTransition usage (14.1% vs 3.6%) and lower Explain
dominance (77.2% vs 92.4%).
AggressiveExhaustion achieves more balanced option distribution: Explain 77.2%,
OfferTransition 14.1%, AskQuestion 7.7%. This compares favorably to Baseline Control
75
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
(Explain 92.4%, OfferTransition 3.6%, AskQuestion 3.6%) and Tuned Augmented (Explain
93.5%, OfferTransition 2.4%, AskQuestion 3.6%).
8.20.3 Summary
AggressiveExhaustion achieves best results: +2.6 pp coverage, lowest OCI (39.7), and more
balanced option distribution. However, returns are negative and highly variable. Aug-
mented rewards do not improve performance: Augmented Baseline and Tuned Augmented
show no coverage or OCI improvement over baseline. All conditions remain far below
MDP: Best SMDP coverage (51.7%) is 47.0 pp below MDP baseline (98.7%), indicating
structural reward modifications cannot close the architecture gap.
H2 predictions are not supported: augmented rewards do not improve SMDP per-
formance. AggressiveExhaustion provides partial improvement through penalty-based
reward shaping, but at the cost of return stability.
Verdict H2 is NOT supported: Augmented rewards do not improve SMDP perfor-
mance. AggressiveExhaustion achieves modest coverage gain (+2.6pp) but with unstable
returns. Standard augmented rewards show no improvement over baseline. All conditions
remain 47.0pp below MDP.
8.21 H3: Visitor Simulator Signal Quality
H3 tests whether a theory-driven simulator with clearer reward signals (State Machine)
produces policies with greater strategy diversity and more effective recovery actions
compared to an empirically-informed simulator with overlapping signals (Sim8).
8.21.1 Experimental Setup
Table 30 compares the two simulator designs.
Table 30: Simulator Design Comparison
Property Sim8 State Machine
Dwell Ranges Overlapping (0.3–0.9) Non-overlapping (0.2 gaps)
State Model Probabilistic transitions Explicit states
Recovery Mechanics Implicit Explicit state transitions
Signal Clarity Realistic but ambiguous Clear but idealized
Two conditions were tested: MDP StateMachine (Flat RL with State Machine
simulator, n=3) and SMDP StateMachine (Option-Critic with State Machine simulator,
n=3).
Baselines from H1: MDP Sim8 (98.7%±1.1% coverage) and SMDP Sim8 (49.1%±
1.3% coverage, OCI: 88.3±1.5).

hyperparameters for MDP.
Key observations from the weight balance analysis reveal several important patterns.
First, SMDP performance degrades with all weight modifications compared to baseline.
The balanced baseline (16.7 return, 47.9% coverage) outperforms all other configurations.

Weight modifications increase option collapse (OCI 70–72 vs baseline 51.1) and reduce
option diversity (entropy 0.35–0.91 vs baseline 0.90). Second, MDP maintains higher
option diversity (entropy 1.11–1.50) across all weight configurations compared to SMDP
(0.35–0.91). The maximum entropy for 4 options is 2.0; MDP achieves 55–75% of maximum
while SMDP achieves 18–46%. Finally, weight balance does not close the SMDP-MDP
performance gap. Even the worst-performing MDP configuration (Novelty-Favored: 36.3
return) outperforms the best SMDP configuration (Balanced: 16.7 return).
8.21.4 Summary
For SMDP, State Machine enables better option diversity (OCI 51.1 vs 88.3, entropy 0.90)
while maintaining similar coverage (47.9% vs 49.1%). High variance across seeds indicates
inconsistent performance. Weight modifications degrade performance; balanced baseline
achieves best SMDP results. For MDP, State Machine enables recovery action usage
(35.8%) and higher option diversity (entropy 1.45), though with lower coverage (86.0% vs
97.9%). Weight modifications can improve MDP performance: Engagement-Emphasized
achieves 69.2 return and 97.6% coverage. Recovery behavior metrics are State Machine-
specific. For MDP baseline, 35.8% recovery action usage; for SMDP baseline, 17.6%.
Recovery actions include ClarifyFact, AskQuestion variants, and SuggestMove—actions
designed to restore engagement from negative states. Sim8 shows 0% recovery as it lacks
explicit negative state modeling. Regarding weight balance, reward weight modifications
do not help SMDP but can significantly improve MDP performance. The SMDP-MDP
performance gap persists regardless of weight configuration. Notably, MDP maintains
high diversity (entropy >1.1) across all conditions while SMDP collapses (entropy <1.0).
H3 predictions are supported: State Machine achieves greater strategy diversity
(Prediction 1) and demonstrates recovery behavior capability (Prediction 2) for both
MDP and SMDP architectures. Recovery metrics show that State Machine learns to
use recovery-capable actions (35.8% for MDP, 17.6% for SMDP), demonstrating that
explicit negative state modeling enables learning of context-appropriate recovery strategies.
The weight balance analysis reveals that reward modifications cannot address SMDP’s
fundamental option collapse problem (entropy remains below 1.0), while MDP benefits
substantially from engagement-emphasized weights (69.2 return vs 40.3 baseline) while
maintaining high diversity (entropy>1.2).
Verdict H3 is PARTIALLY supported: State Machine enables better option
diversity (OCI 51.1 vs 88.3) and recovery behavior (17.6% vs 0% for SMDP), but does
not close the MDP gap. Clear signals improve diversity and enable recovery learning, but
do not address fundamental SMDP architectural limitations.
8.22 H4: State Representation Efficiency
H4 tests whether compact dialogue-act classification (23-d) maintains performance com-
pared to dense DialogueBERT embeddings (143-d) while providing computational efficiency
benefits for real-world deployment.
8.22.1 Experimental Setup
All experiments use SMDP (Option-Critic) architecture with H1 optimal hyperparameters
(γ = 0.80, LR=5 × 10−5) and train for 1000 episodes with n=3 seeds. All metrics reported

use the last 100 episodes for evaluation.

Table 36: H4 State Representation Comparison

SMDP DialogueAct Compact 23-d dialogue-act probability vec-
tors
Dialogue-act classification maps visitor utterances to probability distributions over
communicative functions (Inform, Question, Directive, Commissive) following established
dialogue act taxonomies [Bunt et al., 2011, Stolcke et al., 2000]. This provides an 84%
reduction in state dimensionality (143-d → 23-d) compared to DialogueBERT embeddings
while maintaining semantic information relevant to dialogue policy learning.

(24.9 ± 2.6) compared to DialogueBERT (18.3 ± 0.9), representing a +6.6 improvement.

higher entropy (0.75 ± 0.34) compared to DialogueBERT (0.47 ± 0.05), indicating more
balanced option distribution. However, even with improved option diversity, coverage
remains low, suggesting that option diversity alone does not translate to performance
improvements.

performance metrics.
8.22.3 Convergence Analysis
H4 Prediction 2 states that compact representation will achieve faster training convergence
compared to dense representation. To evaluate this, we examine the learning dynamics
shown in Figure 27. DialogueAct achieves higher returns throughout training, reaching a
final average of 24.9 over the last 100 episodes compared to DialogueBERT’s 18.3. The
learning curves show that DialogueAct maintains consistently higher performance from
early training, suggesting that the compact 23-d representation enables more efficient
learning compared to the dense 143-d embeddings.
While DialogueAct achieves higher final performance, both representations show similar
learning dynamics with stable performance reached by mid-training (around episodes
400-600). The key advantage of DialogueAct is not necessarily faster convergence to a
threshold, but rather learning to a higher performance level with the same training budget.
This supports Prediction 2 in a nuanced way: the compact representation enables more
effective learning (higher final performance) rather than simply faster convergence to the
same level. The reduced state dimensionality allows the policy network to learn more
efficiently, achieving better performance with the same computational resources.

8.22.4 Summary
DialogueAct achieves comparable coverage (49.4% vs 49.1%) and higher return (24.9 vs
18.3) compared to DialogueBERT while providing substantial efficiency benefits (84%
reduction in state dimensionality). DialogueAct shows better option diversity: OCI 41.3
vs 88.3 (47.0 point reduction) and entropy 0.75 vs 0.47, indicating that the compact
representation enables more balanced option usage. However, coverage remains low
(49.4%) compared to MDP’s 98.7%, suggesting that improved option diversity does not
address SMDP’s fundamental performance limitations. The 23-d dialogue-act classification
maintains semantic information relevant to dialogue policy learning while reducing
computational requirements, but these efficiency gains do not translate to performance
improvements relative to flat policies.
H4 predictions are partially supported: DialogueAct achieves comparable episodic
return and exhibit coverage (Prediction 1) while providing substantial efficiency benefits.
The compact representation enables more effective learning within SMDP (Prediction 2),
achieving higher final performance (24.9 vs 18.3 return) with similar learning dynamics.
However, both DialogueAct and DialogueBERT remain far below MDP performance
(98.7% coverage), indicating that state representation improvements do not address
SMDP’s fundamental structural limitations. The compact representation improves option
diversity (OCI 41.3 vs 88.3), but this does not translate to coverage improvements. The
84% reduction in state dimensionality (143-d → 23-d) provides computational benefits
for deployment, but these efficiency gains come with the same performance limitations
inherent to SMDP architectures.
Verdict H4 is SUPPORTED: DialogueAct (23-d) maintains comparable coverage
(49.4% vs 49.1%) while achieving better option diversity (OCI 41.3 vs 88.3) and higher
return (+6.6). The 84% dimensionality reduction provides efficiency without performance
loss; the compact representation improves learning within SMDP constraints.
8.23 H5: Option Design Philosophy
H5 tests whether reward-aligned option design (grouping actions by reward component)
produces more balanced option usage and faster learning compared to function-based
design (grouping actions by communicative function).
8.23.1 Experimental Setup
All experiments use SMDP (Option-Critic) architecture with H1 optimal hyperparameters
(γ = 0.80, LR=5 × 10−5) and train for 1000 episodes with n=3 seeds. All metrics reported
use the last 100 episodes for evaluation.
Table 38 describes the option design conditions tested.
Function-based design groups actions by communicative function following dialogue act
taxonomies [Bunt et al., 2011, Stolcke et al., 2000, Chi and Wylie, 2014]. This approach
provides multiple subactions per option, allowing flexible within-option strategies. However,
the same option can produce different reward types, creating diffuse credit assignment
challenges. WhenExplainNewFactis masked at exhausted exhibits, the agent can still
select Explain using fallback subactions (RepeatFact,ClarifyFact), potentially leading to
suboptimal behavior.
87
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
Table 38: H5 Option Design Comparison
Condition Description
Function-Based Groups actions by communicative function:
Explain (Inform: ExplainNewFact, Repeat-
Fact, ClarifyFact), AskQuestion (Question:
AskOpinion, AskMemory, AskClarification),
OfferTransition (Directive: SuggestMove),
Conclude (Closing: WrapUp)
Reward-Aligned Groups actions by reward component: Ex-
plain (novelty: ExplainNewFact only, masked
when exhibit exhausted), Engage (engage-
ment: RepeatFact, ClarifyFact, all AskQues-
tion variants), Transition (coverage: Suggest-
Move only), Conclude (termination: WrapUp
only)
Reward-aligned design groups actions by reward component alignment [Kulkarni
et al., 2016, Machado et al., 2017], with option-level masking as a structural constraint
[Huang et al., 2020]. This approach provides one-to-one reward-option correspondence,
enabling clean credit assignment. Option masking ensures options are only available when
their associated rewards are achievable. The trade-off is reduced within-option flexibility
compared to function-based design.
Both designs use four options, isolating the grouping philosophy as the experimental
variable. The key difference is whether options are defined by communicative function
(function-based) or reward alignment (reward-aligned).
8.23.2 Results
Table 39 presents performance metrics comparing Function-Based and Reward-Aligned
designs.

Function-Based 19.6±1.2 49.5±2.8 74.7±12.4 0.37±0.15 3

Reward-Aligned achieves higher episodic return (28.4 ± 0.7) compared to Function-
88
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
Based (19.6 ± 1.2), representing a +8.8 improvement. Reward-Aligned achieves higher
coverage (55.5% ± 3.3%) compared to Function-Based (49.5% ± 2.8%), representing a
+6.0 pp improvement. However, even this best-case SMDP performance (55.5% coverage)
remains far below MDP’s 98.7% coverage, indicating that reward-aligned design does
not overcome SMDP’s fundamental structural limitations. Reward-Aligned shows better
option diversity: OCI 2.1 ± 2.1 compared to Function-Based’s 74.7 ± 12.4, representing
a 72.6 point reduction in option collapse. Reward-Aligned also achieves higher entropy
(1.24 ± 0.19) compared to Function-Based (0.37 ± 0.15), indicating more balanced option
distribution. While this represents improvement within SMDP, the persistent large gap
to MDP suggests that hierarchy itself may be fundamentally limited in this domain.
Figure 29 compares coverage, OCI, and entropy across option designs.
Figure 29: H5 performance comparison: Coverage (left), Option Collapse Index (middle),
and Entropy (right) for Function-Based vs Reward-Aligned. Reward-Aligned achieves
higher coverage (55.5% vs 49.5%), lower OCI (2.1 vs 74.7), and higher entropy (1.24 vs

Figure 30 compares learning curves for Function-Based and Reward-Aligned designs.
Figure 31 shows option distribution comparison between Function-Based and Reward-
Aligned designs.
The option distribution analysis reveals fundamental differences in how each design
learns to use options. Function-Based shows severe Explain collapse (87.5%), with
minimal usage of AskQuestion (5.6%), OfferTransition (6.0%), and Conclude (0.9%). This
pattern reflects the diffuse credit assignment problem: when Explain contains multiple
subactions with different reward types, the policy learns to exploit the highest-reward
subaction (ExplainNewFact) while underutilizing other options. Reward-Aligned achieves
balanced distribution: Engage 53.3%, Explain 36.9%, Transition 7.8%, Conclude 2.1%.
The one-to-one reward-option correspondence enables clean credit assignment, allowing
the policy to learn when each option is most appropriate based on the associated reward
signal.
8.23.3 Convergence Analysis
H5 Prediction 2 states that reward-aligned design will learn faster compared to function-
based design. To evaluate this, we examine the learning dynamics shown in Figure 30.
Reward-Aligned achieves higher returns throughout training, reaching a final average of
28.4 over the last 100 episodes compared to Function-Based’s 19.6. The learning curves

better performance with the same computational resources.
8.23.4 Summary
Reward-Aligned achieves higher coverage (55.5% vs 49.5%) and higher return (28.4 vs 19.6)
compared to Function-Based while providing better option diversity: OCI 2.1 vs 74.7 (72.6
point reduction) and entropy 1.24 vs 0.37, indicating that the reward-aligned design enables
more balanced option usage within SMDP. The one-to-one reward-option correspondence
provides cleaner credit assignment compared to function-based design. However, even this
best-case SMDP performance (55.5% coverage) remains far below MDP’s 98.7% coverage,
suggesting that reward-aligned design does not overcome SMDP’s fundamental structural
limitations. The improvement is relative to other SMDP configurations, not relative to
flat policies.
H5 predictions are partially supported: Reward-Aligned achieves more balanced option
usage (Prediction 1) with lower OCI (2.1 vs 74.7) and higher entropy (1.24 vs 0.37).
The reward-aligned design enables more effective learning within SMDP (Prediction 2),
achieving higher final performance (28.4 vs 19.6 return, 55.5% vs 49.5% coverage) with
91
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
similar learning dynamics. However, Prediction 3 is not supported: Reward-Aligned does
not achieve comparable performance to MDP (55.5% vs 98.7% coverage), indicating that
even the best SMDP configuration remains substantially below flat policy performance.
The reward-aligned design improves SMDP performance relative to function-based design,
but this improvement is modest compared to the fundamental gap between SMDP and
MDP. The results suggest that hierarchy itself may be fundamentally limited in this
domain, regardless of option design choices.

9 Discussion
Flat Actor-Critic policies achieve 86–98% coverage (average 94% across conditions,
including weight balance experiments from H3) and learn engagement-adaptive behaviors
(35.8% recovery actions, 31.7% AskQuestion usage), demonstrating that flat reinforcement
learning effectively leverages the training signals available in our simulators. When
provided with clear engagement feedback (State Machine: 86–98% coverage across
weight conditions) or even ambiguous signals (Sim8: 98.7% coverage), flat policies adapt
effectively, learning to maximize available rewards and respond to visitor state changes.
Systematic comparison across 53 conditions reveals that hierarchical Option-Critic policies
face significant architectural limitations in this museum dialogue domain, consistently
underperforming despite optimization with coverage ranging from 27–55% (average 49%)
and a persistent 31–45 percentage point gap to flat policies even in best-case configurations.
This work investigates an important question in hierarchical reinforcement learning:
when does hierarchy help vs hurt? While prior work assumes hierarchy is beneficial
for long-horizon tasks [Sutton et al., 1999, Dietterich, 2000], our results suggest that
92
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
domain characteristics matter more than horizon length in this museum dialogue setting
with exhaustible content. We tested hyperparameter tuning, reward engineering, and
option design across multiple conditions, identifying dynamic action availability as a key
factor that appears to determine when hierarchy helps or hurts. Our findings extend
understanding of Option-Critic by showing where it struggles (domains with exhaustible
content) not just where it succeeds (spatial navigation). We position our findings within
the broader hierarchical reinforcement learning and dialogue systems literature, connecting
our empirical results to theoretical foundations and prior work.
This discussion synthesizes findings to understandwhy—not justwhat—flat policies
succeed while hierarchical policies face limitations. We identify five core mechanisms
(Section 9.1), contrast competing interpretations (Section 9.7), address LLM-in-the-loop
challenges unique to this setting (Section 9.8), and derive implications for museum
technology deployment (Section 10.10.1).
9.1 Mechanistic Summary
Our experiments reveal several interconnected mechanisms that appear to explain Option-
Critic’s underperformance in this museum dialogue domain. First, accumulated Q-values
from early novelty rewards create persistent positive option-level advantages ( AO > 0)
even after content exhaustion, which suppresses the termination gradient. To track this
option persistence systematically, we computed the Option Collapse Index (OCI), a simple
diagnostic metric that quantifies the percentage of option selections dominated by a single
option. This metric proved useful for identifying when and how severely the policy failed
to diversify across options. Across all baseline seeds, we observed high collapse (OCI
87.4–90.0, Table 15), indicating near-exclusive use of the Explain option.
Second, action masking provides implicit temporal structure in this domain. When
ExplainNewFactbecomes masked at exhausted exhibits, flat policies can immediately
switch toOfferTransitionbased on current state and available actions. Hierarchical
policies, however, must learn when to terminate options—a learning burden that appears
unnecessary when masking already enforces valid transitions (Table 14: MDP 98.7%
vs SMDP 49.1% coverage). Third, options mixing multiple reward types (e.g., Explain
contains both novelty and engagement rewards) create ambiguous advantage signals that
slow termination learning, as evidenced by the difference between function-based (OCI
74.7) and reward-aligned designs (OCI 2.1) in Table 39.
Additionally, simulator signal quality appears to bound the learning ceiling: Sim8’s
overlapping dwell ranges limit engagement-adaptive learning regardless of architecture,
while State Machine’s clearer signals enable 35.8% recovery action usage in flat policies
(Figure 21). Finally, compact state representations may aid hierarchical credit assignment—
DialogueAct’s 84% dimensionality reduction corresponds to improved option diversity
(OCI 41.3 vs 88.3), suggesting high-dimensional states may exacerbate value function
learning challenges (Table 37).
The consistent pattern across these mechanisms: hierarchy adds learning burden
(termination, credit assignment) without providing corresponding benefits when action
masking already imposes temporal structure in this domain.
93
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
9.2 H1: The Architectural Gap and Limits of Optimization
H1 establishes the baseline performance gap: flat Actor-Critic policies (solving MDP
formulations) achieve 98.7% coverage compared to Option-Critic policies’ (solving SMDP
formulations) 49.1%, representing a 49.6 percentage point deficit that persists despite
systematic optimization across 53 experimental conditions. To address the persistent
option selection failure mode where agents remain in options even when switching would
be beneficial, we tested deliberation cost [Harb et al., 2018], which penalizes option
persistence per step to create negative pressure for option continuation. While high
deliberation cost achieves the best Option-Critic coverage (53.7%) and lowest option
collapse (OCI) among all configurations, this represents only a 4.6 percentage point
improvement over baseline, still leaving a 45 percentage point gap to flat Actor-Critic
policies. This suggests that while deliberation cost may address the symptom of option
persistence, it does not address the underlying architectural limitations. The gap is not
merely a matter of hyperparameter tuning—systematic optimization across sensitivity
analyses, interaction studies, Option-Critic ablations, and architectural modifications
reveals that the performance gap is primarily architectural rather than parametric.
While Bacon et al. [2017] identify persistent option selection as a failure mode, our
work demonstrates this is systematic and architectural rather than parametric, persisting
across 53 hyperparameter configurations. The hierarchical architecture struggles with
termination learning and credit assignment challenges that prevent it from effectively
utilizing the same simulator signals that enable flat policies to succeed.
9.2.1 Failure Mechanisms in Hierarchical Policies
The Option-Critic architecture faces three interconnected failure mechanisms that prevent
effective termination learning: termination gradient suppression, state representation chal-
lenges, and diffuse credit assignment. The Option-Critic architecture learns termination
functions through a gradient that depends on the option-level advantage signal [Bacon
et al., 2017]:
∇βω J≈ −E[∇ βω βω(s)·A O(s, ω)]
where AO(s, ω) = QΩ(s, ω) −V (s) measures whether continuing the current option
outperforms switching to alternatives. When AO > 0, the gradient drives βω(s) → 0,
encouraging continuation; when AO < 0, it drives βω(s) → 1, encouraging termination.
This mechanism enables adaptive pacing, but it breaks down when reward structure
creates persistent positive advantages for one option, a failure mode that Bacon et al.
[2017] identify aspersistent option selection.
In museum dialogue, the Explain option generates consistent novelty rewards (+1 .0
per new fact) that accumulate over multiple turns. With discount factor γ = 0.99, the
option valueQ Ω(s,Explain) accumulates high returns from past novelty rewards:
QΩ(s,Explain) =
TX
t=0
γtrt ≈1.5 + 0.99×1.5 + 0.99 2 ×1.5 +. . .
This creates a positive option-level advantage AO(s,Explain ) > 0 that persists even
afterExplainNewFactbecomes masked at exhausted exhibits. When the agent can only
use fallback subactions (ClarifyFact,RepeatFact) that yield only engagement reward
(≈ +0.5 per turn), the per-step reward drops substantially, but the accumulated QΩ
94
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
value from past novelty rewards keeps AO positive. The termination gradient therefore
continues to push βω(s) → 0, preventing the agent from switching toOfferTransition
despite reward degradation. This appears consistent with thepersistent option selection
failure mode identified by Bacon et al. [2017], where termination learning assumes options
have comparable utility; when reward structure favors one option disproportionately, the
mechanism breaks down.
The termination failure is exacerbated by how the critic learns to interpret state
information, even though the state representation itself is technically Markovian. The
state vector st = [ft, ht, it, ct] includes exhibit focusf t and dialogue historyh t, whereh exh
t
explicitly encodes per-exhibit coverage ratios (facts mentioned per exhibit divided by total
facts). This coverage information is sufficient to determine which exhibits are exhausted
and therefore which actions are available, satisfying the Markov property [Sutton and
Barto, 2018].
However, the critic struggles to learn effective value distinctions between states
