that differ primarily in action availability. The value function QΩ(s,Explain ) must
learn to distinguish between “Explain option at exhibit with fresh facts” (high value:
ExplainNewFactavailable, novelty rewards achievable) and “Explain option at exhausted
exhibit” (low value: onlyClarifyFact/RepeatFactavailable, no novelty rewards). While the
state contains coverage information inh exh
t that could theoretically enable this distinction,
the coverage ratios are embedded within a larger state vector that also includes option usage
counts, intent embeddings, and dialogue context, making it difficult for the value function
to extract and utilize the action availability signal. The value function therefore averages
across both high-value and low-value states, creating a positive bias that suppresses
termination even when the agent is at an exhausted exhibit.
Sutton and Barto [2018] emphasize that state representation design is fundamental
to reinforcement learning: while the Markov property requires that the state contains
sufficient information to predict future rewards, thelearnabilityof value functions depends
on how easily the critic can extract and utilize that information. When state information
is present but not easily accessible (e.g., embedded in a larger vector without explicit
labeling), value functions struggle to learn effective distinctions, leading to suboptimal
policies. In our hierarchical setting, this prevents the termination function from learning
effective switching behavior, contributing to option collapse.
Hierarchical policies face a fundamental credit assignment challenge: rewards received
during option execution must be attributed to both the option-level decision (which option
to pursue) and the intra-option decisions (which subaction to take within that option).
The Option-Critic architecture uses separate value functions and advantages for each level
[Bacon et al., 2017]:
AO(s, ω) =Q Ω(s, ω)−V(s) (option-level advantage) (1)
AU(s, ω, a) =Q U(s, ω, a)−Q Ω(s, ω) (intra-option advantage) (2)
This hierarchical credit assignment follows the value function decomposition principles
established by Dietterich [2000] in the MAXQ framework, which decomposes the value
function hierarchically to enable efficient learning in complex domains. The options
framework [Sutton et al., 1999] extends this decomposition to learned options, where
option valuesQ Ω(s, ω) represent the expected return from executing optionωin states,
and intra-option values QU(s, ω, a) represent the expected return from taking subaction a
within optionω.
95
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
This hierarchical credit assignment works well when options have distinct reward profiles
and clear termination conditions. However, in museum dialogue, the function-based option
design createsdiffuse credit assignment: the Explain option contains multiple subactions
(ExplainNewFact,ClarifyFact,RepeatFact) that produce different reward types. When
ExplainNewFactgenerates novelty rewards whileClarifyFactgenerates only engagement
rewards, the option-level value QΩ(s,Explain ) averages across these different reward
profiles, creating ambiguity about which subactions are responsible for the option’s value.
Sutton et al. [1999] note that effective option design requires options to have clear,
distinct purposes; when options mix multiple reward types, credit assignment becomes
ambiguous. The temporal difference learning mechanism [Sutton and Barto, 2018] used
to update value functions relies on bootstrapping from future value estimates, but when
option values average across heterogeneous reward profiles, these estimates become less
informative, slowing learning and preventing effective termination.
When these three failure mechanisms interact—termination gradient suppression,
state representation challenges, and diffuse credit assignment—Option-Critic performance
degrades substantially compared to flat Actor-Critic policies.
9.2.2 Why Flat Actor-Critic Policies Succeed
A key finding from H1 is that flat Actor-Critic policies achieve high coverage (98.7%) while
learning engagement-adaptive behaviors, demonstrating that flat reinforcement learning is
not merely a baseline but an effective approach for this domain. Flat Actor-Critic policies
avoid the termination gradient suppression problem because they make primitive action
decisions at each turn without temporal abstraction. WhenExplainNewFactbecomes
masked, the flat policy can immediately switch toOfferTransitionbased on current state
and available actions, without needing to learn when to terminate a high-level option.
The flat policy’s action-level Q-values update rapidly in response to masking, enabling
faster adaptation to changing action availability.
Sutton and Barto [2018] explain that flat reinforcement learning policies (solving MDP
formulations) make direct action selections based on action-value functions Q(s, a), which
estimate the expected return from taking action a in state s. These action values update
using temporal difference learning, where the TD target r + γmax a′ Q(s′, a′) bootstraps
from the best available action in the next state. When action availability changes (e.g.,
ExplainNewFactbecomes masked), the action values immediately reflect this change,
enabling the policy to adapt without needing to learn termination behavior.
Actor-Critic methods for flat policies [Mnih et al., 2016] use policy gradients to update
action selection probabilities based on advantage estimates A(s, a) = Q(s, a) −V (s).
These advantages directly measure whether each action outperforms the average, enabling
efficient credit assignment without hierarchical decomposition. Henderson et al. [2018]
emphasize that flat Actor-Critic policies can achieve high performance when the action
space is manageable and state representation is sufficient, which is the case in our dialogue
domain where the action space consists of 8-12 primitive actions.
Flat Actor-Critic policies’ success across different simulator conditions highlights
their flexibility and ability to learn effective policies despite environmental limitations.
Under Sim8’s ambiguous signals, flat Actor-Critic policies achieve 98.7% coverage by
learning to maximize the rewards they can control. Despite Sim8’s overlapping dwell
ranges that create credit assignment challenges, flat policies learn to exploit the available
reward signals effectively, focusing on actions that yield consistent rewards (novelty from
96
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
ExplainNewFact, engagement from dialogue continuation). This demonstrates flat policies’
robustness to imperfect training signals, learning effective policies even when reward
signals are ambiguous.
When explicit engagement and disengagement modeling is introduced in the State
Machine simulator, flat Actor-Critic policies’ flexibility becomes even more apparent. Flat
policies learn to introduce questions (31.7% AskQuestion usage) when engagement drops,
answer visitor questions appropriately, and make engagement-based decisions (35.8%
recovery action usage). The State Machine’s clear state transitions and non-overlapping
dwell ranges enable flat policies to learn context-appropriate responses: usingAskQuestion
when the visitor is fatigued,ClarifyFactwhen confused, andOfferTransitionwhen ready
to move. This demonstrates that flat Actor-Critic policies can learn engagement-adaptive
behaviors when provided with clear training signals.
The fundamental difference is that flat Actor-Critic policies (MDP formulation) only
need to learn action selection, while Option-Critic policies (SMDP formulation) must
learn both option selection and termination behavior. This additional learning burden
creates multiple potential failure modes that interact to degrade Option-Critic performance
substantially compared to flat Actor-Critic policies.
9.2.3 The Limits of Hyperparameter Optimization
H1’s systematic optimization across 53 conditions reveals that hyperparameter tuning
alone cannot address the fundamental architectural limitations identified above. The best
Option-Critic configuration (high deliberation cost: 53.7% coverage) represents only a
4.6 percentage point improvement over baseline (49.1%), while remaining 45 percentage
points below flat Actor-Critic policies. This suggests that the performance gap is primarily
architectural rather than parametric.
Deliberation cost penalizes option persistence per step ( rt ←r t −c delib), creating
negative pressure for option continuation. Harb et al. [2018] introduce this mechanism to
address the persistent option selection failure mode, where agents remain in options even
when switching would be beneficial. The deliberation cost creates consistent negative
pressure for option continuation, encouraging termination when AO(s, ω) ≈ 0 and making
persistent option execution marginally costly.
However, as our H1 results demonstrate, deliberation cost addresses the symptom
(option persistence) rather than the root causes (termination gradient suppression, state
representation challenges, diffuse credit assignment). High deliberation cost (0.015)
achieves the best Option-Critic coverage (53.7%), but this improvement is modest compared
to the gap to flat Actor-Critic policies, and deliberation cost introduces instability (negative
returns in some configurations). The fundamental issue is that deliberation cost does not
address the challenges in learning to use state information effectively or the diffuse credit
assignment problem that underlie termination failure.
Harb et al. [2018] note that deliberation cost is most effective when options have
comparable utility; when reward structure creates persistent positive advantages for one
option, deliberation cost is not sufficient to overcome termination gradient suppression.
Our results are consistent with this: deliberation cost helps but cannot fully address the
architectural limitations that cause option collapse.
Interaction studies (gamma×learning rate, gamma×entropy) reveal limited synergy
between hyperparameters. The best configuration ( γ = 0 .80, LR=5 × 10−5) achieves
52.7% coverage, only 3.6 percentage points above baseline. Lower gamma values (0.80
97
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
vs 0.99) reduce Q-value accumulation, which should help termination learning, but the
improvement is modest because the fundamental credit assignment and state representation
issues remain.
Henderson et al. [2018] emphasize that deep reinforcement learning is highly sensitive
to hyperparameter choices, with small changes in learning rate, discount factor, or entropy
coefficient producing large performance differences. However, they also note that hyperpa-
rameter optimization operates within a constrained performance envelope: architectural
choices fundamentally limit achievable performance, regardless of hyperparameter tuning.
Our results are consistent with this: systematic optimization across 53 conditions improves
Option-Critic performance modestly but cannot close the gap to flat Actor-Critic policies.
Andrychowicz et al. [2020] conduct a large-scale empirical study of on-policy re-
inforcement learning, finding that design choices (architecture, reward function, state
representation) have larger effects than hyperparameter tuning. They recommend focusing
on architectural improvements rather than exhaustive hyperparameter search, which aligns
with our findings: reward-aligned option design (H5) produces larger improvements than
any hyperparameter configuration.
The relatively flat optimization landscape—coverage ranges from 27% to 54% across
all Option-Critic configurations, with overlapping confidence intervals preventing clear
statistical differentiation—suggests that hyperparameter tuning operates within a con-
strained performance envelope. The consistent large gap to flat Actor-Critic policies (45–49
percentage points) indicates that parametric optimization cannot overcome architectural
limitations.
Flat Actor-Critic policies’ ability to learn these behaviors suggests that with better
simulators or real visitor data, flat policies could learn even more nuanced engagement
strategies. Real visitors provide multimodal feedback (gaze patterns, verbal expressions,
body language) that may create clearer signals than either simulator, potentially enabling
flat policies to learn more engagement-adaptive behaviors. The fact that flat policies learn
engagement-based decisions with State Machine’s explicit modeling suggests that with real
visitor data, flat Actor-Critic policies could learn to adapt to individual visitor preferences,
engagement patterns, and learning styles. This represents a promising direction for future
work: deploying flat Actor-Critic policies trained on real visitor interactions to assess
whether the learned engagement-adaptive behaviors generalize to deployment.
Flat Actor-Critic policies’ success in this domain demonstrates that flat reinforcement
learning (solving MDP formulations) can be effective for dialogue tasks, particularly
when action availability changes dynamically and rapid adaptation is critical. The results
suggest that for museum dialogue systems, flat Actor-Critic policies provide a robust
foundation that can learn effective strategies despite simulator limitations, and may learn
additional engagement-adaptive behaviors with better training data.
9.3 H2: Reward Shaping Limitations
H2 demonstrates that reward augmentation provides limited benefit for addressing option
collapse. Augmented rewards (responsiveness, transition penalties, conclude bonus,
question-asking incentive; see Section 4.4.1) do not improve Option-Critic performance
compared to baseline, suggesting that reward shaping may not overcome architectural
limitations when option design creates diffuse credit assignment.
While Ng et al. [1999] establish the theory of reward shaping, showing that reward
transformations that preserve policy optimality can accelerate learning without changing
98
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
the optimal policy, our results show architectural limitations can prevent effective reward
signal utilization. Ng et al. [1999] note that reward shaping is most effective when the
reward function is well-aligned with the learning algorithm; when architectural limitations
prevent the algorithm from effectively using reward signals, reward shaping does not help.
Sutton and Barto [2018] emphasize that reward function design is fundamental to
reinforcement learning, but they also note that reward shaping operates within the
constraints of the learning algorithm. When the algorithm cannot effectively learn from
reward signals due to architectural limitations (e.g., termination gradient suppression),
reward shaping may not overcome these limitations.
Our results suggest that reward augmentation fails not because rewards are wrong,
but because the architecture cannot leverage them effectively. The function-based option
design creates diffuse credit assignment that prevents the policy from effectively learning
from auxiliary reward components, regardless of how well-designed those components
are. This finding suggests that addressing option collapse requires architectural changes
(option design) rather than reward engineering, which aligns with H5’s demonstration
that reward-aligned design achieves larger improvements where reward augmentation fails.
9.4 H3: Simulator Design as a Fundamental Limitation
While H2 demonstrates that reward engineering cannot overcome architectural limitations,
H3 reveals that simulator design appears to constrain what agents can learn, regardless
of architectural choices. Poor simulator signals prevent learning of engagement-adaptive
behaviors, while clear signals enable recovery strategies. The comparison between Sim8
(data-driven, overlapping signals) and State Machine (theory-driven, explicit models)
demonstrates that signal quality is a critical bottleneck that determines whether agents
can learn to detect engagement drops, adapt to dwell time, and use context-appropriate
recovery actions.
Extending Shi and Yu [2019]’s finding that user simulators for RL dialogue must
provide learnable signals with sufficient dynamic range, we demonstrate signal quality
fundamentally determines what behaviors agents can learn, not just how fast they learn.
Our results show that clear signals enable learning of behaviors that are fundamentally
impossible with ambiguous signals.
9.4.1 Sim8’s Fundamental Weaknesses: Ambiguous Signals Prevent Learning
Sim8’s design creates fundamental limitations that prevent agents from learning
engagement-adaptive behaviors. First, overlapping dwell ranges create ambiguous reward
signals where the same dwell value (e.g., 0.45) could indicate confusion (0.25–0.50
range) or moderate curiosity (0.40–0.70 range). This ambiguity makes credit assignment
impossible: when the agent receives a dwell signal of 0.45, it cannot determine whether
its previous action was effective or ineffective, preventing it from learning which actions
boost engagement.
Second, Sim8 lacks explicit state modeling: there are no clear negative states (OVER-
LOADED, CONFUSED, FATIGUED) that signal when engagement has dropped. Without
explicit state transitions, agents cannot learn to detect when engagement is declining,
making it impossible to learn recovery actions. When a visitor becomes overloaded, Sim8
may produce a lower dwell value, but this signal is ambiguous—it could reflect confusion,
fatigue, or simply natural variation. The agent cannot learn thatAskQuestionactions are
99
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
appropriate for OVERLOADED states because there is no explicit OVERLOADED state
to respond to.
Third, Sim8 has no recovery mechanisms: there are no state-specific recovery actions
with defined success rates. Agents cannot learn context-appropriate responses to engage-
ment drops because there are no explicit recovery pathways. This fundamental limitation
means that even if an agent could somehow detect engagement drops (which it cannot
due to ambiguous signals), it would not know which actions to use for recovery.
Fourth, probabilistic transitions make credit assignment difficult: state changes occur
probabilistically based on heuristics, making it unclear when and why states transition.
This creates a credit assignment problem where agents cannot attribute reward changes
to specific actions, preventing learning of engagement-adaptive strategies.
These limitations combine to create a fundamental barrier: agents cannot learn which
actions boost engagement because signals are ambiguous, and they cannot learn to ask
questions when engagement drops because there is no clear signal that engagement has
dropped. Sim8’s 4.4% AskQuestion usage (compared to State Machine’s 31.7%) reflects
this limitation: without explicit negative state modeling and clear recovery mechanisms,
agents cannot learn engagement-responsive behaviors.
9.4.2 State Machine’s Explicit Models and Punishments: Enabling Learning
State Machine addresses these limitations through explicit models and clear punishments
that enable learnable signals. First, non-overlapping dwell ranges provide clear reward
signals: there is a 0.20 gap between positive states (minimum 0.75 for ENGAGED) and
negative states (maximum 0.55 for OVERLOADED/CONFUSED). When engagement
drops, the dwell signal immediately reflects this change, creating unambiguous feedback
that enables credit assignment.
Second, explicit 9-state finite state machine provides deterministic state transitions
based on agent behavior. When the agent performs 3+ consecutiveExplainNewFact
actions, the visitor transitions deterministically to OVERLOADED state. When the
agent goes 3+ turns without asking questions, the visitor transitions to FATIGUED state.
These explicit transitions create clear signals that enable agents to learn which actions
trigger negative states and which actions prevent them.
Third, explicit punishments create strong learning signals: recovery fatigue penalizes
repeated recovery attempts (-15% per recovery, max -45%), cumulative overload penalties
discourage exploitative strategies, and escalation to DISENGAGED state (dwell 0.05–
0.15) creates an unmistakable penalty for failing to transition at exhausted exhibits.
These punishments make exploitative strategies unprofitable, forcing agents to learn
engagement-adaptive behaviors.
Fourth, explicit recovery mechanisms provide state-specific recovery actions with de-
fined success rates:ClarifyFactrecovers CONFUSED at 90%,AskQuestionvariants recover
OVERLOADED at 85%,SuggestMoverecovers multiple negative states (OVERLOADED,
FATIGUED, BORED OF TOPIC) at 75–95%. These explicit recovery pathways enable
agents to learn context-appropriate responses to engagement drops.
Fifth, stricter thresholds (3+ actions vs Sim8’s 4+) make exploitative strategies
unprofitable, forcing agents to learn engagement-adaptive behaviors rather than relying
on simple heuristics.
100
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
9.4.3 Flat Actor-Critic Policies’ Ability to Learn Engagement-Boosting Ac-
tions: The Key Finding
A key finding from H3 is that flat Actor-Critic policies can learn engagement-adaptive
behaviors when given clear signals. Flat Actor-Critic policies achieve 35.8% recovery
action usage under State Machine, demonstrating that they can learn to use recovery
actions when given explicit negative state modeling and clear recovery mechanisms. This
represents a fundamental capability that Sim8’s ambiguous signals prevent: flat policies
learn to detect when engagement is dropping (via explicit state transitions), adapt to dwell
time (via non-overlapping ranges providing clear feedback), and use context-appropriate
recovery actions (via state-specific success rates).
Most notably, flat Actor-Critic policies achieve 31.7% AskQuestion usage under State
Machine compared to 4.4% under Sim8, representing a 7.2-fold increase. This demonstrates
that flat policies learn to ask questions when engagement drops—a behavior that requires
detecting negative states and selecting appropriate recovery actions. With clear signals,
flat policies learn to recognize when visitors enter OVERLOADED or FATIGUED states
and respond withAskQuestionvariants that recover engagement at 80–85% success rates.
Flat Actor-Critic policies also achieve higher entropy (1.45 vs 1.20 for Sim8), indicating
more balanced action distribution and adaptive behavior. The combination of high recovery
action usage, high AskQuestion usage, and balanced action distribution demonstrates
that flat policies can learn engagement-responsive strategies when simulator signals are
clear and informative.
Shi and Yu [2019] emphasize that user simulators for RL dialogue must provide
learnable signals with sufficient dynamic range that agents can discover which actions
improve engagement. Our results extend this finding: not only do clear signals enable
faster learning, but they enable learning of behaviors that are fundamentally impossible
with ambiguous signals. Flat Actor-Critic policies’ ability to learn engagement-boosting
actions (35.8% recovery, 31.7% AskQuestion) demonstrates that the quality of simulator
signals fundamentally determines what agents can learn.
9.4.4 Real-World Implications: Better Simulators Enable Better Learning
The finding that flat Actor-Critic policies can learn engagement-adaptive behaviors with
clear signals has important real-world implications. First, investment in better simulators
and more accurate training data enables agents to learn engagement-responsive behaviors.
With training data that approaches State Machine’s clarity (explicit state modeling,
non-overlapping signals, clear recovery mechanisms), agents can learn to adapt to dwell
time and ask questions when engagement drops.
Second, multimodal feedback in real deployment may approach State Machine clarity
rather than Sim8 ambiguity. Real visitors provide feedback through multiple modali-
ties: gaze patterns (dwell time, saccades), verbal expressions (”that’s too much”, ”I’m
confused”), and physical proximity (moving away, leaning in). When combined, these
multimodal signals may provide clear, unambiguous feedback that approaches State
Machine’s explicit state modeling. A visitor who becomes overloaded does not maintain
neutral 0.40–0.70 dwell; they look away (dwell < 0.30), verbally express confusion, or
physically disengage. These combined signals create clear, learnable feedback that enables
engagement-adaptive learning.
Third, the quality of simulator signals fundamentally determines what agents can
learn. Sim8’s overlapping ranges represent measurement noise and individual variability,
101
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
but they obscure the central tendency that State Machine captures: engaged visitors
provide distinctly different signals than disengaged ones. Real deployment likely falls
between these extremes, and H3 establishes that the algorithm’s performance scales with
signal quality—a finding with direct implications for human-in-the-loop training where
multimodal visitor feedback may enable engagement-adaptive learning.
Davidson and Yu [2023] survey user simulation approaches for task-oriented dialogue,
finding that rule-based simulators (like our State Machine) can provide clearer signals
than data-driven simulators (like Sim8), though they may sacrifice realism. However,
our results suggest that signal clarity may be more important than realism for learning
engagement-adaptive behaviors: flat Actor-Critic policies learn recovery strategies (35.8%
recovery, 31.7% AskQuestion) with State Machine’s clear signals, while Sim8’s realistic
but ambiguous signals prevent this learning (4.4% AskQuestion, 0% explicit recovery).
9.4.5 Option-Critic Policies and Signal Quality
For Option-Critic policies, State Machine enables better option diversity (OCI 51.1 vs
88.3) while maintaining similar coverage (47.9% vs 49.1%). However, as discussed in H1,
the fundamental termination gradient suppression mechanism still operates: accumulated
Q-values from past rewards keep AO(s,Explain ) > 0 even when current rewards degrade.
Clear signals help diversity by making termination conditions more learnable, but they do
not fully overcome the credit assignment challenges inherent in hierarchical policies. Option-
Critic policies achieve 17.6% recovery action usage under State Machine, demonstrating
some ability to learn recovery behaviors, but this is substantially lower than flat Actor-
Critic policies’ 35.8%, suggesting that architecture still matters more than signal quality
alone for hierarchical policies.
9.5 H4: State Representation Efficiency
Beyond simulator design and reward engineering, state representation efficiency also affects
hierarchical policy learning.
H4 reveals that compact state representation (DialogueAct: 23-d) not only maintains
performance but actually improves option diversity (OCI 41.3 vs 88.3) compared to
dense embeddings (DialogueBERT: 143-d). This suggests that reduced dimensionality
facilitates more effective policy learning by reducing the complexity of the value function
approximation problem.
While Zhang et al. [2021b] demonstrate that DialogueBERT captures rich semantic
information through dense embeddings, we show compact representations can improve
learning effectiveness. The 84% reduction in state dimensionality (143-d → 23-d) not only
reduces computational requirements but actually improves option diversity, suggesting
that for hierarchical policies, compact representations enable more effective value function
learning.
The 84% reduction in state dimensionality (143-d → 23-d) reduces the complexity of
the value function approximation, potentially enabling the critic to learn more effectively
with limited data. The dialogue-act representation maps visitor utterances to probability
distributions over communicative functions (Inform, Question, Directive, Commissive),
providing semantic information relevant to dialogue policy learning while eliminating
redundant information in dense embeddings.
Sutton and Barto [2018] explain that function approximation in reinforcement learning
102
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
faces a fundamental trade-off: richer state representations provide more information
but require more data to learn effectively, while compact representations may sacrifice
information but enable faster learning. The curse of dimensionality means that value
function approximation becomes exponentially harder as state dimensionality increases,
making compact representations particularly valuable for sample-efficient learning.
This reduction in learning complexity is particularly important for hierarchical policies,
which must learn three value functions (V (s), QΩ(s, ω), QU(s, ω, a)) simultaneously. With
fewer parameters to learn, the value functions may converge more reliably, potentially
improving credit assignment and enabling better termination learning. The improved
option diversity (OCI 41.3 vs 88.3) suggests that the compact representation may help
the termination function distinguish between option states more effectively.
Dialogue-act classification follows established dialogue act taxonomies [Bunt et al., 2011,
Stolcke et al., 2000]. The ISO 24617-2 taxonomy [Bunt et al., 2011] defines communicative
functions (Inform, Question, Directive, Commissive) that map to dialogue policy decisions:
Inform actions (Explain) provide information, Question actions (AskQuestion) seek
information, Directive actions (OfferTransition) guide behavior, and Commissive actions
(Conclude) commit to future behavior. The SWBD-DAMSL taxonomy [Stolcke et al.,
2000] provides empirical validation of these categories in conversational speech, supporting
their use in dialogue systems.
Zhang et al. [2021b] introduce DialogueBERT, a self-supervised dialogue pre-training
encoder that learns dense embeddings from dialogue context. While DialogueBERT
captures rich semantic information, it requires 143 dimensions to represent dialogue
state, creating a high-dimensional learning problem. Dialogue-act classification provides
a compact alternative that maintains semantic information relevant to dialogue policy
learning while substantially reducing dimensionality.
The efficiency gain is substantial: DialogueAct uses 23-d state vectors compared to
DialogueBERT’s 143-d embeddings, representing an 84% reduction in state dimensionality.
This reduction provides computational benefits for deployment: lower memory require-
ments, faster inference, and reduced training data needs while maintaining or improving
performance metrics.
DialogueAct achieves comparable coverage (49.4% vs 49.1%) and higher return (24.9
vs 18.3) compared to DialogueBERT while providing substantial efficiency benefits. This
represents a favorable trade-off: the compact representation not only reduces computational
requirements but actually improves learning effectiveness. The 84% reduction in state
dimensionality (143-d → 23-d) provides clear benefits for real-world deployment (lower
memory, faster inference) while maintaining or improving performance metrics.
The computational efficiency gains are substantial: DialogueAct uses 6.2×smaller
state vectors (23-d vs 143-d), reducing memory footprint and accelerating inference. For
hierarchical policies that must learn three value functions ( V (s), QΩ(s, ω), QU(s, ω, a))
simultaneously, this dimensionality reduction translates to fewer parameters to learn
and faster convergence. The compact representation also enables more sample-efficient
learning: with 84% fewer dimensions, the value function approximation problem becomes
more tractable, potentially requiring less training data to achieve comparable or better
performance. These efficiency benefits are particularly valuable for real-world deployment
where computational resources and training data may be limited.
Sutton and Barto [2018] note that state representation design is a fundamental choice
in reinforcement learning, with implications for both learning efficiency and computational
requirements. Compact representations that maintain task-relevant information can enable
103
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
more efficient learning while reducing computational costs, making them particularly
valuable for real-world deployment.
9.6 H5: When Hierarchy Helps—Reward-Aligned Design
While H1–H4 demonstrate hierarchical limitations, H5 tests whether improved option
design can overcome these challenges.
H5 compares reward-aligned option design (grouping actions by reward component)
with function-based design (grouping actions by communicative function). Reward-aligned
design achieves 55.5% coverage and OCI 2.1, while function-based design achieves 49.5%
coverage and OCI 74.7. This 6.0 percentage point improvement in coverage and 72.6 point
reduction in option collapse is the largest improvement observed across H1–H5, though it
still leaves a 43.2 percentage point gap to flat Actor-Critic baseline (98.7% coverage).
Extending Kulkarni et al. [2016] and Machado et al. [2017]’s reward-component
alignment principle, we demonstrate one-to-one reward-option correspondence improves
Option-Critic performance. The substantial difference between function-based and reward-
aligned designs (OCI 74.7 vs 2.1) shows that structural choices fundamentally affect
learning dynamics, independent of algorithmic parameters.
The reward-aligned design groups actions by reward component rather than com-
municative function, creating one-to-one correspondence between options and reward
types: Explain (novelty), Engage (engagement), Transition (coverage), and Conclude
(termination). WhenExplainNewFactbecomes masked at exhausted exhibits, the entire
Explain option becomes unavailable through option-level masking [Huang et al., 2020],
forcing the policy to switch rather than relying on learned termination. This may address
the persistence problem observed in function-based design, where the Explain option could
continue using fallback subactions (ClarifyFact,RepeatFact) even afterExplainNewFact
was masked.
The improvement in option diversity (OCI 2.1 vs 74.7) results from both cleaner credit
assignment enabled by one-to-one reward-option correspondence and option-level masking
providing a structural constraint that prevents persistence. Reward-aligned design achieves
balanced option distribution (Engage 53.3%, Explain 36.9%, Transition 7.8%, Conclude
2.1%) while function-based design shows Explain collapse (87.5%), demonstrating that
both mechanisms contribute to improved performance.
Function-based design groups actions by communicative function following dialogue
act taxonomies [Bunt et al., 2011, Stolcke et al., 2000, Chi and Wylie, 2014], which aligns
with linguistic and pedagogical theory but creates credit assignment challenges. The key
difference is thatExplainNewFactandClarifyFactboth serve the Inform function but
produce different reward types (novelty vs engagement). Function-based design groups
them together, creating diffuse credit assignment; reward-aligned design separates them,
enabling cleaner credit assignment.
When options have distinct reward profiles, the option-level advantage AO(s, ω)
provides clearer signals for termination learning. When options mix reward types, the
advantage signal becomes ambiguous, making it more difficult for the termination function
to learn when to switch. This suggests that reward-component alignment is more important
than communicative-function alignment for Option-Critic learning in this domain.
The comparison reveals a trade-off between theoretical alignment (with linguistic
theory) and learning alignment (with reward structure). Function-based design has
theoretical grounding in dialogue act taxonomies and pedagogical frameworks, but fails in
104
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
practice due to credit assignment challenges. Reward-aligned design, while less aligned
with linguistic theory, works better because it aligns with how Option-Critic learns
termination. This suggests that for hierarchical reinforcement learning, learning dynamics
matter more than theoretical elegance.
The principle of reward-component alignment connects to intrinsic motivation theory
[Singh et al., 2005, Pathak et al., 2017] and hierarchical learning frameworks [Kulkarni
et al., 2016, Machado et al., 2017], which suggest that aligning option boundaries with
reward structure enables effective learning. Our results demonstrate that this alignment
improves termination learning and credit assignment by creating distinct reward profiles
for each option, enabling clearer advantage signals for termination decisions.
Reward-aligned design still falls short of flat Actor-Critic performance (55.5% vs
98.7% coverage), suggesting that even with improved option design, hierarchical credit
assignment and termination learning remain challenging. The improvement observed (6.0
percentage points) is meaningful but modest relative to the overall gap, raising questions
about whether additional design choices beyond option structure could further improve
performance, or whether there are inherent limits to hierarchical approaches in dialogue
domains with dynamic action availability.
9.7 Contrasting Interpretations
Two interpretations explain Option-Critic’s underperformance:
Interpretation A: Hierarchy is unnecessary when prompts structure be-
havior.The LLM-based action execution already provides temporal coherence through
structured prompts (e.g., OPTION: Explain | SUBACTION: ExplainNewFact | FOCUS:
[exhibit]). Action masking enforces valid transitions (noExplainNewFactat exhausted
exhibits). Together, these structural constraints provide the benefits hierarchy is meant
to deliver—temporal abstraction and option coherence—without requiring learned termi-
nation.
Interpretation B: Hierarchy could help, but Option-Critic is the wrong
algorithm.Option-Critic’s reliance on learned termination via advantage signals fails
when reward structure creates persistent positive advantages [Bacon et al., 2017]’s
“persistent option selection”). Alternative HRL algorithms with fixed managers (FeUdal
Networks [Dayan and Hinton, 1993]), constraint-based termination (HAM [Parr and
Russell, 1998]), or state-based termination could succeed.
Our results are more consistent with Interpretation A.Three findings support
this:
1. Reward-aligned design with option-level masking (H5) achieves OCI 2.1 by bypassing
learned termination entirely—but still trails flat policies by 43.2pp. If the issue were
just Option-Critic’s termination mechanism, structural masking should close the
gap.
2. Flat policies learn engagement-adaptive behaviors (35.8% recovery actions, 31.7%
AskQuestion) without any hierarchy, demonstrating that temporal abstraction is
not required for engagement-adaptive dialogue in this domain.
3. The 8–12 action space is small enough that direct policy learning is tractable;
hierarchy provides no sample efficiency benefit.
105
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
We cannot rule out Interpretation B without testing alternative HRL algorithms, and
with n = 3 seeds and simulator-only evaluation, these interpretations remain tentative.
However, the available evidence suggests hierarchy itself—not just Option-Critic—may be
unnecessary in this domain when external structure (prompts, masking) already provides
temporal coherence.
9.8 LLM-in-the-Loop Nonstationarity
The environment is not a classic stationary MDP from the learner’s perspective. Three
sources of nonstationarity exist:
Prompt template variation: Different action selections produce different prompt
templates (e.g., SUBACTION: ExplainNewFact vs SUBACTION: ClarifyFact ), which yield
different LLM response distributions even for identical visitor states.
LLM generation stochasticity: Temperature-based sampling creates response
variability that affects subsequent visitor reactions and reward signals.
Dynamic action masking: Available actions change based on episode history (exhibit
exhaustion), creating non-stationary transition dynamics.
What we assume stationary: The simulators (Sim8, State Machine) are fixed
programs with deterministic or pseudo-random dynamics. Reward definitions are fixed
throughout training. The LLM itself is not fine-tuned during training.
Implications: Flat policies may be more robust to this nonstationarity because
they make independent action decisions each turn. Hierarchical policies accumulate
nonstationarity across option duration—the Q-value estimate for a 5-turn Explain option
reflects 5 turns of LLM variation, compounding credit assignment challenges. This may
partially explain why shorter option durations (hard budgets) and structural termination
(masking) help: they limit exposure to accumulated nonstationarity.
9.9 Synthesis: When Hierarchy Helps and When It Hurts
Flat Actor-Critic policies provide a robust, efficient solution for museum dialogue, achieving
98.7% coverage while learning engagement-adaptive behaviors. While hierarchy can
improve with reward-aligned design, the persistent 43.2 percentage point gap demonstrates
that architectural complexity does not provide benefits in this domain. The experimental
results across five hypotheses reveal a clear pattern: hierarchy helps when option structure
aligns with reward structure and credit assignment is clean, but it hurts when option
structure creates diffuse credit assignment and termination learning is suppressed. The
fundamental trade-off is between temporal abstraction (hierarchy’s advantage) and credit
assignment clarity (hierarchy’s challenge).
Reward-aligned design (H5) achieves the best Option-Critic performance when three
conditions are met. First, one-to-one reward-option correspondence ensures each option
has a single, well-defined reward profile, reducing credit assignment ambiguity. This follows
the principle of reward-component alignment established by Kulkarni et al. [2016] and
Machado et al. [2017]. Second, structural constraints complement learning: option-level
masking ensures options cannot persist when their reward sources are unavailable, even
if termination learning fails. This extends the action masking principles of Huang et al.
[2020] to hierarchical settings. Third, clear termination conditions enable more effective
termination learning: when an option’s primary reward source becomes unavailable,
106
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
the option-level advantage drops, triggering termination. This partially addresses the
persistent option selection failure mode (discussed in H1) identified by Bacon et al. [2017].
Under these conditions, Option-Critic policies achieve 55.5% coverage and OCI 2.1,
representing the best Option-Critic performance observed. However, this best-case
Option-Critic performance remains far below flat Actor-Critic policies’ 98.7% coverage,
representing a 43.2 percentage point gap. This persistent large gap suggests that hierarchy
has fundamental structural limitations in this domain that cannot be overcome through
option design alone.
Flat Actor-Critic policies maintain their performance advantage because they avoid
the fundamental challenges of hierarchical credit assignment. First, flat policies require no
termination learning: they make primitive action decisions at each turn, eliminating the
need to learn when to terminate high-level options. This follows the flat reinforcement
learning principles of Sutton and Barto [2018]. Second, flat policies enable more direct
credit assignment: action-level Q-values directly capture the value of specific actions,
without needing to attribute credit across hierarchical levels. This avoids the value function
decomposition challenges identified by Dietterich [2000]. Third, flat policies achieve faster
adaptation: when action availability changes (e.g.,ExplainNewFactbecomes masked),
flat policies can immediately switch based on current state, without needing to learn
termination behavior. This enables rapid adaptation to changing context.
These advantages are particularly important in dialogue domains where action avail-
ability changes dynamically (exhibits become exhausted, visitor states transition) and
rapid adaptation is critical. The results demonstrate that for museum dialogue, the costs
of credit assignment complexity and termination learning challenges outweigh any benefits
of temporal abstraction. The persistent large gap between best-case Option-Critic (55.5%
coverage) and flat Actor-Critic (98.7% coverage) indicates that hierarchy does not help in
this domain, regardless of design choices.
9.9.1 Decision Framework for Practitioners
Based on our experimental findings across five hypotheses, we provide a decision framework
for choosing between flat and hierarchical reinforcement learning approaches in dialogue
systems.
Use flat Actor-Critic policies when: (1) Action availability changes dynamically (e.g.,
content becomes exhausted, context-dependent actions), (2) Rapid adaptation is critical
(e.g., visitor state transitions require immediate response), (3) Action space is manageable
(8–12 primitive actions), and (4) State representation is sufficient for direct action selection.
Our results demonstrate that flat policies achieve 86–99% coverage (average 94%) in these
conditions, substantially outperforming hierarchical approaches.
Consider hierarchical RL with reward-aligned option design when: (1) Action space
is stable (no dynamic masking), (2) Clear temporal structure exists (e.g., multi-step
subgoals with distinct phases), (3) Option boundaries can be aligned with reward
components (one-to-one correspondence), and (4) Structural constraints (option-level
masking) can complement termination learning. Even under these conditions, our results
show hierarchical policies achieve at most 55.5% coverage, leaving a 43.2 percentage point
gap to flat policies.
Key principles: (1) Signal quality outweighs architecture complexity: Investment in
better simulators and training data provides larger gains than architectural complexity.
(2) Reward-component alignment matters: When using hierarchy, align option boundaries
107
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
with reward structure rather than theoretical frameworks. (3) Dynamic action availability
favors flat RL: Domains with exhaustible resources or context-dependent actions benefit
from flat policies’ direct adaptation. (4) Compact representations help: DialogueAct’s
84% dimensionality reduction with maintained or improved performance demonstrates
the value of efficient state representations.
9.9.2 Implications for Hierarchical Reinforcement Learning Research
This work addresses a fundamental question in hierarchical reinforcement learning: when
does temporal abstraction help vs hurt? Prior work assumes hierarchy is beneficial for
long-horizon tasks [Sutton et al., 1999, Dietterich, 2000]; we provide empirical evidence
that domain characteristics matter more than horizon length. We systematically ruled out
hyperparameter tuning, reward engineering, and option design as solutions, identifying
dynamic action availability as the key factor that determines when hierarchy helps or hurts.
This extends Option-Critic theory by showing where it fails (domains with exhaustible
content) not just where it succeeds (spatial navigation).
The experimental results have important implications for hierarchical reinforcement
learning (Option-Critic) research and practice. First, option design philosophy appears
more critical than hyperparameter tuning or reward engineering. The substantial difference
between function-based and reward-aligned designs (OCI 74.7 vs 2.1) demonstrates that
structural choices fundamentally affect learning dynamics, independent of algorithmic
parameters. This extends the option design principles of Sutton et al. [1999] and Bacon
et al. [2017] by suggesting that reward-component alignment may be critical for effective
termination learning.
Second, termination learning appears to be a fundamental bottleneck for Option-Critic
architectures. The persistent option collapse observed across H1–H4 suggests that learned
termination functions may struggle when reward structure creates persistent positive
advantages for one option. Structural constraints (option-level masking) can complement
termination learning, but they may not fully replace it. This is consistent with the findings
of Bacon et al. [2017] and Harb et al. [2018] that termination learning is challenging in
Option-Critic architectures.
Third, the performance gap between Option-Critic and flat Actor-Critic policies
demonstrates that hierarchy is not beneficial in this domain. Even the best Option-Critic
configuration (reward-aligned: 55.5% coverage) remains far below flat Actor-Critic policies
(98.7% coverage), indicating that hierarchy does not help despite temporal abstraction
seeming theoretically advantageous. In dialogue domains where action availability
changes dynamically and rapid adaptation is critical, flat policies substantially outperform
hierarchical policies. This challenges the assumption that hierarchy is always beneficial
for long-horizon tasks [Sutton et al., 1999, Dietterich, 2000], at least in domains with
dynamic action availability.
Finally, while reward-aligned design achieves the best Option-Critic performance, it
still falls far short of flat Actor-Critic policies, suggesting that option design improvements
are insufficient to overcome hierarchy’s fundamental limitations in this domain. Future
hierarchical RL research should acknowledge that hierarchy may not always be beneficial,
even with optimal option design. The one-to-one reward-option correspondence principle
may help improve Option-Critic performance relative to other Option-Critic configurations,
but it does not address the fundamental gap between hierarchical and flat policies.
108
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
9.9.3 Implications for Dialogue Systems
Our findings have specific implications for hierarchical reinforcement learning (Option-
Critic) in dialogue systems. Prior work has demonstrated HRL success in spoken dialogue
[Cuay´ ahuitl et al., 2010], large-domain dialogue management [Casanueva et al., 2018], and
open-domain dialogue [Saleh et al., 2020]. However, these works often use hand-crafted
option structures or focus on domains with stable action availability. Our results suggest
that option design is critical: function-based designs that align with linguistic theory
may fail due to credit assignment challenges, while reward-aligned designs that align with
learning dynamics can succeed.
Wang et al. [2021] demonstrate that option frameworks can effectively model hier-
archical structure between dialogue policy and natural language generation, but they
use hand-crafted options aligned with dialogue structure. Our results extend this by
suggesting that reward-component alignment may be more important than structural
alignment for effective learning. Rohmatillah and Chien [2023] show that hierarchical
policies with guidance can improve multi-domain dialogue performance, but they assume
effective option design. Our results suggest that option design choices may fundamentally
affect whether hierarchy helps or hurts.
The museum dialogue domain presents unique challenges: dynamic action avail-
ability (exhibits become exhausted), visitor state transitions (engagement changes),
and multi-objective rewards (novelty, engagement, coverage). These challenges make
credit assignment particularly difficult for hierarchical policies, which explains why flat
Actor-Critic policies substantially outperform Option-Critic policies. Reward-aligned
design improves Option-Critic performance relative to function-based design, but even
the best Option-Critic configuration (55.5% coverage) remains far below flat Actor-Critic
policies (98.7% coverage), indicating that hierarchy does not help in this domain.
For dialogue system practitioners, our results suggest that flat Actor-Critic policies
(solving MDP formulations) should be preferred over Option-Critic policies (solving
SMDP formulations) for domains with dynamic action availability. While reward-aligned
design improves Option-Critic performance relative to function-based design, it does not
overcome hierarchy’s fundamental limitations. The persistent large gap between best-case
Option-Critic and flat Actor-Critic policies indicates that hierarchy does not provide
benefits in this domain, regardless of design choices.
10 Limitations
This work provides valuable insights into hierarchical reinforcement learning (Option-Critic)
for dialogue systems, but several limitations should be acknowledged that affect the
interpretation and generalizability of our findings. We first rank limitations by impact,
then provide detailed analysis organized by category.
10.1 Limitations Ranked by Impact
We rank limitations by their potential to affect the validity of our core findings:
No Real User Evaluation (Critical).The most significant limitation affecting
interpretation of our findings is the lack of real user evaluation. All experiments used
simulators; we did not deploy the system with actual museum visitors. As Schatzmann et al.
109
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
[2007] demonstrate, simulator-trained policies may not predict deployment success, and
our coverage/engagement metrics may not reflect user satisfaction or learning outcomes.
Small Sample Sizes (High).Our experiments use n≥ 3 random seeds per condition,
with some H3 conditions having n = 2 due to incomplete data. While this sample size
enables descriptive comparisons and effect size estimation, it limits our ability to perform
robust statistical significance testing. As Henderson et al. [2018] emphasize, RL algorithms
exhibit high variance across random seeds, and overlapping confidence intervals with small
sample sizes can mask true performance differences.
Prompt Templates as Confound (High).Structured prompts provide temporal
coherence independent of the RL architecture, creating a potential confound in interpreting
results. We cannot fully isolate whether flat policy success stems primarily from RL
learning or from the prompt structure imposing appropriate behavior. This limitation
means the RL contribution may be smaller than our results suggest if much of the
performance comes from prompt engineering.
Single Domain (Moderate).All experiments use a single museum collection: five
Dutch Golden Age portrait exhibits with 21 facts total. Results may not generalize to
other museums, domains, or languages. Findings may be specific to art historical content,
small knowledge bases, or this particular exhibit structure.
Option-Critic Only (Moderate).We tested only the Option-Critic algorithm for
hierarchical RL; alternative HRL algorithms (FeUdal Networks, HAM, HPPO) may have
different failure modes or may be better suited to dialogue domains with dynamic action
availability. The observed failures—option collapse, termination learning challenges—may
be Option-Critic-specific rather than fundamental to all hierarchical approaches.
Coverage as Proxy Metric (Moderate).Exhibit coverage (percentage of facts
mentioned) is our primary performance metric, but coverage may not correlate with user
satisfaction or educational effectiveness. Policies optimized for coverage may maximize
fact mentions without ensuring comprehension or engagement quality. This means our
main success criterion may not reflect the true objectives of museum dialogue systems.
The detailed analysis of each limitation follows in subsequent sections.
10.2 Methodological Limitations
10.2.1 Sample Size and Statistical Inference
Our experiments use n≥ 3 random seeds per condition. While this sample size enables
descriptive comparisons and effect size estimation, it limits our ability to perform robust
statistical significance testing. Henderson et al. [2018] emphasize the importance of multiple
seeds (n5) for reliable reinforcement learning evaluation, noting that RL algorithms exhibit
high variance across random seeds. Colas et al. [2019] demonstrate that overlapping
confidence intervals with small sample sizes can mask true performance differences, making
it difficult to distinguish between meaningful improvements and random variation.
The trade-off we faced was between breadth (exploring 53 experimental conditions in
H1) and depth (more seeds per condition). This is a common challenge in exploratory
research where systematic exploration of the parameter space is prioritized over extensive
replication. Agarwal et al. [2021] note that high variance in RL results requires extensive
replication, but also acknowledge that exploratory research benefits from broad parameter
space coverage. Our results should therefore be interpreted as descriptive comparisons
supported by effect sizes and consistency patterns rather than statistically validated
differences.
110
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
The consistency of patterns across conditions (e.g., option collapse observed across
all Option-Critic configurations, flat Actor-Critic policies consistently outperforming
Option-Critic policies) suggests that the effects are robust despite limited sample sizes.
However, future work should replicate the best-performing configurations with n5 seeds
to enable statistical validation and more robust confidence intervals.
10.2.2 Fixed Experimental Conditions
All experiments fix the exhibit itinerary, visitor persona seed, and knowledge base across
runs, changing only the model variant. This design choice isolates architectural and
algorithmic effects from environmental variability, which is appropriate for controlled
comparisons. However, Henderson et al. [2018] recommend varying environmental condi-
tions to assess robustness, noting that performance may depend on specific environmental
characteristics.
Our fixed conditions mean that results may not generalize to different exhibit orders,
visitor persona types, or knowledge base configurations. Performance may depend on the
specific exhibit structure (5 exhibits with 21 facts total), the particular persona seed used,
or the knowledge base content (Dutch Golden Age portraits). Shi and Yu [2019] note that
simulator-based evaluation benefits from controlled conditions but limits generalizability,
suggesting that our findings may be specific to the tested configuration.
The implications are that we cannot assess interaction effects between architectural
choices and environmental variability. For example, option collapse may be more or less
severe with different exhibit orders, or termination learning may be easier or harder with
different visitor persona characteristics.
10.2.3 Evaluation Window
We compute all metrics from the final 100 episodes after 1000-episode training, assuming
convergence by episode 900. Henderson et al. [2018] recommend post-convergence analysis
but note that convergence timing varies across conditions and algorithms. Colas et al.
[2019] emphasize the importance of analyzing learning dynamics throughout training, as
late-stage learning or instability may be missed by focusing only on the final episodes.
This limitation means we may underestimate performance if learning continues beyond
episode 900, or miss instability or performance degradation in later training. Some
conditions may require more than 1000 episodes to converge, particularly hierarchical
policies with complex credit assignment. The evaluation window also assumes that the
final 100 episodes represent stable, converged performance, which may not hold for all
conditions.
10.3 Simulator-Based Evaluation Limitations
10.3.1 Simulator Fidelity vs Signal Clarity Trade-off
We evaluate two simulators representing different points on the fidelity-clarity trade-off:
Sim8 (realistic but ambiguous signals) and State Machine (clear but idealized signals).
Neither simulator fully captures real visitor behavior, and results may not generalize to
deployment with real museum visitors. Shi and Yu [2019] note that simulator design is
critical to what agents learn, with design choices fundamentally affecting learned policies.
Davidson and Yu [2023] survey user simulation approaches for task-oriented dialogue,
111
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
finding that rule-based simulators (like our State Machine) can provide clearer signals
than data-driven simulators (like Sim8), though they may sacrifice realism.
Schatzmann et al. [2007] demonstrate that user simulators can produce policies that
fail with real users, showing that simulator-based evaluation may not predict deployment
performance. This is a fundamental limitation of simulator-based research: we cannot
determine true deployment performance without human-in-the-loop evaluation. However,
H3 results suggest that real visitors (with multimodal feedback through gaze, verbal
expressions, and physical proximity) may enable better learning than Sim8’s ambiguous
signals, potentially approaching State Machine’s clarity.
The implications are that Sim8’s ambiguous signals may underestimate learning
capacity (H3 flat Actor-Critic results with State Machine suggest this), while State
Machine’s explicit state modeling may overestimate achievable performance with real
visitors. Real deployment likely falls between these extremes, with multimodal feedback
providing clearer signals than Sim8 but less idealized than State Machine.
10.3.2 Simulator Design Assumptions
Both simulators make assumptions about visitor behavior that may not hold in practice.
Sim8 uses overlapping dwell ranges reflecting empirical data from VR-based museum
interactions, while State Machine uses non-overlapping ranges with explicit state modeling
grounded in museum learning literature. Bitgood [2013] note that visitor attention patterns
are highly variable, and Falk and Dierking [2016] emphasize individual differences in
museum visitor behavior, suggesting that both simulators may oversimplify real visitor
diversity.
Sim8’s ambiguity may underestimate learning capacity, as H3 flat Actor-Critic results
with State Machine demonstrate that clear signals enable engagement-adaptive learning
(35.8% recovery action usage, 31.7% AskQuestion usage). However, State Machine’s
explicit states may not reflect how real visitors communicate engagement: real visitors
may provide feedback through multiple modalities (gaze patterns, verbal expressions,
body language) that create clearer signals than Sim8’s single-modality dwell time, but
may not match State Machine’s deterministic state transitions.
10.3.3 No Real User Evaluation
All evaluation is simulator-based; we have not deployed the system with real museum
visitors or collected user feedback. This limitation prevents assessment of real-world
performance, user satisfaction, or deployment feasibility. Schatzmann et al. [2007] show
that simulator-trained policies can fail with real users, demonstrating that simulator
performance may not predict deployment success.?emphasize the importance of real
user evaluation for dialogue systems, noting that user satisfaction and dialogue quality
cannot be fully assessed through simulator metrics.
The implications are that our performance metrics (coverage, engagement, episodic
return) may not translate to user satisfaction or educational effectiveness. Real visitors
may have different engagement patterns, question types, or interaction preferences than
simulators. Deployment challenges such as latency, error handling, user adaptation, and
real-time dialogue quality are not addressed by our simulator-based evaluation.
112
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
10.4 Domain and Task Limitations
10.4.1 Single Museum Domain
All experiments use a single museum collection: five Dutch Golden Age portrait exhibits
with 21 facts total. Results may not generalize to other museums, domains, or languages.
Derda and colleagues [2025] note that museum dialogue systems must adapt to different
collections and visitor types, suggesting that our findings may be specific to art historical
content and portrait exhibits. Woo et al. [2024] emphasize domain-specific challenges in
conversational agents, noting that different domains (science museums, history museums,
art museums) may have different dialogue requirements.
The exhibit-based structure (facts per exhibit, coverage tracking, action masking) may
be specific to this domain. Factual content (art historical information about portraits) may
differ from other museum types (scientific explanations, historical narratives, interactive
exhibits). Language (English) and cultural context (Dutch Golden Age) may not generalize
to other languages or cultural settings.
10.4.2 Limited Knowledge Base Size
The knowledge base contains 21 facts across 5 exhibits (average 4.2 facts per exhibit),
which is substantially smaller than real museum collections that may contain hundreds
or thousands of facts per exhibit. Derda and colleagues [2025] note that real museum
knowledge bases contain extensive information, and Bitgood [2013] show that exhibit
complexity affects visitor engagement, suggesting that our small knowledge base may not
reflect real-world challenges.
The implications are that coverage optimization (percentage of facts mentioned) may be
easier with a small knowledge base, and exhaustion detection (when all facts are mentioned)
occurs quickly, potentially affecting termination learning. Our small knowledge base may
not capture the challenges of managing large knowledge bases with thousands of facts,
where coverage optimization becomes more complex and exhaustion detection less relevant.
10.4.3 Fixed Action Space
The action space consists of 4 options with 8–12 subactions total, all hand-designed
based on dialogue act taxonomies and museum dialogue requirements. This fixed action
set may not capture the full range of museum dialogue behaviors. Bunt et al. [2011]
note that dialogue act taxonomies are continuously evolving, and Woo et al. [2024] show
that adaptive dialogue systems benefit from flexible action spaces, suggesting that our
hand-designed actions may miss important dialogue behaviors.
The implications are that we may miss important dialogue behaviors not captured
in the current action set, and hand-designed options may not be optimal for all visitor
types or situations. We cannot assess whether different action spaces (e.g., more options,
different subactions, learned actions) would improve performance. The fixed action space
also means we cannot evaluate the impact of action space design on hierarchical learning.
113
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
10.5 Architectural Limitations
10.5.1 Option-Critic Specific Limitations
All hierarchical experiments use the Option-Critic algorithm (a hierarchical Actor-Critic
method); we have not tested alternative hierarchical reinforcement learning algorithms.
This limitation means we cannot determine whether observed failures (option collapse,
termination learning challenges) are Option-Critic-specific or general to hierarchical RL.
Bacon et al. [2017] acknowledge termination learning challenges in Option-Critic, and
Harb et al. [2018] note that deliberation cost addresses symptoms but not root causes,
suggesting that termination learning may be a fundamental challenge for Option-Critic.
Alternative HRL algorithms (FeUdal Networks [Dayan and Hinton, 1993], HAM [Parr
and Russell, 1998], HPPO [Klissarov and Precup, 2021]) may have different failure modes
or may be better suited to dialogue domains with dynamic action availability. FeUdal
Networks use hand-crafted managers that may avoid termination learning challenges,
while HAM uses fixed hierarchies that may be more stable. We cannot assess whether
hierarchy itself is the problem or just Option-Critic’s specific implementation.
The implications are that termination gradient suppression may be Option-Critic-
specific rather than fundamental to hierarchy. Alternative architectures (e.g., FeUdal
Networks with hand-crafted managers) may perform better, or the problem may be specific
to learned termination in Option-Critic.
10.5.2 Implementation Validation
We have not validated our Option-Critic implementation on standard HRL benchmarks
(e.g., Four-Rooms gridworld), which means we cannot verify that the implementation
correctly implements the algorithm. Bacon et al. [2017] use Four-Rooms as a standard
validation benchmark, and Henderson et al. [2018] emphasize the importance of imple-
mentation validation, noting that implementation errors can cause performance issues
that are mistaken for algorithm limitations.
The implications are that observed failures could be due to implementation bugs rather
than algorithm limitations. We cannot rule out implementation errors as a contributing
factor to option collapse or termination learning challenges. While our implementation
follows Option-Critic specifications, validation on standard benchmarks would provide
confidence that failures are algorithmic rather than implementation-specific.
10.5.3 Termination Learning Assumptions
Termination learning relies entirely on the option-level advantage signal AO(s, ω) =
QΩ(s, ω) −V (s) to generate learning gradients. This assumes that the advantage signal
provides sufficient learning signal for effective termination, which may not hold for this
task. Bacon et al. [2017] note that termination learning assumes options have comparable
utility; when reward structure favors one option disproportionately, the mechanism may
break down. Harb et al. [2018] show that persistent option selection occurs when reward
structure creates persistent positive advantages for one option.
The implications are that termination gradient suppression may be fundamental to
Option-Critic’s design rather than fixable through hyperparameter tuning or reward
engineering. Alternative termination mechanisms (e.g., hard timeouts, state-based
termination, learned termination with different signals) may be necessary. Our results
114
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
suggest that option-level masking (structural constraint) can complement termination
learning, but may not fully replace it.
10.6 Experimental Design Limitations
10.6.1 Confounded Comparisons
Some interventions were tested under different hyperparameter regimes, creating con-
founded comparisons. For example, deliberation cost and termination regularization were
tested with γ = 0.99, while gamma/entropy tuning used γ = 0.80–0.95. This means we
cannot directly compare intervention effectiveness due to confounded hyperparameters.
Henderson et al. [2018] emphasize the importance of controlled comparisons in RL
experiments, and Colas et al. [2019] note that confounded designs limit causal inference.
The implications are that we cannot test for synergistic effects (e.g., does deliberation
cost work better with lower gamma?) or determine if the best configuration is truly optimal
or just best among tested combinations. We may miss optimal hyperparameter-intervention
combinations that would improve performance. Our claim that reward-aligned design is
the best intervention must be qualified: it’s the best among tested configurations, not
necessarily the globally optimal solution.
10.6.2 Missing Baseline Comparisons
We have not compared Option-Critic with alternative HRL algorithms, simpler tasks,
or hard termination baselines. This limitation means we cannot determine if failures
are algorithm-specific, task-specific, or implementation-specific. Henderson et al. [2018]
recommend comprehensive baseline comparisons, and Colas et al. [2019] emphasize the
importance of ablation studies to isolate component effects.
Missing baselines include: (1) Option-Critic on Four-Rooms gridworld (standard
benchmark) to validate implementation and assess task-specificity; (2) alternative HRL
algorithms (FeUdal Networks, HAM, HPPO) to determine if failures are Option-Critic-
specific; (3) hard termination baseline (force termination after N steps) to assess whether
bypassing termination learning improves performance; (4) flat policy with same action
space to determine if option collapse is hierarchy-specific or action-space-specific.
The implications are that we cannot determine if Option-Critic is fundamentally
incompatible with this task or just needs different hyperparameters, whether simpler tasks
would enable successful hierarchical learning, or whether hard termination would improve
performance.
10.6.3 Limited Hyperparameter Space Exploration
While we systematically explored 53 conditions in H1, the hyperparameter space is
high-dimensional and difficult to explore exhaustively. Henderson et al. [2018] note that
RL hyperparameter spaces are high-dimensional and difficult to explore exhaustively,
and Andrychowicz et al. [2020] show that architectural choices matter more than hy-
perparameter tuning, but optimal hyperparameters may still lie outside the explored
space.
The implications are that we may have missed optimal hyperparameter configurations.
However, the systematic exploration across 53 conditions provides reasonable coverage,
and the persistent architectural gap to flat Actor-Critic policies (45–49 percentage points
115
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
across all configurations) suggests that hyperparameters are not the primary issue. The
relatively flat optimization landscape (coverage ranges from 27% to 54% with overlapping
confidence intervals) further suggests that hyperparameter tuning operates within a
constrained performance envelope.
10.7 Evaluation Metrics Limitations
10.7.1 Coverage as Primary Metric
Exhibit coverage (percentage of facts mentioned) is our primary performance metric, but
coverage may not correlate with user satisfaction or educational effectiveness. Derda and
colleagues [2025] note that visitor satisfaction depends on engagement quality, not just
coverage, and Falk and Dierking [2016] show that learning outcomes depend on visitor
interest and prior knowledge, suggesting that high coverage may not indicate good user
experience.
The implications are that policies optimized for coverage may not optimize user
satisfaction or learning outcomes. High coverage may incentivize policies that mention
facts without ensuring understanding, or that prioritize breadth over depth. Coverage
does not measure educational effectiveness, visitor learning, or dialogue quality. Our focus
on coverage as the primary metric may have led us to optimize for a proxy measure rather
than the true objective.
10.7.2 Engagement Measurement via Simulator
Engagement is measured via simulator-generated dwell time, which may not reflect real
visitor engagement. Bitgood [2013] note that gaze patterns are imperfect proxies for
engagement, and Falk and Dierking [2016] emphasize that engagement is multidimensional
(cognitive, emotional, behavioral), suggesting that dwell time may not capture all aspects
of engagement.
The implications are that policies optimized for simulator engagement may not optimize
real visitor engagement. Dwell time may not capture curiosity, understanding, enjoyment,
or other dimensions of engagement. Real visitors may have different engagement patterns
than simulators, and engagement may be communicated through multiple modalities
(gaze, verbal, physical) that simulators may not fully capture.
10.7.3 Limited Behavioral Analysis
We focus on aggregate metrics (coverage, return, OCI) rather than detailed behavioral
patterns or qualitative dialogue analysis. This limitation means we may miss important
qualitative differences in dialogue strategies.?emphasize the importance of qualitative
dialogue analysis, and Derda and colleagues [2025] note that dialogue quality matters
more than coverage, suggesting that aggregate metrics may not capture important aspects
of dialogue performance.
The implications are that we cannot assess whether policies produce natural, coherent,
or engaging dialogues. We may miss important failure modes (e.g., repetitive responses,
inappropriate transitions, unnatural language) that aggregate metrics don’t capture. Our
evaluation does not assess user experience, dialogue quality, or educational effectiveness
beyond coverage and engagement.
116
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
10.8 Generalizability Limitations
10.8.1 Task Structure Specificity
The exhibit-based structure with factual content, coverage tracking, and action masking
may be specific to museum dialogue tasks. Results may not generalize to other dialogue
tasks with different structures. Cuay´ ahuitl et al. [2010] show that HRL performance
varies across dialogue domains, and Saleh et al. [2020] note that task structure affects
hierarchical learning effectiveness, suggesting that our findings may be domain-specific.
The implications are that findings may be specific to museum dialogue with exhibit-
based structure, and may not apply to other dialogue tasks (customer service, educational
tutoring, social chat). Action masking and coverage tracking may be domain-specific
features that don’t generalize. The dynamic action availability (exhibits become exhausted)
may be specific to museum dialogue and may not occur in other domains.
10.8.2 Visitor Persona Limitations
We use three personas (Agreeable, Conscientious, Neurotic) based on Big Five traits,
which may not capture the full diversity of real visitor characteristics. Falk and Dierking
[2016] note that visitor characteristics are highly diverse, and Bitgood [2013] show that
individual differences affect engagement patterns, suggesting that our persona modeling
may oversimplify real visitor diversity.
The implications are that policies may not generalize to visitors with different
personality traits, prior knowledge, interests, or cultural backgrounds. We may miss
important visitor types (e.g., experts, families, children, non-native speakers) that have
different dialogue needs. Persona modeling may oversimplify the complexity of real visitor
diversity.
10.9 Positive Findings and Generalizability
Despite limitations, several findings appear robust and generalizable. Flat Actor-Critic
policies’ superior performance (98.7% vs 55.5%) is consistent across simulators, reward
functions, and state representations, suggesting this finding generalizes to similar domains
with dynamic action availability. The signal quality outweighs architecture complexity
finding (H3) appears generalizable to any RL domain where training signals vary in clarity:
when signals are ambiguous, neither architecture can learn effectively; when signals are
clear, both can learn, but flat policies adapt faster.
DialogueAct’s 84% dimensionality reduction with maintained or improved performance
suggests compact representations are valuable across dialogue domains. The finding
that dynamic action availability favors flat RL likely generalizes to other domains with
exhaustible resources or context-dependent actions (e.g., tutoring systems with exhaustible
explanations, customer service with context-dependent responses).
The consistency of patterns across 53 conditions and the clear identification of when
flat RL succeeds provide valuable contributions despite methodological limitations. The
systematic exploration establishes that flat RL is a robust solution for dialogue domains
with dynamic action availability, with implications extending beyond museum dialogue to
any domain with similar characteristics.
117
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
10.10 Summary and Future Directions
