While these limitations constrain generalizability, the consistency of patterns across
53 conditions and the clear identification of when flat RL succeeds provide valuable
contributions. The systematic exploration establishes that flat RL is a robust solution for
dialogue domains with dynamic action availability, with implications extending beyond
museum dialogue to any domain with similar characteristics. The small sample sizes,
simulator-based evaluation, domain specificity, and Option-Critic-specific focus mean that
some findings may not fully generalize to real-world deployment or other domains, but the
core finding—that flat RL outperforms hierarchical RL in domains with dynamic action
availability—appears robust.
10.10.1 Implications for Interactive Museum Technology
Museum designers care about: engagement (visitor attention), coverage (content delivery),
safety (appropriate responses), controllability (predictable behavior), and robustness
(handling diverse visitors). Our findings address each concern:
Engagement: Flat policies achieve 35.8% recovery action usage with clear signals
(State Machine), demonstrating they can detect and respond to engagement drops.
Investment in better engagement sensing (multimodal feedback: gaze, verbal cues,
proximity) may enable better adaptation than architectural complexity.
Coverage: Flat policies achieve 98.7% coverage vs hierarchical policies’ 55.5%
maximum. For content delivery goals, flat RL is unambiguously superior in this domain.
Safety and controllability: Action masking provides structural guarantees (no
invalid transitions) without learned behavior. Hierarchical policies’ option persistence
failure mode poses controllability risks—agents may “lock in” to inappropriate behaviors.
Flat policies’ turn-by-turn decisions enable easier intervention and correction.
Robustness: Flat policies maintain high performance across simulator types (Sim8:
98.7%, State Machine: 86–98% across weight conditions), suggesting robustness to
signal quality variation. Hierarchical policies show higher variance and sensitivity to
hyperparameters.
Recommendation for museum deployments: Use flat Actor-Critic with structured
prompts and action masking. Invest in engagement sensing rather than algorithmic
complexity. The 43.2pp performance gap and superior controllability make this a clear
recommendation for production museum systems.
10.10.2 Future Directions
The limitations identified above point to four priority areas for future research. First, real-
world validation is essential: deploying the best-performing configurations (flat Actor-Critic
with structured prompts) with actual museum visitors would validate whether simulator-
based findings translate to deployment performance, and replicating key comparisons with
larger sample sizes (n≥10) would enable robust statistical validation.
Second, algorithmic exploration could clarify whether the observed limitations are
specific to Option-Critic or fundamental to hierarchy: testing fixed-manager HRL (FeUdal
Networks) and constraint-based HRL (HAM) would isolate the role of learned termination,
while ablating prompt structure would quantify the RL contribution separately from
prompt engineering benefits.
118
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
Third, domain generalization is needed to assess broader applicability: testing on
additional museum types (science, history), larger knowledge bases (100+ facts), and
other languages would determine whether findings extend beyond art dialogue with small
knowledge bases.
Finally, evaluation improvements would strengthen validity: incorporating user sat-
isfaction measures, learning outcome assessments, and qualitative dialogue analysis
would validate that coverage improvements correspond to better visitor experiences
and educational effectiveness.
11 Conclusion
This thesis investigated hierarchical reinforcement learning for museum conversational
agents through five controlled comparisons: architecture (SMDP vs MDP), reward shaping,
simulator design, state representation efficiency, and option design philosophy.
11.1 Contributions
This thesis contributes six empirical and methodological advances in the museum dialogue
domain:
1. Systematic SMDP vs MDP comparison: A controlled comparison of Option-
Critic and flat Actor-Critic under identical reward functions, action masking, and
prompt engineering across 53 experimental conditions, demonstrating flat policies’
43–49pp advantage in this dialogue setting with dynamic action availability.
2. Option collapse diagnostic: Use of Option Collapse Index (OCI), a simple
quantitative diagnostic for tracking option persistence patterns, revealing systematic
termination gradient suppression (OCI 87–90) across all baseline Option-Critic
configurations in this domain.
3. Signal quality analysis: Evidence that simulator signal clarity bounds the learning
ceiling rather than just learning speed in this setting—State Machine enables
engagement-adaptive behaviors (35.8% recovery actions) not observed under Sim8’s
ambiguous signals.
4. State representation efficiency: Results showing that dialogue-act classification
(23-d) maintains performance while improving option diversity (OCI 41.3 vs 88.3)
compared to dense embeddings (143-d), providing 84% dimensionality reduction for
potential deployment.
5. Empirical findings for practitioners: Experimental results informing architecture
choices in dialogue systems, identifying dynamic action availability as a factor
favoring flat policies in this museum dialogue setting.
11.2 Answers to Research Questions
RQ1: Does hierarchical structure improve performance?No, not in this setting.
Flat Actor-Critic achieves 98.7% coverage vs Option-Critic’s 49.1% baseline and 55.5%
best-optimized across our experiments (Table 14). Systematic optimization across 53
119
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
conditions improved SMDP by only 4.6–6.0pp while leaving a 43–45pp gap to flat policies.
Termination gradient suppression appears to cause option collapse (OCI 87–90), preventing
effective learning. Under these conditions, hierarchy adds learning burden without
corresponding benefit when action masking provides implicit temporal structure.
RQ2: Is reward shaping necessary?No, not in this setting. Augmented
rewards do not improve Option-Critic performance over baseline in our experiments.
AggressiveExhaustion achieves +2.6pp coverage but with unstable negative returns. Our
results suggest reward modifications alone cannot overcome the architectural limitations
observed; diffuse credit assignment in function-based options appears to prevent effective
reward signal utilization.
RQ3: Does simulator signal clarity affect policy quality?Yes, but for diversity
rather than performance. State Machine enables better option diversity (OCI 51.1 vs 88.3)
and recovery action usage (35.8% flat, 17.6% hierarchical) compared to Sim8. However,
clearer signals do not close the SMDP-MDP gap in our experiments. Signal quality
appears to bound what behaviors can be learned; flat policies exploit clear signals more
effectively than hierarchical policies in this domain.
RQ4: Can state representation be made efficient?Yes, in this domain.
DialogueAct (23-d) maintains coverage (49.4% vs 49.1%) while substantially improving
option diversity (OCI 41.3 vs 88.3) compared to DialogueBERT (143-d) (Table 37). The
84% dimensionality reduction provides computational benefits without performance loss,
suggesting compact representations may aid hierarchical credit assignment in this setting.
RQ5: Does option design philosophy matter?Yes, in this domain. Reward-
aligned design achieves the largest improvement across H1–H5 in our experiments: +6.0pp
coverage (55.5% vs 49.5%) and OCI 2.1 vs 74.7 (Table 39). One-to-one reward-option
correspondence enables cleaner credit assignment and option-level masking prevents
persistence. However, even best SMDP (55.5%) remains 43.2pp below flat policies (98.7%),
indicating option design alone cannot overcome the hierarchical limitations observed in
this setting.
11.3 Main Findings
Across five hypotheses, our experiments reveal a consistent pattern: flat Actor-Critic
policies substantially outperform hierarchical Option-Critic policies in this museum
dialogue domain. In baseline comparisons (H1), flat policies achieve 98.7% coverage while
the best-optimized hierarchical configuration reaches only 55.5%, leaving a 43.2 percentage
point gap that persists across reward engineering (H2), simulator improvements (H3),
state representation changes (H4), and alternative option designs (H5). Reward-aligned
option design provides the largest hierarchical improvement (72.6 point reduction in
option collapse), but cannot close the performance gap. These results suggest that
when dynamic action masking already provides temporal structure, hierarchical learning
introduces termination and credit assignment challenges without corresponding benefits
in this domain.
11.4 Closing Remarks
These simulator-based results suggest that flat Actor-Critic policies may be an effec-
tive architecture for museum dialogue agents with exhaustible content and dynamic
action availability. The 43–49pp performance gap to Option-Critic observed across our
120
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
experiments appears to be architectural rather than parametric: hierarchy introduces
termination learning and credit assignment challenges that were not overcome through
hyperparameter optimization, reward engineering, or option design improvements in this
setting. Deliberation cost tuning and reward-aligned option design provide measurable
improvements, but cannot fully address the termination gradient suppression identified in
Option-Critic architectures [Bacon et al., 2017].
The findings also suggest that simulator signal quality affects learning dynamics, with
clearer signals enabling better option diversity and engagement-adaptive behaviors, though
not closing the architecture gap. Compact state representations (DialogueAct: 23-d)
maintain performance while providing substantial efficiency benefits (84% dimensionality
reduction), suggesting that reduced dimensionality may aid hierarchical credit assignment.
For museum dialogue systems with similar characteristics—exhaustible content, dy-
namic action masking, structured prompts—our findings suggest that flat RL may
provide a practical foundation. However, further validation with real museum visitors
remains necessary to confirm whether these simulator-based improvements translate to
deployment performance. Future work should test with real visitors, explore alternative
HRL algorithms (FeUdal Networks, HAM) that avoid learned termination, and extend to
larger knowledge bases and additional museum domains to assess the generalizability of
these findings.

S,A,OSets (states, actions, options)
s, a, oIndividual elements (state, action, option)
st, at Element at timestept
R(s, a) Reward function (deterministic)
rt Realized reward at timet
γDiscount factor
π(a|s) Policy (action distribution given state)
πω(a|s) Intra-option policy for optionω
βω(s) Termination probability for optionω
QΩ(s, ω) Option-value function
V(s) State-value function (under the policy over options)
ft,h t,i t,c t State vector components (focus, history, intent, context)
14 Knowledge Base
The museum knowledge graph consists of 5 exhibits from a Dutch Golden Age portrait
collection, with approximately 25 facts total (5 facts per exhibit) and 21 Areas of Interest
(AOIs). The exhibits are:
• King Caspar: “The African King Caspar” by Hendrick Heerschop (1654) – 7 AOIs
•Turban: “Head of a Boy in a Turban” by Gerrit Dou (1635) – 4 AOIs
• Dom Miguel: “Portrait of Dom Miguel de Castro” by Jeronimus Beckx (1643) – 4
AOIs
•Pedro Sunda: “Portrait of Pedro Sunda” by Jeronimus Beckx (1643) – 3 AOIs
•Diego Bemba: “Portrait of Diego Bemba” by Jeronimus Beckx (1643) – 3 AOIs
Each exhibit contains structured facts derived from:
•Description: Primary artwork description
•More info: Additional historical/contextual information
•Artist + Year: Creator and date metadata
•Location: Current museum/collection location
•Style: Artistic technique (e.g., “Oil on panel”)
Facts are assigned unique IDs (e.g., KC 001, TU 002) for grounding verification. AOIs
represent specific visual elements within each painting that visitors can focus on (e.g.,
“Incense Pot”, “Turban”, “Ivory tusk”).
122
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
15 Prompt Templates
The dialogue planner constructs structured prompts that guide LLM generation while
maintaining dialogue coherence and fact grounding. Each prompt includes context sections
showing recent conversation history and available facts.
15.1 Agent Action Prompts
ExplainNewFact (abbreviated):
[CONTEXT - DO NOT REPEAT]
Museum guide at: {exhibit_id}
Progress: {coverage}% covered
---
VISITOR SAID: "{last_utterance}"
RECENT CONVERSATION: [last 4 utterances]
PRIORITY #1: RESPOND TO WHAT THE VISITOR SAID
NEW FACTS AVAILABLE (pick 1-2 from this list ONLY):
{available_facts_with_ids}
ALREADY MENTIONED (DO NOT USE): {used_fact_ids}
RESPONSE STRUCTURE:
1. FIRST: Acknowledge what they said
2. THEN: Share 1-2 NEW facts using EXACT IDs: [KC_001]
3. Keep it conversational (2-3 sentences total)
ClarifyFact (abbreviated):
[CONTEXT]
Museum guide at: {exhibit_id}
VISITOR SAID: "{last_utterance}"
YOUR TASK:
1. Show you understand what confused them
2. Clarify this fact using a simple analogy: "{fact}"
NO new facts or [FACT_IDs] - just clarify what we
already discussed. Keep it 2-3 sentences.
RepeatFact (abbreviated):
[CONTEXT]
Museum guide at: {exhibit_id}
VISITOR SAID: "{last_utterance}"
PREVIOUSLY MENTIONED FACTS:
{used_facts_with_ids}
123
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
YOUR TASK:
1. Acknowledge their question/confusion
2. Restate ONE fact from PREVIOUSLY MENTIONED using
simpler wording or a different angle
3. Do NOT introduce new facts - only rephrase what
we already covered
Keep it 2-3 sentences, conversational.
AskOpinion (abbreviated):
[CONTEXT]
Museum guide at: {exhibit_id}
VISITOR SAID: "{last_utterance}"
RECENT TOPICS: {recent_facts_discussed}
YOUR TASK:
1. Briefly acknowledge what they said
2. Ask for their PERSONAL VIEW on an aspect of the
artwork (style, symbolism, emotional response)
3. Make it open-ended and inviting
Keep it 1-2 sentences. Example: "What do you think
the artist was trying to convey with...?"
AskMemory (abbreviated):
[CONTEXT]
Museum guide at: {exhibit_id}
VISITOR SAID: "{last_utterance}"
RECENT TOPICS: {recent_facts_discussed}
YOUR TASK:
1. Connect to what we just discussed
2. Ask if this reminds them of something from their
own experience or other artworks they’ve seen
3. Encourage personal connection
Keep it 1-2 sentences. Example: "Does this remind
you of anything you’ve seen before?"
AskClarification (abbreviated):
[CONTEXT]
Museum guide at: {exhibit_id}
VISITOR SAID: "{last_utterance}"
YOUR TASK:
1. Show you’re trying to understand them
2. Ask a specific clarifying question about what
124
Daniel Bourdon Comparative RL for Museum Dialogue
they meant or what they’d like to know more about
3. Be helpful, not dismissive
Keep it 1-2 sentences. Example: "I want to make sure
I understand - are you asking about...?"
OfferTransition prompts include exhibit coverage statistics and suggest specific target
exhibits based on the knowledge graph’s exhibit connections.
WrapUp (abbreviated):
[CONTEXT]
Museum guide concluding session
EXHIBITS VISITED: {exhibits_covered}
KEY FACTS SHARED: {total_facts_mentioned}
VISITOR SAID: "{last_utterance}"
YOUR TASK:
1. Warmly thank the visitor for their time
2. Briefly mention 1-2 highlights from the visit
3. Invite them to return or explore more
Keep it 2-3 sentences. End on a positive,
memorable note. NO new facts.
15.2 Visitor State Prompts
The State Machine simulator uses state-specific prompts to generate contextually appro-
priate visitor responses. See Section 5.2.2 for the full set of visitor state prompts.
16 Hyperparameter Settings
The agent uses an Actor-Critic architecture with Option-Critic extensions for hierarchical
learning. Training uses per-step updates (not batched) with the following hyperparameters:
